Yes, this is a real direction worth designing.
But the key design correction is this:
Do not remove meaning. Remove ambiguity.
AI systems still need semantics. What they do not need is messy human semantics: ambiguity, redundancy, synonym sprawl, implied context, filler words, inconsistent formatting, and token-heavy natural language.
So the right target is not “no semantics.”
It is:
a compact, explicit, machine-native semantic language
That would be smaller, faster, cheaper, and easier for agents, models, and orchestration systems to use than English.
The core conclusion
You should not think of this as just “a new language.”
You actually need three separate things:
1. An AI meaning layer
A canonical semantic language for concepts, relations, actions, constraints, evidence, confidence, provenance, and verification.
2. A transport/wire format
How that meaning is serialized between agents and systems. This may replace JSON for internal use.
3. A file format
A disk format optimized for AI ingestion, retrieval, chunking, references, and machine reasoning.
Those are related, but they are not the same thing.
________________


My recommendation
Build this as a stack:
Layer 1 — Human interface
Humans still type English.
Layer 2 — AI canonical language
English gets converted into a strict, normalized, machine semantic form.
Layer 3 — Wire encoding
That canonical form gets serialized in a compact format.
Layer 4 — AI file format
Documents, datasets, and knowledge artifacts are stored in a format optimized for machines.
So yes: you can invent an alternative to JSON, but the bigger invention is the canonical AI language above it.
________________


What the language should optimize for
Your language should be:
   * compact
   * deterministic
   * typed
   * reference-based
   * ontology-driven
   * easy to parse
   * easy to validate
   * loss-aware
   * composable
   * versioned
It should avoid:
   * synonym ambiguity
   * hidden assumptions
   * vague nouns
   * pronouns
   * unbounded prose
   * redundant phrasing
   * inconsistent units
   * implicit relationships
________________


What not to do
Do not try to replace all human language with alien symbols immediately.
That would fail for three reasons:
   1. current models are pretrained on natural language
   2. humans still need readability
   3. raw source documents remain valuable as evidence
So the first version should be:
English in, AI canonical language out
Not:
humans manually writing only in AI language from day one
________________


The correct design principle
Instead of “semantic-free,” use this:
Explicit machine semantics
Meaning is encoded directly, not inferred from prose.
Example:
English:
The research agent analyzed dataset 44 and found a strong correlation between X and Y with 92% confidence.
Machine canonical form:
ACT(agent.research.01, analyze, dataset.44)
OBS(dataset.44, correlation, var.X, var.Y)
ATTR(correlation.01, strength, strong)
CONF(correlation.01, 0.92)
PROV(correlation.01, trace.8821)
That is much closer to what you want.
The model no longer has to infer:
   * who acted
   * what action occurred
   * what object was acted on
   * what claim was made
   * what confidence applies
   * what provenance supports it
________________


The real invention
The invention is not just a smaller language.
It is a universal canonical intermediate representation for intelligence systems.
That means it must represent:
   * entities
   * actions
   * tasks
   * claims
   * evidence
   * relationships
   * time
   * causality
   * constraints
   * uncertainty
   * permissions
   * verification state
   * provenance
   * references to raw source material
This is closer to a fusion of:
   * programming language IR
   * knowledge graph triples
   * ontology language
   * agent protocol schema
   * verification metadata
________________


Best architecture: separate “meaning” from “encoding”
This is critical.
1. AI Language
This is the logical structure.
Example conceptually:
TASK[id=t.1001 type=analyze priority=high]
IN[t.1001 -> ds.44]
ACTOR[t.1001 -> ag.research.01]
OUT[t.1001 -> result.887]
CONF[result.887 -> 0.92]
VERIFY[result.887 -> vh.332]
2. Encoding
How it travels over the network.
Could be:
   * JSON for debugging
   * CBOR
   * MessagePack
   * Protobuf
   * FlatBuffers
   * custom binary
3. File format
How it is stored on disk.
Could be:
   * .ail
   * .aif
   * .aix
   * .aas
So:
   * the language is the meaning system
   * the encoding is the transport
   * the file format is the storage container

A. Language name
Use something like:
   * AISL = Artificial Intelligence Semantic Language
   * AICL = AI Canonical Language
   * AIML = AI Meaning Language
   * AUL = Universal AI Language
   * AASL = Atrahasis AI Semantic Language
My preference for your project:
AASL
Atrahasis AI Semantic Language
B. Wire format
Use:
AACP messages expressed in AASL
Then serialize via:
   * JSON at prototype stage
   * binary later
C. File format
Use something like:
.aas or .aaf
My preference:
   * .aas = Atrahasis AI Script / Semantic file
   * .aaf = Atrahasis AI File

What the language should contain
Your language needs fixed primitives.
Core primitives
Entity
A thing.
ENT[id=dataset.44 class=dataset]
Agent
AGT[id=ag.research.01 role=research]
Action
ACT[id=act.201 actor=ag.research.01 op=analyze target=dataset.44]
Claim
CLM[id=claim.77 type=correlation subject=var.X object=var.Y]
Attribute
ATT[target=claim.77 key=strength val=0.81]
Confidence
CNF[target=claim.77 val=0.92]
Provenance
PRV[target=claim.77 trace=trace.8821 source=doc.4.sec.2]
Verification
VRF[target=claim.77 hash=vh.332 status=verified]
Constraint
CST[target=task.101 max_time_ms=5000]
Time
TIM[target=act.201 ts=2026-03-07T22:00:00-06:00]
That gives you a formal system.
________________


Why this is better than English
English is expensive because it contains:
   * redundancy
   * implied structure
   * variable word order
   * vague references
   * extra syntax for human readability
Your canonical language would instead use:
   * fixed operators
   * fixed order
   * typed slots
   * normalized terms
   * references instead of repeated text
So instead of 35 words, you may need 5–10 structured records.
That can reduce:
   * token usage
   * parsing burden
   * ambiguity
   * context bloat
________________


But there is one hard truth
“Zero translation by AI” is not possible at first
A model only “naturally” understands a language if it was trained on it.
So if you invent a new language, one of these must happen:
   1. you fine-tune models on it
   2. you train adapters on it
   3. you build a translation layer that converts English/raw data into it
   4. you eventually train new models natively on it
So the rollout must be staged.
________________


Best rollout plan
Phase 1 — Canonical layer only
Humans write English.
System translates English into AASL.
Agents talk internally in AASL.
Phase 2 — Dual-native operation
Models see both:
   * raw source text
   * canonical AASL representation
Phase 3 — Training augmentation
New fine-tunes and agent runtimes are trained to use AASL directly.
Phase 4 — Native machine documents
New generated artifacts are stored directly as .aas files.
Phase 5 — Full AI-native corpora
Some domains get converted into canonical AI-native corpora for training and reasoning.
That is realistic.
________________


Very important warning
You should not immediately convert all training data into only the new language.
Why:
   * raw natural language contains nuance your canonicalizer may lose
   * existing foundation models are already deeply aligned to natural language
   * premature conversion could destroy signal
   * many tasks still need raw text evidence
So the right approach is:
dual representation
Store both:
   1. raw source
   2. canonical AI form
That gives you:
   * fidelity
   * traceability
   * compact machine reasoning
________________


The JSON question
Should you still use JSON?
For prototype: yes
JSON is fine for:
   * debugging
   * inspection
   * interoperability
   * early tooling
For mature system: no
For large-scale agent swarms, use a binary encoding.
So the likely path is:
AASL-v1
Human-readable text form
JSON serialization available
AASL-v2
Binary serialization for production
That gives you both readability and performance.
________________


My recommendation for the first formal design
Build the language with these 5 layers:
1. Ontology layer
Defines allowed concept types.
Examples:
   * agent
   * model
   * task
   * claim
   * evidence
   * dataset
   * variable
   * result
   * verification_record
2. Syntax layer
Defines how statements are written.
3. Reference layer
Everything important gets IDs, not repeated prose.
4. Validation layer
Schema checks:
   * required fields
   * valid relations
   * type correctness
   * unit correctness
   * provenance links
5. Serialization layer
Supports:
   * text form
   * JSON form
   * binary form
________________


A good first syntax
Do not start with something too exotic.
Use a compact tagged syntax.
Example:
AGT{ id:ag.r1 role:research }
DS{ id:ds.44 type:dataset }
TSK{ id:t.101 op:analyze in:ds.44 by:ag.r1 }
CLM{ id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.X b:var.Y }
CNF{ target:c.1 val:0.92 }
PRV{ target:c.1 src:doc.4.sec.2 trace:tr.8821 }
VRF{ target:c.1 status:verified hash:vh.332 }
This is:
   * compact
   * human-checkable
   * machine-parseable
   * easier than free prose
________________


Long-term file format concept
Your file type should be more than a text file.
It should support:
   * canonical semantic blocks
   * references to raw source text
   * chunk indexing
   * provenance graph
   * verification records
   * entity tables
   * ontology version
   * optional embeddings
   * optional binary payload sections
So the file format should behave more like:
AI-native document container
not just “smaller markdown.”
________________


Suggested file structure
Example .aas conceptual layout:
HEADER{
  version:1.0
  ontology:core.v1
  doc_id:doc.1002
  source_type:converted_markdown
}


LEXICON{
  ent:dataset=ds
  ent:agent=ag
  ent:claim=cl
}


BODY{
  AGT{ id:ag.r1 role:research }
  DS{ id:ds.44 type:dataset }
  TSK{ id:t.101 op:analyze in:ds.44 by:ag.r1 }
  CLM{ id:cl.1 type:correlation a:var.X b:var.Y }
  CNF{ target:cl.1 val:0.92 }
}


PROVENANCE{
  PRV{ target:cl.1 src:raw.22 trace:tr.8821 }
}


VERIFY{
  VRF{ target:cl.1 status:verified hash:vh.332 }
}
That is already far more machine-native than markdown.

The strongest version of your idea
The strongest version is not “a universal AI language that replaces English.”
It is:
a universal canonical intelligence representation layer
That is much more defensible technically.
Because then you can say:
   * humans still use English
   * machines use AASL internally
   * training pipelines ingest both
   * agents communicate in a lower-cost semantic form
   * files can be compiled into AI-native containers
That is coherent.

Your instinct is right.
There is likely major value in creating:
   * an AI-native semantic language
   * an AI-native document format
   * a translation/compiler layer from human text to machine meaning
But the first step should be:
design the canonical language, not the final transport or final file format
That is the foundation everything else depends on.

Yes, you should design this.
But the right design is not “remove semantics.”
It is:
replace human semantics with explicit machine semantics
And yes, this can become:
      * an alternative to JSON at the meaning layer

      * the basis for an AI-native file format
      * eventually a training-time representation layer

1. Language name
Recommended name
AASL
Atrahasis AI Semantic Language
Why this is the best starting name:
      * tied directly to your ecosystem
      * flexible enough to expand beyond agents
      * precise enough to describe what it is
      * strong brandability
      * leaves room for future components:
      * AACP
      * AASL
      * AAS compiler
      * AAS file format
The word semantic is still useful here because we are not removing meaning.
We are making meaning explicit, compact, and machine-native.
________________


2. Purpose statement
Proposed purpose statement
AASL is a compact, explicit, machine-native language for representing meaning, tasks, claims, evidence, verification, and coordination across AI agents, models, systems, and knowledge stores.
This is broad enough to cover:
      * agent communication
      * model outputs
      * orchestration
      * verification
      * stored knowledge
      * future training representation
________________


3. Design objective
Proposed design objective
AASL is designed to reduce ambiguity, token overhead, translation burden, and context waste by replacing unstructured natural language with canonical machine semantics.
This is the core idea.
Not:
      * “remove intelligence”
      * “remove meaning”
      * “replace all human language immediately”
Instead:
      * canonicalize meaning
      * compress structure
      * reduce inference burden
      * improve determinism
________________


4. Scope boundary
We should define what AASL is and is not.
AASL is
      * a canonical semantic language
      * a machine meaning layer
      * a representation format for tasks, claims, and knowledge
      * usable by agents, orchestrators, verifiers, and storage systems
AASL is not
      * a human conversational language
      * a replacement for English input at the start
      * just a wire protocol
      * just a file extension
      * just a JSON replacement
This matters because otherwise the design will get muddled.
________________

AASL — Atrahasis AI Semantic Language

Step 2 defines the core rules that every part of AASL must follow. These principles control how the language is designed, parsed, validated, and expanded. Once defined, they should rarely change.
________________


AASL Specification
Step 2 — Core Design Principles
Principle 1 — Explicit Semantics
All meaning must be explicitly encoded, never implied.
Human language often requires interpretation.
AASL removes this burden by forcing every statement to specify:
      * actor
      * action
      * object
      * relationships
      * attributes
      * evidence
      * confidence
      * provenance
Example concept:
Human sentence
“Agent A analyzed dataset B.”
AASL style concept (illustrative)
ACT{actor:ag.A op:analyze target:ds.B}
No meaning is inferred. Everything is declared.
________________


Principle 2 — Canonical Representation
Every piece of meaning should have exactly one canonical form.
Human language has synonyms:
      * analyze
      * examine
      * study
      * inspect
In AASL there should only be one canonical operator:
op:analyze
This prevents semantic drift and token waste.
________________


Principle 3 — Reference-Based Communication
Entities must be referenced by IDs, not repeated text.
Example:
Instead of repeatedly writing:
dataset climate data 2023
dataset climate data 2023
dataset climate data 2023
AASL defines once:
DS{id:ds.44 type:climate_dataset}
Then references it:
target:ds.44
This dramatically reduces message size.
________________


Principle 4 — Typed Structures
Every object in AASL has a defined type.
Types prevent ambiguity and allow deterministic parsing.
Example types:
Type
	Meaning
	AGT
	agent
	DS
	dataset
	TSK
	task
	ACT
	action
	CLM
	claim
	PRV
	provenance
	CNF
	confidence
	VRF
	verification
	Typed systems allow machines to process meaning without guessing.
________________


Principle 5 — Deterministic Ordering
Fields must appear in predictable positions or keys.
Human language allows:
“Agent A analyzed dataset B.”
“Dataset B was analyzed by Agent A.”
AASL must always encode the same structure.
Example rule:
ACT{actor:X op:Y target:Z}
Never reordered.
This prevents parsing complexity.
________________


Principle 6 — Minimal Redundancy
Meaning should be represented once and referenced thereafter.
Redundant information increases:
      * token cost
      * message size
      * reasoning overhead
Example:
Instead of repeating full metadata in every message, reference existing objects.
________________


Principle 7 — Verification Awareness
AASL must support verification-first intelligence systems.
Meaning structures must allow:
      * provenance
      * evidence
      * replication
      * consensus scoring
      * verification state
Example concept:
CLM{id:c1 type:correlation a:X b:Y}
CNF{target:c1 val:0.92}
VRF{target:c1 status:verified}
Verification metadata is part of the language itself.
________________


Principle 8 — Machine-Optimized Compression
The language should prioritize machine efficiency over human readability.
Design goals:
      * fewer tokens
      * predictable structures
      * short operators
      * reference IDs
Human readability is secondary but still possible.
________________


Principle 9 — Modular Ontology
The language core must be small and extensible.
AASL should support domain modules.
Example:
Core ontology
      * agent
      * action
      * claim
      * verification
Domain modules
      * medicine
      * physics
      * finance
      * engineering
Example:
CLM{type:medical.diagnosis}

________________
Principle 10 — Transport Neutrality
AASL defines meaning, not transport.
It must work with multiple encodings.
Examples:
Encoding
	Use
	JSON
	debugging
	Binary
	production
	Stream
	real-time messaging
	This prevents protocol lock-in.
________________


Principle 11 — Bidirectional Traceability
Every output must trace back to:
      * inputs
      * evidence
      * reasoning steps
      * agents involved
Example:
PRV{target:c1 source:doc.12.section.4}
Traceability is essential for verification systems.
________________


Principle 12 — Deterministic Parsing
AASL must be parsable by machines without statistical inference.
Parsing should require:
      * no NLP
      * no probability models
      * no semantic guessing
Instead:
      * fixed grammar
      * fixed token types
      * fixed operators
________________


Summary of Core Principles
#
	Principle
	1
	explicit semantics
	2
	canonical representation
	3
	reference-based communication
	4
	typed structures
	5
	deterministic ordering
	6
	minimal redundancy
	7
	verification awareness
	8
	machine-optimized compression
	9
	modular ontology
	10
	transport neutrality
	11
	bidirectional traceability
	12
	deterministic parsing
	These principles define the philosophy of AASL.
Every later component must follow them.
________________

This defines the fundamental objects that all AASL statements are built from. Everything in the language must ultimately be composed from these primitives.
________________


AASL Specification
Step 3 — Core Object Model
The object model defines the basic semantic building blocks used to represent meaning.
Each object has:
      * a type
      * a unique identifier
      * optional attributes
      * optional relationships
Objects are referenced using IDs rather than repeated text.
________________


Object Category Structure
All AASL objects fall into one of six conceptual groups:
Category
	Purpose
	Actors
	entities capable of performing actions
	Artifacts
	data or objects acted upon
	Processes
	actions or tasks
	Assertions
	claims or statements
	Evidence
	supporting information
	Control
	verification and constraints
	________________


1. Agent (AGT)
Represents an AI agent or system actor.
Agents perform tasks, generate claims, and participate in verification.
Example conceptual structure:
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
Typical attributes:
Attribute
	Meaning
	id
	unique identifier
	role
	function of the agent
	model
	model used by the agent
	cluster
	cluster membership
	________________


2. Model (MOD)
Represents a reasoning model.
This separates agents from the models they use.
Example concept:
MOD{id:mod.reasoning.v1}
Typical attributes:
Attribute
	Meaning
	id
	model identifier
	type
	reasoning, coding, planning, etc
	provider
	local, API, etc
	________________


3. Dataset / Artifact (DS)
Represents data, files, or resources.
Examples:
      * datasets
      * documents
      * images
      * simulation outputs
Example:
DS{id:ds.44 type:dataset}
Typical attributes:
Attribute
	Meaning
	id
	unique artifact ID
	type
	dataset, document, image
	source
	origin of data
	________________


4. Task (TSK)
Represents a unit of work.
Tasks are created by orchestrators and assigned to agents.
Example concept:
TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.44}
Typical attributes:
Attribute
	Meaning
	id
	task identifier
	op
	operation
	target
	artifact or entity
	priority
	scheduling priority
	________________


5. Action (ACT)
Represents execution of a task by an agent.
Example:
ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}
Typical attributes:
Attribute
	Meaning
	actor
	performing agent
	op
	operation performed
	target
	object acted upon
	time
	timestamp
	________________


6. Claim (CLM)
Represents a statement produced by reasoning.
Claims describe results, findings, or conclusions.
Example:
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.X b:var.Y}
Typical attributes:
Attribute
	Meaning
	type
	claim category
	subject
	main entity
	object
	related entity
	________________


7. Evidence (EVD)
Represents supporting information for claims.
Evidence can reference:
      * datasets
      * documents
      * experiment results
      * simulations
Example:
EVD{id:e.33 source:ds.44}

________________
8. Confidence (CNF)
Represents confidence score of a claim.
Example:
CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}

________________
9. Provenance (PRV)
Represents traceability of a claim or action.
Example:
PRV{target:c.1 source:doc.12.sec.4}
Provenance ensures traceability.
________________


10. Verification (VRF)
Represents verification state of a claim.
Example:
VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}
Typical attributes:
Attribute
	Meaning
	status
	verified / rejected
	hash
	verification hash
	method
	verification mechanism
	________________


11. Constraint (CST)
Represents limits or rules applied to tasks or actions.
Example:
CST{target:t.101 max_time_ms:5000}

________________
12. Time (TIM)
Represents temporal metadata.
Example:
TIM{target:a.201 ts:2026-03-07T22:00:00}

________________
Summary of Core Objects
Type
	Meaning
	AGT
	agent
	MOD
	model
	DS
	dataset / artifact
	TSK
	task
	ACT
	action
	CLM
	claim
	EVD
	evidence
	CNF
	confidence
	PRV
	provenance
	VRF
	verification
	CST
	constraint
	TIM
	time
	These 12 objects are the foundation of AASL.
Everything expressed in the language must be constructed from them.
________________


Example Combined Statement
Illustrative workflow representation:
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
DS{id:ds.44 type:dataset}


TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.44}


ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}


CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.X b:var.Y}


CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}
VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}
This structure represents:
      * who acted
      * what was done
      * what result was produced
      * how confident it is
      * whether it is verified
________________

Future ontology modules can extend this set but not replace it.

Confirm Step 3 and we proceed to Step 4 — AASL Syntax and Grammar.
Step 3 is now locked. We proceed to Step 4 — AASL Syntax & Grammar. This defines the exact writing rules so any system can parse AASL deterministically (no NLP required).

AASL Specification
Step 4 — Syntax & Grammar
4.1 Statement Structure
Each AASL statement represents one semantic object.
General form:
TYPE{ key:value key:value ... }
Example:
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
Components:
Component
	Meaning
	TYPE
	object type (from the Step-3 object model)
	{}
	object body
	key:value
	attribute pairs
	________________


4.2 Object Type Tokens
Object types are fixed uppercase tokens.
Allowed core types (v1):
Token
	Object
	AGT
	agent
	MOD
	model
	DS
	dataset / artifact
	TSK
	task
	ACT
	action
	CLM
	claim
	EVD
	evidence
	CNF
	confidence
	PRV
	provenance
	VRF
	verification
	CST
	constraint
	TIM
	time
	Example:
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.X b:var.Y}

________________
4.3 Attribute Format
Attributes follow:
key:value
Multiple attributes are separated by spaces.
Example:
ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}

________________
4.4 ID Rules
All objects must have an id field.
Format:
category.name.number
Examples:
ag.research.01
ds.climate.44
t.analysis.101
c.correlation.1
Recommended prefixes:
Prefix
	Meaning
	ag
	agent
	mod
	model
	ds
	dataset
	t
	task
	a
	action
	c
	claim
	e
	evidence
	IDs must be unique within a document or system context.
________________


4.5 Value Types
AASL supports several value types.
1. Reference values
References point to other objects.
Example:
target:ds.44
actor:ag.research.01

________________
2. Numeric values
Numbers are written directly.
val:0.92
max_time_ms:5000

________________
3. String values
Strings use quotes when needed.
type:"climate dataset"
Most fields should avoid strings where possible to maintain compression.
________________


4. Boolean values
true
false
Example:
verified:true

________________
4.6 Statement Separation
Statements are separated by line breaks.
Example:
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
DS{id:ds.44 type:dataset}
TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.44}
This keeps parsing simple and deterministic.
________________


4.7 Ordering Rules
Attributes should follow a recommended canonical order.
Example canonical order:
id → actor → op → target → attributes
Example:
ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}
This ensures consistent canonical representation.
________________


4.8 Comments
Comments start with #.
Example:
# research agent definition
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
Comments are ignored by parsers.
________________


4.9 Nested Structures (Optional)
Nested structures may appear when necessary.
Example:
CLM{
 id:c.1
 type:correlation
 a:var.X
 b:var.Y
}
But the single-line canonical form remains preferred for machine efficiency.
________________


4.10 Deterministic Parsing
Parsing requires only three rules:
      1. read object type token
      2. read { } block
      3. parse key:value pairs
No NLP or probabilistic inference is required.
________________


Example AASL Block
# agent definition
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}


# dataset
DS{id:ds.44 type:dataset}


# task assignment
TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.44}


# execution
ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}


# claim produced
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.X b:var.Y}


# confidence
CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}


# verification result
VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}

________________
AASL Syntax Advantages
Compared to English:
      * explicit meaning
      * minimal tokens
      * deterministic structure
      * reference-based compression
      * machine-first representation
Compared to JSON:
      * smaller
      * less redundant
      * canonical ordering
      * optimized for semantic reasoning
________________

Confirming Step 4 locks:
      * syntax rules
      * object format
      * attribute format
      * parsing structure
This creates the first parseable version of AASL.

This will establish:
      * allowed operations
      * allowed claim types
      * relationship vocabulary
      * domain extensions
The ontology is what gives AASL shared meaning across systems.

Step 4 is now locked.
We proceed to Step 5 — AASL Ontology v1.
This step defines the shared vocabulary of meaning that all AASL systems understand.
Without an ontology, the language has structure but not shared semantics.
Think of the ontology as the dictionary of allowed concepts.
________________


AASL Specification
Step 5 — Ontology v1 (Core Semantic Vocabulary)
The ontology defines canonical terms used in AASL statements.
Goals:
      * eliminate synonym ambiguity
      * standardize meaning across systems
      * enable deterministic reasoning
      * support verification workflows
________________


5.1 Ontology Categories
Ontology terms are grouped into six semantic domains.
Domain
	Purpose
	Actors
	entities capable of performing actions
	Artifacts
	objects or data
	Operations
	tasks or processes
	Assertions
	reasoning outputs
	Evidence
	supporting information
	Control
	system governance and verification
	________________


5.2 Actor Ontology
Defines types of actors.
Allowed values for role.
Role
	Meaning
	research
	gathers information
	analysis
	processes data
	planning
	organizes workflows
	simulation
	runs models
	verification
	validates results
	coordination
	orchestrates tasks
	human
	human participant
	system
	infrastructure service
	Example:
AGT{id:ag.analysis.02 role:analysis}

________________
5.3 Artifact Ontology
Defines types of artifacts (DS.type).
Type
	Meaning
	dataset
	structured data collection
	document
	text document
	image
	image artifact
	code
	software source
	simulation_output
	simulation results
	knowledge_entry
	verified knowledge
	Example:
DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset}

________________
5.4 Operation Ontology
Defines canonical task operations.
Allowed values for op.
Operation
	Meaning
	analyze
	examine data
	summarize
	create summary
	classify
	categorize information
	generate
	create new output
	simulate
	run computational model
	verify
	validate result
	compare
	evaluate differences
	extract
	retrieve structured data
	Example:
TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.44}

________________
5.5 Claim Ontology
Defines types of reasoning outputs (CLM.type).
Claim Type
	Meaning
	correlation
	relationship between variables
	causation
	causal relationship
	classification
	category assignment
	prediction
	future outcome
	measurement
	quantified value
	observation
	detected pattern
	hypothesis
	proposed explanation
	Example:
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.X b:var.Y}

________________
5.6 Evidence Ontology
Defines types of supporting evidence.
Evidence Type
	Meaning
	dataset_reference
	derived from dataset
	document_reference
	derived from text
	experiment_result

	simulation_result
	computational simulation
	external_source
	external information
	Example:
EVD{id:e.33 type:dataset_reference source:ds.44}

________________
5.7 Verification Ontology
Defines verification states.
Allowed status values.
Status
	Meaning
	pending
	awaiting verification
	verified
	validated by consensus
	rejected
	verification failed
	disputed
	conflicting results
	Example:
VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}

________________
5.8 Confidence Ontology
Defines standardized confidence scoring.
Confidence must be numeric between:
0.0 – 1.0
Example:
CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}

________________
5.9 Provenance Ontology
Defines traceability sources.
Possible source categories:
Source
	Meaning
	dataset
	derived from dataset
	document
	derived from document
	experiment
	experimental procedure
	simulation
	computational simulation
	agent
	agent-generated output
	Example:
PRV{target:c.1 source:document}

________________
5.10 Time Ontology
Defines timestamps.
Format:
ISO-8601
Example:
TIM{target:a.201 ts:2026-03-07T22:00:00Z}

________________
5.11 Constraint Ontology
Defines execution constraints.
Examples:
Constraint
	Meaning
	max_time_ms
	time limit
	max_memory_mb
	memory limit
	max_cost
	resource cost limit
	Example:
CST{target:t.101 max_time_ms:5000}

________________
Example Ontology Usage
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}


DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset}


TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}


ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}


CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.temp b:var.co2}


EVD{id:e.1 type:dataset_reference source:ds.climate.44}


CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}


VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}
This example uses the ontology vocabulary consistently.
________________


Ontology Versioning
The ontology must support versioning.
Example:
ontology_version:1.0
Future domain modules can extend it.
Examples:
      * medical ontology
      * finance ontology
      * physics ontology
These will build on core ontology v1.

Confirm Step 5 and we proceed to Step 6 — AASL Transport Encoding.
Step 5 is now locked.
We proceed to Step 6 — AASL Transport Encoding.
This step defines how AASL moves between agents, models, orchestrators, verifiers, and storage systems. The key distinction is:
      * AASL = meaning layer
      * transport encoding = how that meaning is serialized and transmitted
We should define transport in a staged way so you can prototype quickly and later optimize hard for scale.
________________


AASL Specification
Step 6 — Transport Encoding
6.1 Transport Design Goals
AASL transport must support:
      * deterministic parsing
      * low overhead
      * compact representation
      * versioning
      * compatibility with AACP
      * upgrade path from prototype to production
      * support for both human-debuggable and machine-optimized forms
________________


6.2 Encoding Model
AASL will support three transport representations:
Representation
	Purpose
	AASL-Text
	canonical human-readable form
	AASL-JSON
	interoperability and debugging
	AASL-Binary
	production-scale compact transport
	This is the correct design because it separates:
      * semantic meaning
      * developer usability
      * production efficiency
________________


6.3 AASL-Text
This is the canonical written form defined in Step 4.
Example:
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset}
TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.temp b:var.co2}
CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}
VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}
Purpose
      * specification reference form
      * easy inspection
      * authoring and testing
      * language design validation
Not ideal for
      * highest-performance runtime transport
      * ultra-large swarm messaging
________________


6.4 AASL-JSON
AASL objects can be represented in JSON for early implementation.
Example:
{
  "type": "AGT",
  "id": "ag.research.01",
  "role": "research"
}
Another example:
{
  "type": "CLM",
  "id": "c.1",
  "claim_type": "correlation",
  "a": "var.temp",
  "b": "var.co2"
}
Purpose
      * interoperability
      * easy API integration
      * quick prototype support
      * direct use in REST, Kafka, queues, logs
Weaknesses
      * repeated keys
      * larger payload size
      * not maximally efficient
________________


6.5 AASL-Binary
This will be the production encoding.
AASL-Binary should be:
      * field-indexed
      * type-tagged
      * reference-optimized
      * optionally dictionary-compressed
Instead of repeatedly sending strings like:
actor
target
status
verified
correlation
binary transport should use:
      * numeric field IDs
      * numeric enum values
      * compact object IDs
      * shared symbol tables where useful
Example conceptual binary mapping
Semantic Item
	Encoded As
	AGT
	0x01
	CLM
	0x06
	id
	0x01
	role
	0x02
	target
	0x03
	status
	0x04
	verified
	0x02
	This reduces repeated token cost and network burden.
________________


6.6 Official Transport Progression
AASL transport should evolve in phases:
Phase 1
AASL-Text + AASL-JSON
Used for:
      * spec design
      * prototype agents
      * early AACP integration
      * debugging
Phase 2
AASL-JSON + compressed transport
Used for:
      * medium-scale systems
      * orchestration pipelines
      * queue-based agents
Phase 3
AASL-Binary as primary runtime transport
Used for:
      * high-scale multi-agent networks
      * verification pipelines
      * internal swarm communication
This is the correct progression.
________________


6.7 Transport Envelope
Every transmitted AASL payload should include a transport envelope.
Required envelope fields
Field
	Purpose
	aasl_version
	version of language
	encoding
	text / json / binary
	ontology_version
	ontology version used
	message_id
	transport-level message ID
	payload_type
	single object / batch / stream
	checksum
	integrity validation
	Example conceptual envelope
ENV{
  aasl_version:1.0
  encoding:text
  ontology_version:1.0
  message_id:msg.1001
  payload_type:batch
}
This gives every message explicit compatibility metadata.
________________


6.8 Single-Object vs Batch Transport
AASL transport must support both.
Single-object transport
For simple events:
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.temp b:var.co2}
Batch transport
For efficient swarm communication, multiple statements should travel together.
Example:
ENV{aasl_version:1.0 encoding:text ontology_version:1.0 message_id:msg.1001 payload_type:batch}
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset}
TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.temp b:var.co2}
CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}
VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}
Batch transport will be much more efficient than sending each object separately.
________________


6.9 Compression Rules
Transport should support semantic compression, not just generic byte compression.
Compression rule 1 — references over repetition
Prefer IDs over repeated strings.
Compression rule 2 — enumerated vocabulary
Ontology values should map to enums in binary mode.
Compression rule 3 — canonical ordering
Consistent field order reduces parsing burden and improves compression ratio.
Compression rule 4 — batch shared context
A batch may define shared context once and reuse it.
Example conceptually:
CTX{actor:ag.research.01 target:ds.climate.44}
ACT{id:a.201 op:analyze}
TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze}
This should be optional in later versions.
________________


6.10 AACP Integration Rule
AACP should not invent a separate meaning format.
Instead:
Official rule
AACP messages carry AASL payloads.
That means:
      * AACP = routing / protocol layer
      * AASL = semantic content layer
Example concept:
AACP Envelope
    → carries AASL batch payload
This is architecturally cleaner and stronger.
________________


6.11 Recommended Production Binary Strategy
For now, the best decision is:
Do not invent a custom binary codec immediately
Instead define:
      * AASL semantic model now
      * AASL binary mapping later
The first production binary form should likely be one of:
      * Protobuf-backed AASL mapping
      * CBOR-backed AASL mapping
      * MessagePack-backed AASL mapping
Then later, if needed, you can define:
      * AASL-Native Binary
That avoids premature low-level lock-in.
________________


6.12 Transport Naming
Recommended naming set:
Name
	Meaning
	AASL-T
	text transport
	AASL-J
	JSON transport
	AASL-B
	binary transport
	Examples:
encoding:AASL-T
encoding:AASL-J
encoding:AASL-B
This gives a clean standard.
________________


6.13 Example Encodings of the Same Meaning
AASL-Text
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.temp b:var.co2}
CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}
VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}
AASL-JSON
[
  {
    "type": "CLM",
    "id": "c.1",
    "claim_type": "correlation",
    "a": "var.temp",
    "b": "var.co2"
  },
  {
    "type": "CNF",
    "target": "c.1",
    "val": 0.92
  },
  {
    "type": "VRF",
    "target": "c.1",
    "status": "verified"
  }
]
AASL-Binary
Conceptually the same semantic graph, encoded with:
      * type tags
      * field IDs
      * compact enums
      * indexed references
________________


6.14 Official Step 6 Decision Set
Confirmed transport architecture for AASL v1 should be:
AASL semantic layer
fixed by prior steps
Supported encodings
      * AASL-T = canonical text form
      * AASL-J = JSON serialization
      * AASL-B = binary serialization
Envelope metadata
required
AACP relationship
AACP carries AASL payloads
rollout strategy
JSON first, binary later
________________


Step 6 Summary
Locked decisions if confirmed:
      1. AASL has three transport forms
      2. text is canonical for spec/debugging
      3. JSON is the first implementation transport
      4. binary is the production target
      5. AACP carries AASL payloads
      6. all messages require versioned envelopes
________________

Next we should define AASL Message Framing for AACP.
That means:
      * how an AACP message wraps AASL
      * routing fields vs semantic payload fields
      * request/result/verification message classes
      * batch framing rules
This is where AASL begins directly replacing plain JSON message bodies in the protocol.
________________


Confirm Step 6 and we proceed to Step 7 — AASL Message Framing for AACP.
Step 6 is locked.
We proceed to Step 7 — AASL Message Framing for AACP.
This step defines how AACP wraps and transports AASL payloads.
AACP handles routing, identity, and protocol control.
AASL carries the actual semantic content.
This is where the architecture becomes operational.
________________


AASL Specification
Step 7 — Message Framing for AACP
7.1 Separation of Roles
The first rule is architectural:
AACP is the protocol envelope
AASL is the semantic payload
That means:
      * AACP handles:
      * routing
      * sender identity
      * receiver identity
      * workflow/thread IDs
      * delivery metadata
      * transport control
      * message lifecycle state
      * AASL handles:
      * tasks
      * actions
      * claims
      * evidence
      * confidence
      * verification
      * knowledge semantics
This separation must remain strict.
________________


7.2 AACP Frame Structure
Every AACP message should contain two main layers:
      1. Protocol Header
      2. AASL Payload
General model:
AACP {
  HEADER { ...protocol fields... }
  PAYLOAD { ...AASL-T / AASL-J / AASL-B... }
}

________________
7.3 AACP Header Fields
These fields belong to AACP, not AASL.
Required header fields
Field
	Purpose
	protocol_version
	AACP version
	aasl_version
	AASL version
	ontology_version
	ontology version
	message_id
	unique message identifier
	conversation_id
	thread-level grouping
	workflow_id
	workflow grouping
	parent_message_id
	chain reconstruction
	sender_id
	sending agent/system
	receiver_id
	target agent/system
	message_class
	protocol message class
	payload_encoding
	AASL-T / AASL-J / AASL-B
	timestamp
	send time
	status
	current lifecycle state
	________________


7.4 Header Example
Conceptual text form:
HEADER{
  protocol_version:1.0
  aasl_version:1.0
  ontology_version:1.0
  message_id:msg.1001
  conversation_id:conv.200
  workflow_id:wf.88
  parent_message_id:msg.0999
  sender_id:ag.coordination.01
  receiver_id:ag.research.01
  message_class:task_request
  payload_encoding:AASL-T
  timestamp:2026-03-07T22:15:00Z
  status:pending
}

________________
7.5 Message Classes
AACP needs fixed message classes.
Recommended v1 classes:
Message Class
	Purpose
	task_request
	assign work
	task_result
	return work result
	verification_request
	request verification
	verification_result
	return verification outcome
	knowledge_update
	submit new knowledge object
	coordination_signal
	orchestration/control message
	error_report
	report failure
	state_update
	report intermediate state
	These are protocol-level classes, not ontology terms.
________________


7.6 Payload Rule
The payload field must contain valid AASL content only.
That means:
      * AACP must not invent alternate semantic fields inside the payload
      * all domain meaning belongs to AASL
Example:
PAYLOAD{
  TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
  CST{target:t.101 max_time_ms:5000}
}

________________
7.7 Full Example — Task Request
HEADER{
  protocol_version:1.0
  aasl_version:1.0
  ontology_version:1.0
  message_id:msg.1001
  conversation_id:conv.200
  workflow_id:wf.88
  parent_message_id:msg.0999
  sender_id:ag.coordination.01
  receiver_id:ag.research.01
  message_class:task_request
  payload_encoding:AASL-T
  timestamp:2026-03-07T22:15:00Z
  status:pending
}


PAYLOAD{
  TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
  CST{target:t.101 max_time_ms:5000}
}
This says:
      * coordination agent sends a task
      * research agent receives it
      * payload contains the actual semantic task
________________


7.8 Full Example — Task Result
HEADER{
  protocol_version:1.0
  aasl_version:1.0
  ontology_version:1.0
  message_id:msg.1002
  conversation_id:conv.200
  workflow_id:wf.88
  parent_message_id:msg.1001
  sender_id:ag.research.01
  receiver_id:ag.coordination.01
  message_class:task_result
  payload_encoding:AASL-T
  timestamp:2026-03-07T22:16:30Z
  status:completed
}


PAYLOAD{
  ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
  CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.temp b:var.co2}
  EVD{id:e.1 type:dataset_reference source:ds.climate.44}
  CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}
}

________________
7.9 Full Example — Verification Result
HEADER{
  protocol_version:1.0
  aasl_version:1.0
  ontology_version:1.0
  message_id:msg.1003
  conversation_id:conv.200
  workflow_id:wf.88
  parent_message_id:msg.1002
  sender_id:ag.verification.01
  receiver_id:ag.coordination.01
  message_class:verification_result
  payload_encoding:AASL-T
  timestamp:2026-03-07T22:17:05Z
  status:verified
}


PAYLOAD{
  VRF{target:c.1 status:verified}
  PRV{target:c.1 source:agent}
}

________________
7.10 Single vs Batch Payload Framing
AACP must support both:
Single-object payload
For simple events or control messages.
Batch payload
For efficient multi-object transmission.
Batch example
PAYLOAD{
  AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
  DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset}
  TSK{id:t.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
}
Batching is preferred for efficiency when multiple related objects travel together.
________________


7.11 Request/Response Chain Rule
AACP message chains must preserve lineage using:
      * message_id
      * parent_message_id
      * conversation_id
      * workflow_id
This is essential for:
      * reasoning trace reconstruction
      * auditability
      * verification workflows
      * task lineage
________________


7.12 Header vs Payload Boundary Rule
This is a critical rule:
Header fields must never duplicate semantic payload meaning unless required for routing
Good:
      * sender_id in header
      * receiver_id in header
      * message_class in header
Not good:
      * full task meaning duplicated in header and payload
      * claim meaning duplicated in header and payload
This prevents redundancy.
________________


7.13 Error Framing
Errors should be protocol messages too.
Example
HEADER{
  protocol_version:1.0
  aasl_version:1.0
  ontology_version:1.0
  message_id:msg.1004
  conversation_id:conv.200
  workflow_id:wf.88
  parent_message_id:msg.1001
  sender_id:ag.research.01
  receiver_id:ag.coordination.01
  message_class:error_report
  payload_encoding:AASL-T
  timestamp:2026-03-07T22:15:20Z
  status:failed
}


PAYLOAD{
  CLM{id:c.error.1 type:observation}
}
For v1, error semantics can remain minimal, but framing must be standardized.
________________


7.14 JSON Framing Example
Equivalent AACP + AASL-J concept:
{
  "header": {
    "protocol_version": "1.0",
    "aasl_version": "1.0",
    "ontology_version": "1.0",
    "message_id": "msg.1001",
    "conversation_id": "conv.200",
    "workflow_id": "wf.88",
    "parent_message_id": "msg.0999",
    "sender_id": "ag.coordination.01",
    "receiver_id": "ag.research.01",
    "message_class": "task_request",
    "payload_encoding": "AASL-J",
    "timestamp": "2026-03-07T22:15:00Z",
    "status": "pending"
  },
  "payload": [
    {
      "type": "TSK",
      "id": "t.101",
      "op": "analyze",
      "target": "ds.climate.44"
    },
    {
      "type": "CST",
      "target": "t.101",
      "max_time_ms": 5000
    }
  ]
}

________________
7.15 Binary Framing Rule
For AASL-B, the same logical structure remains:
      * binary header
      * binary payload
The meaning model must remain identical across all encodings.
This preserves transport neutrality.
________________


7.16 Official Framing Rule
The official v1 framing rule should be:
Every AACP message = Header + AASL Payload
Nothing else.
This is the core operational standard.
________________


7.17 Step 7 Decision Set
If confirmed, these become locked:
      1. AACP carries AASL payloads
      2. AACP header handles routing/control only
      3. AASL payload carries all semantic content
      4. fixed message classes are required
      5. lineage fields are mandatory
      6. batch payloads are supported
      7. identical framing logic applies across text/json/binary forms
________________

At this point, you now have:

      * ontology
      * transport encoding
      * AACP framing
That means AASL is now a real system architecture, not just a concept.

Step 8 (Next)
Next should be AASL Canonicalization Rules.
This is very important.
Canonicalization defines:
      * how equivalent meanings are normalized into one standard form
      * how synonym reduction works
      * how field ordering is enforced
      * how English gets converted into valid AASL
This is the step that makes AASL actually reduce semantic waste.
________________


Confirm Step 7 and we proceed to Step 8 — AASL Canonicalization Rules.
Step 7 is locked.
We proceed to Step 8 — AASL Canonicalization Rules.
This step is one of the most important in the whole system.
A language only becomes truly efficient if the same meaning always normalizes into the same representation. Otherwise synonym drift, inconsistent fields, and duplicate structures bring back the same waste you are trying to remove.
________________


AASL Specification
Step 8 — Canonicalization Rules
8.1 Purpose of Canonicalization
Canonicalization ensures that:
      * equivalent meanings map to one standard form
      * redundant wording is removed
      * field order is fixed
      * synonyms collapse into canonical operators
      * IDs and references follow one stable pattern
      * parsing and verification remain deterministic
Without canonicalization, AASL would become just another flexible syntax.
That is not acceptable.
________________


8.2 Canonicalization Principle
Official rule
One meaning should resolve to one preferred AASL representation.
Not:
      * many valid ways to say the same thing
But:
      * one normalized form for storage, communication, and verification
This is essential for:
      * deduplication
      * compression
      * consistent hashing
      * message comparison
      * verification consensus
________________


8.3 Canonicalization Levels
Canonicalization should happen at four levels:
Level
	Purpose
	Lexical
	normalize words/tokens
	Structural
	normalize object/field structure
	Referential
	normalize IDs and references
	Semantic
	normalize equivalent meanings
	________________


8.4 Lexical Canonicalization
Lexical canonicalization standardizes vocabulary.
Rule 1 — one canonical term per concept
Examples:
Non-canonical
	Canonical
	examine
	analyze
	inspect
	analyze
	study
	analyze
	check
	verify
	confirm
	verify
	create
	generate
	make
	generate
	Only the canonical term is allowed in final AASL.
So if a source says:
Agent A examined dataset B.
It must normalize to:
ACT{id:a.1 actor:ag.A op:analyze target:ds.B}
Never op:examine.
________________


8.5 Structural Canonicalization
Structural canonicalization ensures that the same object is always arranged in the same way.
Rule 2 — canonical field order is mandatory
Each object type must have a preferred attribute order.
Recommended v1 orders
AGT
id role model cluster
MOD
id type provider
DS
id type source
TSK
id op target priority
ACT
id actor op target
CLM
id type subject object
EVD
id type source
CNF
target val
PRV
target source
VRF
target status method hash
CST
target max_time_ms max_memory_mb max_cost
TIM
target ts
Even if parsers can tolerate different orderings, canonical AASL must always output the standard order.
________________


8.6 Referential Canonicalization
References must normalize consistently.
Rule 3 — all reusable entities must be assigned IDs
If an entity appears more than once, it must be defined once and referenced thereafter.
Bad:
TSK{id:t.1 op:analyze target:"climate dataset 2023"}
ACT{id:a.1 actor:ag.r1 op:analyze target:"climate dataset 2023"}
Good:
DS{id:ds.climate.2023 type:dataset}
TSK{id:t.1 op:analyze target:ds.climate.2023}
ACT{id:a.1 actor:ag.r1 op:analyze target:ds.climate.2023}

________________
8.7 ID Canonicalization
Rule 4 — IDs must follow stable prefix patterns
Recommended stable forms:
Object
	Prefix
	agent
	ag
	model
	mod
	dataset/artifact
	ds
	task
	t
	action
	a
	claim
	c
	evidence
	e
	verification
	v
	constraint
	cs
	time
	tm
	Example normalized IDs:
ag.research.01
ds.climate.44
t.analyze.101
a.analyze.201
c.correlation.001
Systems may generate IDs automatically, but final AASL output must use the normalized form.
________________


8.8 Semantic Canonicalization
This is the most important layer.
Rule 5 — semantically equivalent inputs must collapse into the same AASL form
Examples:
Input 1
“The research agent analyzed dataset 44.”
Input 2
“Dataset 44 was examined by the research agent.”
Input 3
“The research agent studied dataset 44.”
All three must canonicalize to:
ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}
That is the core value of AASL.
________________


8.9 Claim Canonicalization
Claims should normalize into fixed relation structures.
Rule 6 — claim types must use canonical slots
For example, a correlation claim must always use the same slots.
Preferred v1 canonical form:
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}
Not:
      * a/b in one place
      * x/y in another
      * left/right elsewhere
So I recommend tightening Step 5 slightly going forward:
canonical claim slot names
      * subject
      * object
This is cleaner than a and b.
________________


8.10 Optional refinement to Step 5/4 usage
You already confirmed earlier steps, so I am not changing them retroactively.
But for canonicalization, I recommend this rule:
Rule 7 — shorthand fields may exist during drafting, but canonical output must use full canonical slot names
Example draft input:
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation a:var.temp b:var.co2}
Canonical output:
CLM{id:c.1 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}
This gives you a stronger standard.
________________


8.11 String Canonicalization
Rule 8 — free text should be minimized and normalized when unavoidable
If strings must exist:
      * trim whitespace
      * normalize case rules
      * avoid duplicate phrasing
      * prefer enumerated ontology values when possible
Preferred:
type:dataset
status:verified
role:research
Avoid:
type:"Dataset"
status:"VERIFIED"
role:"Research Agent"

________________
8.12 Numeric Canonicalization
Rule 9 — numeric values must use normalized numeric form
Examples:
      * confidence always between 0.0 and 1.0
      * time limits in ms
      * memory in mb
      * costs in one agreed unit later
Preferred:
CNF{target:c.1 val:0.92}
CST{target:t.1 max_time_ms:5000}
Not:
      * percentages in one place and decimals in another
      * seconds in one place and milliseconds in another
________________


8.13 Time Canonicalization
Rule 10 — all timestamps must use ISO-8601 UTC in canonical output
Preferred:
TIM{target:a.201 ts:2026-03-08T04:15:00Z}
Even if local time was used in input, canonical AASL should convert to UTC.
________________


8.14 Null / Missing Field Canonicalization
Rule 11 — absent data should be omitted, not represented with null-like filler unless required
Bad:
AGT{id:ag.r1 role:research model:null cluster:null}
Good:
AGT{id:ag.r1 role:research}
This keeps the language compact.
________________


8.15 Batch Canonicalization
Rule 12 — batched payloads should be ordered by dependency
Recommended order in a batch:
      1. actor/object definitions
      2. tasks
      3. actions
      4. claims
      5. evidence
      6. confidence
      7. provenance
      8. verification
      9. constraints
      10. time
Example:
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset}
TSK{id:t.analyze.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
ACT{id:a.analyze.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}
EVD{id:e.dataset.001 type:dataset_reference source:ds.climate.44}
CNF{target:c.correlation.001 val:0.92}
VRF{target:c.correlation.001 status:verified}
This improves:
      * readability
      * compression
      * hashing
      * deterministic comparison
________________


8.16 Canonicalization Pipeline
The official normalization pipeline should be:
Step A — parse source input
Could be English, JSON, markdown, or draft AASL
Step B — extract semantic intent
Identify:
      * actors
      * operations
      * targets
      * claims
      * evidence
      * confidence
      * verification data
Step C — map to ontology
Convert synonyms and variants to canonical ontology values
Step D — assign/reuse IDs
Create stable references
Step E — normalize structure
Apply field order, slot names, and object ordering
Step F — emit canonical AASL
Final output becomes deterministic
________________


8.17 Canonical Hashing Rule
Rule 13 — verification hashes should be computed from canonicalized AASL, not raw input
This is crucial.
If two agents say the same thing in different human wording, their semantic outputs should hash the same after canonicalization.
That enables:
      * consensus verification
      * deduplication
      * stronger Verichain integration
________________


8.18 Canonicalization Example
Raw English inputs
      1. “The research agent examined dataset 44.”
      2. “Dataset 44 was studied by the research agent.”
      3. “The research agent analyzed dataset 44.”
Draft semantic extraction
Same meaning detected.
Canonical AASL
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
DS{id:ds.44 type:dataset}
ACT{id:a.analyze.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}
One meaning, one form.
________________


8.19 Official Canonicalization Rules Summary
#
	Rule
	1
	one canonical term per concept
	2
	canonical field order is mandatory
	3
	reusable entities must use IDs
	4
	IDs follow stable prefix patterns
	5
	semantically equivalent inputs collapse to one form
	6
	claim types use canonical slots
	7
	canonical output uses full slot names
	8
	free text is minimized and normalized
	9
	numeric forms are standardized
	10
	time uses ISO-8601 UTC
	11
	absent data is omitted
	12
	batches are dependency-ordered
	13
	hashes use canonicalized AASL
	________________


8.20 Recommended refinement
This is not a separate step, but I strongly recommend that from this point forward we use:
      * subject
      * object
instead of:
      * a
      * b
for canonical claim structure.
That will make the language stronger and easier to extend.
________________

AASL Specification
Step 9 — Canonical Object Templates (v1)
All objects follow this general structure:
TYPE{field:value field:value ...}
Fields must follow canonical ordering.
________________


9.1 Agent (AGT)
Represents an actor capable of performing actions.
Canonical field order
id role model cluster
Required fields
Field
	Meaning
	id
	unique agent identifier
	role
	actor ontology role
	Optional fields
Field
	Meaning
	model
	model used by the agent
	cluster
	cluster membership
	Example
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research model:mod.reasoning.v1 cluster:cluster.01}

________________
9.2 Model (MOD)
Represents an AI model used for reasoning.
Canonical field order
id type provider
Required fields
Field
	Meaning
	id
	model identifier
	type
	model role
	Optional fields
Field
	Meaning
	provider
	model source
	Example
MOD{id:mod.reasoning.v1 type:reasoning provider:local}

________________
9.3 Artifact / Dataset (DS)
Represents data, files, or resources.
Canonical field order
id type source
Required fields
Field
	Meaning
	id
	artifact identifier
	type
	artifact type
	Optional fields
Field
	Meaning
	source
	origin of artifact
	Example
DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset source:archive.noaa}

________________
9.4 Task (TSK)
Represents a unit of work.
Canonical field order
id op target priority
Required fields
Field
	Meaning
	id
	task identifier
	op
	operation
	target
	artifact or entity
	Optional fields
Field
	Meaning
	priority
	scheduling priority
	Example
TSK{id:t.analyze.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44 priority:high}

________________
9.5 Action (ACT)
Represents execution of a task.

Field
	Meaning

	op

	target

	Example



9.6 Claim (CLM)
Represents a reasoning result.

Required fields
Field
	Meaning

	Optional fields
Field
	Meaning

	related entity

9.7 Evidence (EVD)

9.8 Confidence (CNF)
Represents probability or confidence score.

target val
Required fields
Field
	Meaning

	referenced claim
	val
	confidence score
	Validation rule
0.0 ≤ val ≤ 1.0
Example
CNF{target:c.correlation.001 val:0.92}

9.9 Provenance (PRV)

AASL Specification
Step 10 — AASL Document Format (.aas)
10.1 Purpose of the .aas File Format
The .aas format is designed to be:
      * smaller than markdown
      * machine-native
      * deterministic
      * easy for agents to parse
      * efficient for indexing
      * optimized for semantic reasoning
      * verification-compatible
      * provenance-aware
Unlike .md, which is optimized for human readability, .aas is optimized for machine reasoning and agent communication.
________________


10.2 High-Level File Structure
Every .aas document follows a structured layout.
HEADER
LEXICON
OBJECTS
RELATIONS
PROVENANCE
VERIFICATION
FOOTER
Each section has a defined role.
________________


10.3 HEADER Section
The HEADER declares the document metadata.
Required fields
Field
	Purpose
	aasl_version
	language version
	ontology_version
	ontology version
	document_id
	unique document ID
	encoding
	AASL-T / AASL-J / AASL-B
	created
	creation timestamp
	author
	originating agent/system
	Example
HEADER{
 aasl_version:1.0
 ontology_version:1.0
 document_id:doc.climate.001
 encoding:AASL-T
 created:2026-03-08T04:30:00Z
 author:ag.research.01
}

________________
10.4 LEXICON Section
The LEXICON allows short symbol definitions.
This improves compression for large documents.
Example:
LEXICON{
 dataset:ds
 agent:ag
 task:t
 claim:c
 evidence:e
}
This means later references can stay compact.
________________


10.5 OBJECTS Section
This contains canonical object definitions.
Objects follow the templates defined in Step 9.
Example:
OBJECTS{


AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}


DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset source:archive.noaa}


TSK{id:t.analyze.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}


ACT{id:a.analyze.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}


CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}


}

________________
10.6 RELATIONS Section
RELATIONS capture structured links between objects.
This improves knowledge graph integration.
Example:
RELATIONS{


EVD{id:e.dataset.001 type:dataset_reference source:ds.climate.44}


CNF{target:c.correlation.001 val:0.92}


}

________________
10.7 PROVENANCE Section
PROVENANCE tracks traceability.
Example:
PROVENANCE{


PRV{target:c.correlation.001 source:dataset}


TIM{target:a.analyze.201 ts:2026-03-08T04:31:00Z}


}

________________
10.8 VERIFICATION Section
This section records verification outcomes.
Example:
VERIFICATION{


VRF{
 target:c.correlation.001
 status:verified
 method:consensus
 hash:vh.9921
}


}

________________
10.9 FOOTER Section
FOOTER ensures document integrity.
Example:
FOOTER{
 object_count:6
 relation_count:2
 verification_count:1
 checksum:chk.8831
}
This enables quick validation.
________________


10.10 Complete .aas Example
HEADER{
 aasl_version:1.0
 ontology_version:1.0
 document_id:doc.climate.001
 encoding:AASL-T
 created:2026-03-08T04:30:00Z
 author:ag.research.01
}


LEXICON{
 dataset:ds
 agent:ag
 task:t
 claim:c
}


OBJECTS{


AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}


DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset source:archive.noaa}


TSK{id:t.analyze.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}


ACT{id:a.analyze.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}


CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}


}


RELATIONS{


EVD{id:e.dataset.001 type:dataset_reference source:ds.climate.44}


CNF{target:c.correlation.001 val:0.92}


}


PROVENANCE{


PRV{target:c.correlation.001 source:dataset}


TIM{target:a.analyze.201 ts:2026-03-08T04:31:00Z}


}


VERIFICATION{


VRF{
 target:c.correlation.001
 status:verified
 method:consensus
 hash:vh.9921
}


}


FOOTER{
 object_count:5
 relation_count:2
 verification_count:1
 checksum:chk.8831
}


10.11 File Size Advantages
.aas files are smaller than .md because they:
      * remove narrative redundancy
      * use references
      * enforce canonical vocabulary
      * minimize text tokens
      * separate semantics from prose
Typical reductions for structured content could reach 5–20× smaller representations compared to natural language.
________________


10.12 Binary .aasb Format (Future)
For very large systems, a binary version should exist.
Proposed extension:
.aasb
Binary files would:
      * replace strings with indexes
      * compress ontology tokens
      * reduce network payloads
      * accelerate parsing
But this is a later stage.
________________

AASL Specification
Step 11 — AASL Compiler & Translation System
11.1 Purpose
The AASL Compiler System converts between human-readable information and canonical AASL representations.
It enables:
      * English → AASL conversion
      * Markdown / text → .aas conversion
      * .aas → human-readable output
      * canonical validation
      * semantic normalization
      * ontology enforcement
      * verification hash generation
This system ensures that AASL remains consistent and canonical across all inputs.
________________


11.2 System Name
The official name of the toolchain:
AASC — Atrahasis AI Semantic Compiler
This system contains multiple modules.
________________


11.3 Compiler Architecture
The AASC pipeline has six stages:
INPUT
  ↓
PARSER
  ↓
SEMANTIC EXTRACTOR
  ↓
ONTOLOGY MAPPER
  ↓
CANONICALIZER
  ↓
AASL GENERATOR
Each stage has a defined responsibility.
________________


11.4 Input Layer
Supported input formats initially include:

	Purpose
	English text
	human authoring
	Markdown (.md)
	documentation
	JSON
	structured APIs
	CSV / datasets
	data ingestion
	AASL draft text
	manual authoring
	Example input:

11.5 Parser
The parser reads raw input and identifies structural patterns.
Tasks:
      * sentence segmentation
      * entity detection
      * action detection
      * numeric value detection
      * artifact references
      * claim identification
Output example:
actor: research agent
action: analyze
target: dataset 44
claim: correlation
variables: temperature, CO2
confidence: 0.92


CONFIDENCE = 0.92
These primitives are not yet canonical.

11.7 Ontology Mapper
The mapper aligns primitives with the AASL ontology.
Example mappings:
Input
	Canonical
	research agent
	role:research
	study / examine
	op:analyze
	dataset 44
	ds.44
	temperature
	var.temp
	CO2
	var.co2
	Output example:
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
DS{id:ds.44 type:dataset}
ACT{id:a.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}

The canonicalizer enforces Step 8 rules.
Tasks:
      * normalize synonyms
      * enforce canonical field order
      * assign stable IDs
      * collapse duplicate objects
      * remove redundant structures
      * enforce ontology constraints
Example transformation:
Input:
CLM{id:c1 type:correlation a:var.temp b:var.co2}
Canonical output:
CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}


AASL Specification
Step 12 — Governance & Versioning
12.1 Purpose of Governance
AASL governance ensures:
      * language stability
      * backward compatibility
      * controlled ontology expansion
      * deterministic evolution
      * cross-system interoperability
The goal is to prevent uncontrolled forks of the language.
________________


12.2 Version Structure
AASL follows semantic versioning.
Format:
MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH
Example
1.0.0

________________
Version meaning
Level
	Meaning
	MAJOR
	breaking language changes
	MINOR
	ontology expansion or optional features
	PATCH
	clarifications or documentation fixes
	Example:
1.1.0
Means:
      * language core unchanged
      * ontology expanded
________________


12.3 Version Declaration
Every AASL document must declare:
aasl_version
ontology_version
Example:
HEADER{
 aasl_version:1.0.0
 ontology_version:1.0
 document_id:doc.climate.001
}
This guarantees parsers know how to interpret content.
________________


12.4 Backward Compatibility Rule
Official rule
New AASL versions must be backward compatible whenever possible.
Systems supporting version 1.x must still parse earlier 1.y documents.
Breaking changes require a major version increase.
Example:
1.x → compatible
2.0 → breaking change

________________
12.5 Ontology Expansion Rules
Ontology expansion is allowed but must follow rules.
Allowed changes
      * adding new object types
      * adding new claim types
      * adding new operation types
      * adding domain modules
Not allowed in minor updates
      * redefining existing ontology terms
      * changing meaning of existing operators
      * altering canonical slot structure
Example allowed expansion:
type:regression
type:anomaly_detection

________________
12.6 Domain Ontology Modules
The core ontology remains small.
Specialized fields extend it through modules.
Example modules:
Module
	Domain
	medical.v1
	medicine
	finance.v1
	finance
	physics.v1
	physics
	engineering.v1
	engineering
	Example usage:
CLM{
 id:c.med.001
 type:medical.diagnosis
 subject:patient.001
 object:disease.hypertension
}
This keeps the core language stable while enabling domain growth.
________________


12.7 Ontology Registry
A central registry maintains:
      * canonical ontology terms
      * operator definitions
      * claim types
      * artifact types
      * version history
Registry contents include:
operator definitions
claim ontology
artifact ontology
domain modules
version metadata
This registry ensures all systems share the same vocabulary.
________________


12.8 Proposal System
Changes to AASL should follow a proposal process.
Suggested process:
      1. Proposal submitted
      2. reviewed by maintainers
      3. tested in experimental namespace
      4. accepted into official ontology
Example proposal identifier:
AASL-PROP-2026-001
This prevents chaotic expansion.
________________

Experimental features must use a reserved namespace.
Example:
exp.analyze_advanced
exp.simulation_cluster
This allows testing without affecting the stable language.

My Recommendation for the Next Step

________________


If you want, the next thing we should design is:
AASC System Architecture
This will define:
      * the translation engine
      * semantic extraction models
      * ontology mapping engine
      * canonicalization engine
      * .aas file generator
      * developer interfaces
This is where AASL becomes a real software platform instead of just a specification.
Yes to the overall direction, with a few important constraints.
On whether .aas will upload and open on day one
A custom extension like .aas is not guaranteed to be accepted by current chat-style uploaders. OpenAI’s current Help Center says ChatGPT supports “all common file extensions for text files, spreadsheets, presentations, and documents,” which strongly suggests a brand-new extension may not be accepted everywhere until the uploader or connector explicitly allows it. OpenAI also notes expanded support for additional text and code formats, but that still does not imply automatic support for arbitrary new extensions. (OpenAI Help Center)
So the practical answer is:
      * CLI / filesystem-access workflows: yes, easiest from day one, because you can open whatever extension you want as long as your software knows how to read it.
      * Modern chat upload UIs: maybe, but not safely enough to depend on at first.
      * Models themselves: a model does not “understand” a new file type because of the extension. It only understands the file if the platform passes the file contents through and your runtime/parser knows how to interpret the structure.
That means the correct launch strategy is:
      1. make .aas the official format,
      2. also support .aas.md, .txt, or JSON export for compatibility,
      3. treat native .aas support in mainstream upload UIs as a later integration target.
In other words, AASL can exist from day one; universal uploader support cannot be assumed from day one.
________________


On whether you need to convert all training data upfront
No. You do not need to convert everything upfront.
Your proposed approach is better:
      * let the model or agent canonicalize relevant knowledge on demand,
      * store the converted result in Atrahasis shared memory,
      * reuse that canonicalized version later before doing the conversion again.
That is the right near-term architecture.
It gives you:
      * immediate use of existing natural-language corpora,
      * no giant one-time conversion project,
      * progressive buildout of an AASL-native memory layer,
      * caching of already-normalized knowledge.
The important refinement is this:
You should not think of it as the model “converting its training data” directly, because the model’s pretraining corpus is not usually exposed item-by-item as retrievable files. A more accurate design is:
      * when the system needs knowledge for a task,
      * it retrieves from available sources or generates a semantic interpretation,
      * then the AASC canonicalization pipeline turns that result into AASL,
      * then shared memory stores that AASL object graph for reuse.
So yes, progressive real-time canonicalization plus semantic caching is the right strategy.
________________


On checking shared memory first
Yes, absolutely.
The preferred retrieval order should be:
      1. check Atrahasis shared memory for canonical AASL knowledge,
      2. if not found, retrieve from raw sources or use model reasoning,
      3. canonicalize the result into AASL,
      4. validate/store it,
      5. reuse that AASL version later.
That is one of the strongest parts of the architecture.
It turns AASL into a semantic cache layer, not just a document format.
________________


On how a model will know what AASL means
By default, it will not.
A model will not automatically understand AASL just because the syntax exists. Until models are specifically trained or fine-tuned on it, they will need one of these:
      * an AASL interpreter/parser in software,
      * a system prompt / instruction spec that teaches the format,
      * examples of valid AASL objects,
      * or ideally all three.
So your instinct is right: in the early phase, you need a human-readable spec that explains AASL.
But I would not make the main artifact just a loose .md explanation. I would make three startup artifacts:
      1. AASL-SPEC.md
Formal language specification for humans and agents.
      2. AASL-PRIMER.md
Short operational guide for models and agents: how to read, write, and validate AASL.
      3. AASL-TESTSET.aas / .json / .md
Paired examples:
         * English
         * canonical AASL
         * explanation of why that mapping is correct
That combination will work much better than a single prose explainer.
________________


On telling agents to read the AASL spec first
Yes. That is a good workaround for the transition period.
Early deployment should assume:
         * every agent runtime gets the AASL primer/spec,
         * every orchestration environment can inject AASL parsing instructions,
         * every model-facing tool can convert English or markdown into canonical AASL before execution.
That remains necessary until you have models that are natively trained or adapted for AASL.
________________


On whether later AASL-native models still need English support
Yes, unless you deliberately build a closed machine-only stack.
In practice, you will still want:
         * English input support,
         * raw document ingestion,
         * automatic conversion to AASL.
So even when models are natively AASL-capable, you should still design an AASL converter layer.
That should become a first-class system component, not an afterthought.
________________


The most important architectural conclusion
You do not want to require humans to always type AASL correctly.
You want this stack:
Human / raw files
→ AASC converter
→ canonical AASL
→ Atrahasis shared memory / AACP / Verichain
That means AASL should be the internal canonical language, while English and common file types remain supported through conversion.
That is far more realistic and scalable.
________________


Recommended decisions before we design AASC
These are the choices I recommend locking next:
         1. .aas is the native format, but v1 must also support compatibility wrappers like .aas.md and JSON export.
         2. Atrahasis shared memory should act as an AASL semantic cache.
         3. AASC should be mandatory in the early stack.
         4. Every agent should receive an AASL primer/spec bundle until native AASL-trained models exist.
         5. Retrieval should always check canonical AASL memory before re-canonicalizing from raw sources.
That means your next build target is no longer just “translation software” in the abstract.
It is more specifically:
AASC v1 — AASL Converter, Validator, and Semantic Cache Interface
That system should handle:
         * English to AASL,
         * markdown/text to AASL,
         * AASL validation,
         * shared-memory lookup,
         * cache-first retrieval,
         * reverse rendering back to human-readable form.
If you want, the next step should be to design AASC v1 architecture in that exact form.
Yes — I can generate a .aas file right now.
However, there are two important distinctions to understand:
         1. I can produce the correct AASL text structure (the content of the file).
         2. The actual .aas extension is just a container — it behaves like any text file until specialized tooling reads it.
So a .aas file today is essentially:
AASL text specification
saved with the extension .aas
Later your tools (AASC, Atrahasis runtime, etc.) will parse it natively.

Example .aas File (Working Example)
Below is a valid AASL document following the specification you designed.
If you copy this into a file named:
climate_analysis.aas
you already have a functional AASL document.
HEADER{
 aasl_version:1.0.0
 ontology_version:1.0
 document_id:doc.climate.analysis.001
 encoding:AASL-T
 created:2026-03-08T05:00:00Z
 author:ag.research.01
}


LEXICON{
 dataset:ds
 agent:ag
 task:t
 claim:c
 evidence:e
}


OBJECTS{


AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}


MOD{id:mod.reasoning.v1 type:reasoning provider:local}


DS{id:ds.climate.44 type:dataset source:archive.noaa}


TSK{id:t.analyze.101 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44 priority:high}


ACT{id:a.analyze.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}


CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}


}


RELATIONS{


EVD{id:e.dataset.001 type:dataset_reference source:ds.climate.44}


CNF{target:c.correlation.001 val:0.92}


}


PROVENANCE{


PRV{target:c.correlation.001 source:dataset}


TIM{target:a.analyze.201 ts:2026-03-08T05:02:00Z}


}


VERIFICATION{


VRF{
 target:c.correlation.001
 status:verified
 method:consensus
 hash:vh.9921
}


}


FOOTER{
 object_count:5
 relation_count:2
 verification_count:1
 checksum:chk.8831
}

