It is the component that lets the rest of the world continue producing English, markdown, JSON, datasets, and mixed documents while Atrahasis converts all of that into a precise semantic language.
AASC is not just a translator. It is the semantic manufacturing system for AASL. It takes noisy, ambiguous, redundant, and inconsistently structured inputs and turns them into deterministic semantic artifacts that the parser, runtime, validator, storage engine, and agent systems can use.
It answers the question:
How does knowledge become AASL?
________________


1. Purpose of AASC
The Atrahasis AI Semantic Compiler exists to transform external information into canonical AASL and to transform AASL back into usable human-facing forms when needed.
Its core responsibilities are:
                                    * ingest raw inputs from multiple source formats
                                    * extract semantic meaning from those inputs
                                    * align that meaning with the AASL ontology
                                    * normalize it into canonical structure
                                    * assign stable identifiers and references
                                    * validate semantic correctness
                                    * emit canonical AASL documents
                                    * generate supporting diagnostics and traceability metadata
                                    * support reverse rendering from AASL into human-readable form
AASC is the system that allows AASL to be the internal language of Atrahasis without requiring humans to manually author everything in AASL.
________________


2. Position in the AASL Ecosystem
The compiler sits upstream of almost the entire AASL stack.
Flow:
English / Markdown / JSON / Dataset / Mixed Source
        ↓
AASC Input Adapters
        ↓
AASC Parsing + Semantic Extraction
        ↓
Ontology Mapping
        ↓
Canonicalization
        ↓
Validation
        ↓
AASL Generation
        ↓
Parser / Runtime / File Writer / Knowledge Storage / Agents
In reverse mode:
Canonical AASL
        ↓
AASC Reverse Renderer
        ↓
Human-readable English / Markdown / JSON / reports
So AASC is the bridge between:
                                    * external knowledge formats
                                    * internal machine-native semantic representation
________________


3. Compiler Design Philosophy
AASC must be designed as a full industrial-grade semantic compiler, not a lightweight converter.
Its design philosophy should be:
Multi-source
It must handle many input formats, not just English prose.
Canonical-first
It must always target canonical AASL, not merely parseable AASL.
Loss-aware
It should preserve provenance, uncertainty, source origin, and input traceability.
Deterministic where possible
Once semantic intent is extracted, downstream normalization must be deterministic.
Explainable
Compiler output should be auditable: a human should be able to see how the source became AASL.
Extensible
The compiler must support domain modules, new source adapters, and future ontology growth.
Bidirectional
The system must support both forward compilation and reverse rendering.
________________


4. What AASC Compiles
The compiler should accept multiple source classes.
4.1 Natural Language
Examples:
                                    * user prompts
                                    * research summaries
                                    * internal notes
                                    * instructions
                                    * reports
4.2 Markdown Documents
Examples:
                                    * specifications
                                    * technical plans
                                    * documentation
                                    * architecture notes
4.3 Structured Data
Examples:
                                    * JSON
                                    * CSV
                                    * tabular datasets
                                    * API responses
4.4 Draft AASL
Non-canonical or partially structured AASL that needs normalization.
4.5 Mixed Documents
Documents that combine prose, bullet lists, tables, JSON snippets, and code-adjacent metadata.
That means AASC is not a single compiler path. It is a family of coordinated compilation pipelines sharing a common semantic core.
________________


5. The Core Compilation Problem
AASC exists because raw source data is not already in canonical form.
For example:
The research agent studied dataset 44 and found a strong relationship between temperature and CO2 with very high confidence.
This contains:
                                    * actor
                                    * action
                                    * artifact
                                    * claim
                                    * relationship type
                                    * qualitative confidence
But all of that meaning is entangled in natural language.
AASC must separate that into machine-native parts, map those parts to the ontology, normalize the phrasing, and emit something like:
AGT{id:ag.research.01 role:research}
DS{id:ds.44 type:dataset}
ACT{id:a.analyze.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.44}
CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}
CNF{target:c.correlation.001 val:0.92}
That transformation is the heart of the compiler.
________________


6. Major Compiler Layers
The full compiler should be divided into layered subsystems.
6.1 Input Adaptation Layer
Understands the source format.
6.2 Source Parsing Layer
Extracts structural units from that format.
6.3 Semantic Extraction Layer
Identifies semantic primitives.
6.4 Ontology Mapping Layer
Maps extracted semantics into AASL’s allowed meaning system.
6.5 Identity and Reference Layer
Assigns IDs, links objects, and builds references.
6.6 Canonicalization Layer
Normalizes the output into one canonical form.
6.7 Validation Layer
Checks semantic and structural correctness.
6.8 Emission Layer
Produces .aas, AASL-JSON, AASL-Binary later, and reverse renderings.
This layered approach makes AASC extensible and maintainable.
________________


7. Input Adaptation Layer
The compiler should begin with format-aware input adapters.
Each adapter is responsible for transforming a raw file or payload into a normalized source representation the compiler core can work with.
Adapters should exist for:
                                    * English/plain text
                                    * markdown
                                    * JSON
                                    * CSV/tabular data
                                    * draft AASL
                                    * mixed documents
Each adapter should provide:
                                    * source type classification
                                    * content segmentation
                                    * format metadata
                                    * source location mapping
                                    * extraction hints
For example:
                                    * markdown adapter identifies headings, paragraphs, bullets, code fences
                                    * JSON adapter identifies keys, arrays, records
                                    * CSV adapter identifies columns, row schema, units
This prevents the compiler core from being burdened with every file format directly.
________________


8. Source Parsing Layer
Once adapted, the source parser breaks the content into intermediate structural units.
For natural language this may mean:
                                    * sentence segmentation
                                    * clause segmentation
                                    * entity phrase extraction
                                    * numeric phrase extraction
For markdown this may mean:
                                    * section parsing
                                    * heading hierarchy
                                    * list block extraction
                                    * inline structured fragments
For JSON this may mean:
                                    * object traversal
                                    * key-value record extraction
                                    * type inference
The goal here is not yet full semantics. It is to produce a normalized source structure that semantic extraction can work with.
So by this stage, the compiler should know:
                                    * where semantic content lives in the source
                                    * how it is segmented
                                    * what metadata surrounds it
________________


9. Semantic Extraction Layer
This is where source structure becomes semantic candidates.
The semantic extraction layer identifies:
                                    * entities
                                    * agents
                                    * artifacts
                                    * actions
                                    * claims
                                    * evidence
                                    * relationships
                                    * quantities
                                    * timestamps
                                    * constraints
                                    * provenance references
For example, from:
The verification agent confirmed the result in document section 4 with 97% confidence.
the extraction layer should identify:
                                    * actor = verification agent
                                    * action = confirm / verify
                                    * target = result
                                    * provenance reference = document section 4
                                    * confidence = 0.97
At this stage, the output is still not canonical AASL. It is a semantic candidate set.
The extraction layer should support:
                                    * rule-based extraction
                                    * ontology-guided extraction
                                    * model-assisted extraction where needed
                                    * confidence scoring on extracted elements
                                    * ambiguity preservation for later resolution
This is the first stage where compiler intelligence matters deeply.
________________


10. Semantic Candidate Model
The compiler should represent extracted information in an intermediate semantic candidate model.
This candidate layer is crucial because it separates:
                                    * source-dependent extraction
from
                                    * AASL-specific normalization
A candidate record may include:
                                       * candidate type
                                       * source phrase
                                       * semantic hypothesis
                                       * confidence score
                                       * source location
                                       * possible ontology mappings
                                       * ambiguity state
Example:
                                       * source phrase: “studied”
                                       * candidate semantic role: operation
                                       * candidate mappings: analyze, compare
                                       * confidence: high for analyze
This allows later layers to make explicit mapping decisions instead of hiding uncertainty.
________________


11. Ontology Mapping Layer
Once semantic candidates exist, the compiler maps them into AASL ontology terms.
This is one of the most important parts of the compiler because AASL requires:
                                       * fixed vocabulary
                                       * fixed object classes
                                       * fixed claim types
                                       * fixed verification states
The ontology mapper must:
                                       * choose the correct object type
                                       * choose canonical operation names
                                       * choose claim types
                                       * normalize roles
                                       * classify source artifacts
                                       * decide whether a semantic candidate is explicit enough to become a formal AASL object
Examples:
                                       * “examined” → op:analyze
                                       * “confirmed” → op:verify
                                       * “very likely” → confidence candidate
                                       * “paper appendix B” → provenance/document reference
The mapper should use:
                                       * core ontology registry
                                       * domain ontology modules
                                       * controlled synonym maps
                                       * context-based resolution rules
This is where the language’s controlled meaning system is enforced.
________________


12. Identity and Reference Assignment
AASL is reference-based, so the compiler must assign stable IDs and build references between objects.
This layer is responsible for:
                                       * generating object IDs
                                       * reusing known IDs where possible
                                       * linking actions to actors and targets
                                       * linking confidence to claims
                                       * linking verification to claims
                                       * linking provenance to source objects
This is not trivial. The compiler needs identity strategy rules.
For example:
                                       * if a dataset is already known in the shared semantic memory, reuse its ID
                                       * if a claim is new, generate a new claim ID
                                       * if a confidence object belongs to a claim, reference the claim ID
                                       * if multiple extracted fragments refer to the same object, unify them
This layer is what turns isolated semantic pieces into a coherent object graph.
________________


13. Canonicalization Layer
After ontology mapping and ID assignment, the compiler passes semantic structures into canonicalization.
This layer applies the rules already defined in the AASL specification:
                                       * one canonical term per concept
                                       * canonical field order
                                       * stable prefixes
                                       * normalized timestamps
                                       * normalized numeric values
                                       * canonical claim slots
                                       * omission of null fields
                                       * dependency-ordered batches
This is the layer that collapses semantic variety into singular representation.
Without canonicalization, AASC would just generate “valid enough” AASL.
With canonicalization, it generates the preferred official form.
This is essential for:
                                       * deterministic hashes
                                       * deduplication
                                       * storage normalization
                                       * consensus verification
________________


14. Validation Layer Inside the Compiler
Even though validation also exists as a standalone subsystem, AASC must include validation gates internally.
Before output is emitted, the compiler should validate:
                                       * object templates
                                       * ontology membership
                                       * reference integrity
                                       * numeric bounds
                                       * required fields
                                       * canonical field order
                                       * timestamp normalization
                                       * document section structure
The compiler should distinguish:
                                       * hard errors
                                       * recoverable warnings
                                       * uncertain mappings
                                       * low-confidence semantic conversions
This means compiler output should never just be a file. It should come with a compile report.
________________


15. Compiler Emission Layer
Once semantic objects are canonical and validated, the compiler emits output.
It should support:
15.1 Canonical .aas
The primary human-inspectable semantic artifact.
15.2 AASL-JSON
Useful for APIs, debugging, and interoperability.
15.3 Future AASL-Binary
Production compact form.
15.4 Reverse Human Output
English summaries, markdown reports, semantic explanations.
The emission layer should separate:
                                       * semantic object ordering
                                       * section placement
                                       * formatting policy
                                       * compatibility wrappers
That means output generation should be structured and configurable, not string-concatenation based.
________________


16. Reverse Rendering
AASC must not be only forward-facing.
It must also support:
                                       * AASL → English
                                       * AASL → markdown
                                       * AASL → report summaries
                                       * AASL → explanation traces
This is critical because humans will need to:
                                       * inspect what the machine thinks a document means
                                       * debug conversions
                                       * audit semantic decisions
                                       * publish readable summaries
Reverse rendering should support multiple modes:
Literal mode
Very close to the semantic structure.
Human report mode
Readable prose for users and reviewers.
Explanation mode
Shows how specific source content became specific AASL objects.
This makes the compiler explainable instead of opaque.
________________


17. Compiler Provenance and Audit Trail
Every compile operation should produce a provenance trail.
The compiler must record:
                                       * source file or source text identity
                                       * source spans
                                       * extraction decisions
                                       * ontology mapping decisions
                                       * canonicalization transformations
                                       * generated IDs
                                       * validation results
                                       * output artifact identity
That means every .aas artifact produced by AASC should be traceable not just to the original source, but to the steps used to create it.
This is essential for:
                                       * trust
                                       * debugging
                                       * governance
                                       * later verification systems
AASC therefore needs an internal compilation trace model.
________________


18. Compiler Modes
AASC should support multiple operating modes depending on the task.
18.1 Translate Mode
Convert human-readable content into AASL.
18.2 Normalize Mode
Take draft or partially structured AASL and canonicalize it.
18.3 Validate Mode
Check whether a document is valid and canonical.
18.4 Enrich Mode
Add inferred metadata like provenance or normalized IDs.
18.5 Reverse Mode
Render AASL into human-readable form.
18.6 Batch Ingestion Mode
Compile many files or datasets into AASL at scale.
These modes should all share the same compiler core, not be separate unrelated tools.
________________


19. Compiler Error Model
Because semantic compilation is complex, AASC needs a sophisticated error model.
Compiler failures may occur at several levels:
Input errors
Unreadable or malformed source.
Extraction errors
Unable to identify semantic intent clearly.
Mapping errors
Source meaning cannot be mapped to ontology.
Identity errors
Cannot assign or reconcile references safely.
Canonicalization errors
Output cannot be normalized cleanly.
Validation errors
Canonical AASL would violate the language spec.
Each error should include:
                                       * stage
                                       * message
                                       * source span if applicable
                                       * proposed resolution if possible
                                       * severity
                                       * whether fallback is possible
This makes the compiler usable as a real development system.
________________


20. Ambiguity Management
AASC must deal with ambiguity explicitly.
Not every source statement cleanly maps to one semantic object.
So the compiler should support:
                                       * ambiguity flags
                                       * multiple candidate mappings
                                       * unresolved semantic placeholders
                                       * confidence scores on mappings
                                       * optional human review workflows for uncertain conversions
This is important because a full AASL ecosystem cannot pretend ambiguous language is always resolvable automatically.
The correct design is:
                                       * extract
                                       * score
                                       * map where justified
                                       * preserve uncertainty where necessary
                                       * emit diagnostics or draft states rather than hallucinating certainty
________________


21. Domain Module Support
AASC must support core AASL plus domain extensions.
That means the compiler must be able to load:
                                       * base ontology
                                       * domain-specific concept mappings
                                       * domain-specific claim types
                                       * domain-specific extraction rules
                                       * unit normalization rules
                                       * reference conventions
Examples:
                                       * medical module
                                       * finance module
                                       * engineering module
                                       * legal module later if desired
This means compiler architecture must be plugin-oriented, not rigidly fixed to only one ontology forever.
________________


22. Shared Memory Integration
Even though broader shared memory is outside this one subsystem, AASC must be designed to integrate with it.
Before generating new IDs or emitting new knowledge objects, the compiler should be able to:
                                       * check for existing known entities
                                       * reuse semantic identities
                                       * detect duplicate claims
                                       * determine whether a claim has already been canonicalized
                                       * retrieve prior ontology mappings
This makes the compiler smarter and more efficient over time.
So AASC should include a semantic identity resolution interface for external memory lookups.
________________


23. Batch Compilation Architecture
AASC must support large-scale ingestion, not just one document at a time.
Batch mode should support:
                                       * folder compilation
                                       * corpus ingestion
                                       * dataset stream conversion
                                       * parallel processing
                                       * compile summaries
                                       * failure isolation
                                       * incremental recompilation
For example, converting a full repository of markdown architecture documents into AASL should be a first-class use case.
That means the compiler needs:
                                       * job orchestration
                                       * per-file diagnostics
                                       * batch-level reporting
                                       * reusable ontology caches
                                       * incremental change detection
________________


24. Compiler and Parser Relationship
AASC does not replace the parser.
The correct relationship is:
                                       * AASC generates draft or canonical AASL
                                       * AASL Parser reads the emitted output
                                       * Validator confirms correctness
                                       * Runtime loads the result
In some flows, the compiler may also invoke the parser internally after generation to verify round-trip correctness.
So AASC should be designed with parser-backed self-verification:
                                       * generate AASL
                                       * parse generated AASL
                                       * ensure the result matches expected semantic objects
This creates stronger compiler correctness guarantees.
________________


25. Compiler and Runtime Relationship
The runtime becomes the destination for compiled output.
AASC should be able to:
                                       * emit files
                                       * emit in-memory semantic objects directly into runtime
                                       * emit compile reports
                                       * emit provenance metadata
                                       * request canonical runtime insertion
That means the compiler should support both:
                                       * file-oriented workflows
                                       * direct semantic ingestion workflows
This is important because not all compiled knowledge will need to hit disk first.
________________


26. Developer Interfaces
AASC should be accessible through multiple interfaces.
CLI
For direct compilation and automation.
Examples:
aasc compile spec.md
aasc normalize draft.aas
aasc reverse knowledge.aas
aasc validate report.aas
Library API
For integration into services, agents, and IDE tools.
IDE integration
For one-click conversion, diagnostics, and preview.
Batch service interface
For ingestion pipelines and repository-scale conversion.
This keeps AASC from becoming a tool only usable by one environment.
________________


27. Internal Architecture of AASC
Internally, AASC should be modular.
Recommended internal subsystems:
Source Adapter Manager
Chooses the right input adapter.
Source Parser
Builds normalized source structure.
Semantic Extractor
Builds semantic candidates.
Ontology Mapper
Maps candidates to AASL ontology.
Identity Resolver
Generates and reconciles IDs.
Canonicalization Engine
Normalizes final semantic structure.
Validation Gate
Applies language and ontology rules.
Emission Engine
Writes .aas, JSON, and reverse outputs.
Compile Trace Recorder
Stores audit trail of transformations.
Compile API Layer
Exposes compiler functions externally.
This modularity keeps the compiler maintainable and allows targeted upgrades.
________________


28. Output Artifacts Produced by AASC
AASC should not just output a .aas file. It should output a full result package.
A compile result should include:
                                       * canonical AASL document
                                       * validation results
                                       * compiler diagnostics
                                       * source-to-object mapping trace
                                       * generated IDs
                                       * unresolved ambiguity records if any
                                       * compile metadata
                                       * optional reverse-rendered summary
This makes each compile event inspectable and reusable.
________________


29. What AASC Changes About AASL
Before AASC, AASL is:
                                       * a designed language
                                       * a parser target
                                       * a semantic runtime substrate
After AASC, AASL becomes:
                                       * ingestible from normal human workflows
                                       * producible from documents and data
                                       * auditable as a semantic transformation system
                                       * scalable as a knowledge conversion pipeline
                                       * accessible to humans without forcing manual AASL authoring
This is the component that makes AASL socially and operationally adoptable.
________________


30. Final Summary
The AASC Compiler Architecture is the semantic conversion engine of the AASL ecosystem. It accepts many source formats, parses and segments them, extracts semantic candidates, maps them to the AASL ontology, assigns stable identities, canonicalizes structure, validates correctness, records provenance, and emits canonical AASL artifacts and reverse human renderings.
It is not merely a translator. It is the system that manufactures machine-native meaning from the outside world.
With:
                                       * the parser
                                       * the runtime
                                       * and the compiler
AASL now has:
                                       * a way to be read
                                       * a way to be used
                                       * a way to be produced
________________


The next subsystem we should work on is:
AASL Validator Architecture
That is the right next step because after defining:
                                       * how AASL is parsed
                                       * how it lives in runtime
                                       * how it is compiled
we need to define exactly how the system proves that an AASL artifact is:
                                       * structurally valid
                                       * semantically valid
                                       * ontologically valid
                                       * canonically valid
                                       * safe to trust and store
AASL Validator Architecture
The AASL Validator Architecture defines how the system determines whether an AASL artifact is trustworthy enough to move forward in the ecosystem. If the parser makes AASL readable, the runtime makes it usable, and the compiler makes it producible, the validator makes it acceptable.
This is the subsystem that answers:
Is this AASL actually correct, coherent, canonical, and admissible?
That question matters at every level of the system. A malformed object should not enter shared memory. A semantically invalid claim should not be treated as trustworthy knowledge. A non-canonical representation should not be hashed for verification. A broken reference graph should not be handed to agents as if it were sound.
So the validator is not a minor helper utility. It is the quality gate of the AASL ecosystem.
________________


1. Purpose of the Validator
The validator exists to examine parsed and/or runtime AASL structures and determine whether they satisfy all required correctness rules.
Its responsibilities are:
                                       * verify structural correctness
                                       * verify object-template correctness
                                       * verify ontology correctness
                                       * verify identifier and reference correctness
                                       * verify canonicalization compliance
                                       * verify section/document correctness
                                       * verify numeric and temporal normalization rules
                                       * classify errors, warnings, and policy violations
                                       * produce machine-readable validation reports
                                       * support admission decisions for runtime, storage, hashing, export, and network transport
The validator does not compile meaning from raw text and it does not operate on raw characters like the parser. It operates on structured AASL representations and decides whether those representations conform to the standard.
________________


2. Position in the AASL Stack
The validator sits after parsing and before trusted downstream use.
Flow:
AASL Source / Generated AASL
        ↓
Parser
        ↓
AST / Runtime Objects
        ↓
Validator
        ├── pass → canonicalizer / runtime / storage / export / hashing
        └── fail → diagnostics / repair workflows / rejection / quarantine
It also integrates inside the compiler:
Raw Source
        ↓
AASC Extraction + Mapping + Generation
        ↓
Generated AASL
        ↓
Parser-backed Validation
        ↓
Canonical Output or Compile Failure
And inside runtime mutation flows:
Loaded Runtime Objects
        ↓
Mutation / Update
        ↓
Revalidation
        ↓
Commit or Rollback
So the validator is both:
                                       * an ingestion gate
                                       * an ongoing integrity mechanism
________________


3. Validator Design Philosophy
The validator must follow a stricter standard than the parser.
The parser’s job is to recover structure.
The validator’s job is to determine admissibility.
Its design philosophy should be:
Deterministic
The same input state must always yield the same validation result.
Layered
Different classes of validity must be checked separately and explicitly.
Explainable
Every failure must be attributable to a precise rule.
Complete
Validation must not stop at superficial syntax. It must cover structure, ontology, references, canonicality, and document integrity.
Configurable
Different operating environments may enforce different thresholds, but the core rule set must remain stable.
Safe
Invalid AASL must not silently pass into trusted contexts.
________________


4. What the Validator Validates
The validator must validate at multiple levels.
4.1 Syntax-Level Validity
Did the parser successfully produce structurally interpretable objects?
4.2 Schema-Level Validity
Does each object conform to its canonical object template?
4.3 Ontology-Level Validity
Are object types, roles, operations, statuses, and claim types permitted by the active ontology version?
4.4 Reference-Level Validity
Do references point to valid objects, and are the referenced object types appropriate?
4.5 Canonical-Level Validity
Does the artifact conform to canonical field ordering, slot naming, numeric/time normalization, and stable ID conventions?
4.6 Document-Level Validity
Does the document obey required section, metadata, and structural rules?
4.7 State-Level Validity
Is the artifact suitable for its intended lifecycle state, such as hashing, storage, verification, or publication?
These are distinct validation categories and should remain distinct in the architecture.
________________


5. The Core Role of the Validator in Trust
AASL is not just a formatting language. It is a machine-native semantic language intended for knowledge systems, agent communication, and verification-driven workflows. That means invalid AASL is not merely “bad syntax.” It is potentially:
                                       * misleading machine meaning
                                       * broken semantic graph structure
                                       * unverifiable knowledge
                                       * unusable runtime state
                                       * corrupted canonical hash input
The validator is the system that prevents that corruption from spreading.
It is therefore the first real trust boundary in AASL.
________________


6. Major Validator Layers
The validator should be designed as a layered engine rather than a single pass.
6.1 Structural Validation Layer
Checks parser-level structural integrity.
6.2 Template Validation Layer
Checks object templates and required fields.
6.3 Type and Value Validation Layer
Checks value kinds, ranges, and field typing.
6.4 Ontology Validation Layer
Checks vocabulary, enums, and module compliance.
6.5 Reference Validation Layer
Checks identity, existence, and relationship integrity.
6.6 Canonical Validation Layer
Checks normalization and canonical output compliance.
6.7 Document Validation Layer
Checks document section structure and metadata.
6.8 Policy / Admission Layer
Determines whether the artifact is allowed into a given target environment.
This layered architecture ensures that validation reports are precise and actionable.
________________


7. Structural Validation Layer
The structural layer is the first validation stage after parsing.
Its purpose is to confirm that the parsed result is structurally suitable for deeper semantic checks.
It should check:
                                       * parser failure state
                                       * incomplete objects
                                       * malformed field-value pairs
                                       * invalid nesting
                                       * invalid section blocks
                                       * unrecoverable parse diagnostics
This layer does not decide whether a role is valid or whether a claim type is allowed. It only decides whether the structure is solid enough for further validation.
Example failures:
                                       * missing required closing brace
                                       * field with no value
                                       * duplicate field delimiter
                                       * malformed section block
If structure fails badly enough, later layers may be skipped or run in degraded mode.
________________


8. Template Validation Layer
The template layer checks each object against the canonical object templates defined in the specification.
Examples:
                                       * AGT must have id and role
                                       * TSK must have id, op, and target
                                       * CNF must have target and val
                                       * TIM must have target and ts
This layer checks:
                                       * required fields present
                                       * unrecognized fields where disallowed
                                       * fields appearing more than once where disallowed
                                       * field ordering if the validation mode requires canonical compliance
                                       * object-type-specific expectations
This layer is one of the most important because it transforms “parsed object” into “valid AASL object candidate.”
________________


9. Type and Value Validation Layer
AASL has typed semantics, so values must be validated as values, not just strings.
This layer checks:
9.1 Numeric Constraints
Examples:
                                       * CNF.val must be between 0.0 and 1.0
                                       * max_time_ms must be non-negative
                                       * memory and cost fields must obey agreed constraints
9.2 Temporal Constraints
Examples:
                                       * timestamps must parse as ISO-8601 UTC
                                       * invalid local-format time strings should fail canonical validation
9.3 Boolean Constraints
Only allowed boolean values should be accepted where defined.
9.4 Identifier Constraints
IDs must obey allowed identifier patterns.
9.5 Enum-like Field Constraints
Fields like status, role, type, and op must match active ontology values.
This layer ensures values are not merely present but meaningful and properly formed.
________________


10. Ontology Validation Layer
The ontology validation layer checks whether object semantics belong to the active ontology version and module set.
This includes:
                                       * valid actor roles
                                       * valid operations
                                       * valid claim types
                                       * valid evidence types
                                       * valid verification statuses
                                       * valid artifact types
                                       * valid module namespaces
For example:
                                       * role:research may pass
                                       * role:random_thing should fail unless registered in an active ontology module
This layer must support:
                                       * core ontology validation
                                       * optional module validation
                                       * version-aware ontology compatibility
                                       * experimental namespace handling
Because AASL is ontology-driven, this layer is one of the defining parts of the validator.
________________


11. Reference Validation Layer
Since AASL is reference-based, reference validation is critical.
This layer checks:
11.1 Existence
Does every referenced object actually exist in the document or loaded semantic context?
11.2 Type Compatibility
Does the reference point to an object of an allowed type?
Examples:
                                       * ACT.actor should point to an AGT
                                       * CNF.target should usually point to a CLM
                                       * TIM.target may point to a broader set of object types depending on rules
11.3 Self-reference Rules
Prevent illegal or nonsensical self-references where disallowed.
11.4 Cross-document Reference Policy
If external references are permitted, validate whether they are resolvable or at least properly declared.
11.5 Duplicate ID and Shadowing
Detect duplicate object IDs or conflicting definitions.
This layer is what ensures AASL is not just a bag of objects but a coherent semantic graph.
________________


12. Canonical Validation Layer
The canonical validation layer enforces the normalization rules defined earlier.
This layer checks:
                                       * canonical field ordering
                                       * canonical slot naming
                                       * canonical operator naming
                                       * canonical ID prefixes
                                       * canonical omission of null or filler values
                                       * canonical timestamp normalization
                                       * canonical numeric normalization
                                       * dependency-ordered batch layout where required
                                       * canonical section ordering if applicable
This is the layer that determines whether an artifact is merely valid or fully canonical.
That distinction matters because:
                                       * valid-but-noncanonical AASL may still be reparable
                                       * canonical AASL is what should be hashed, stored as official, and used for deterministic equivalence
________________


13. Document Validation Layer
AASL also has a document format, not just object syntax.
The document validation layer checks:
                                       * required sections present where required
                                       * section names valid
                                       * section ordering rules
                                       * HEADER contains required metadata
                                       * FOOTER integrity values are coherent
                                       * section contents contain allowed object classes
                                       * document ID present and valid
                                       * version fields present and parseable
                                       * encoding declaration valid
For example, the validator should be able to catch:
                                       * missing HEADER
                                       * invalid ontology_version
                                       * VRF object incorrectly placed in OBJECTS if the policy requires VERIFICATION
                                       * mismatched object_count in the footer
This layer makes .aas a true validated document format instead of just a text container.
________________


14. State-Specific Validation
Not every context requires the same standard.
The validator should support state-specific validation profiles.
Examples:
Draft Validation
Used during authoring.
                                       * tolerant
                                       * reports issues
                                       * allows partial structure
Compile Validation
Used in AASC output.
                                       * stricter
                                       * requires semantic consistency
                                       * may allow unresolved future IDs only under explicit rules
Canonical Validation
Used before hashing and official storage.
                                       * requires full canonical compliance
Admission Validation
Used before runtime/shared memory insertion.
                                       * requires strong reference and ontology integrity
                                       * may require verification or provenance presence depending on policy
Publication Validation
Used before exporting or publishing semantic artifacts.
                                       * requires document completeness and stable metadata
This makes the validator useful across the entire lifecycle.
________________


15. Validation Modes
The validator should support explicit modes.
15.1 Strict Mode
Fails on any rule violation.
15.2 Tolerant Mode
Collects issues but tries to continue.
15.3 Canonical Mode
Checks canonicality in addition to ordinary validity.
15.4 Editor Mode
Optimized for real-time incremental diagnostics.
15.5 Policy Mode
Runs standard validation plus environment-specific admission rules.
These modes should be built on one core rule engine, not separate ad hoc implementations.
________________


16. Validation Reports
Validation should not return only pass/fail.
A proper validation report should include:
                                       * overall status
                                       * validation mode
                                       * language version
                                       * ontology version
                                       * list of errors
                                       * list of warnings
                                       * list of informational notes
                                       * affected objects
                                       * affected source spans
                                       * rule identifiers
                                       * suggested remediations where possible
The validator should emit reports in:
                                       * machine-readable form for pipelines
                                       * human-readable form for developers and reviewers
This makes the validator a practical engineering tool, not just a gatekeeper.
________________


17. Rule Registry
The validator should be driven by a formal rule registry.
Each validation rule should have:
                                       * rule ID
                                       * description
                                       * validation layer
                                       * severity default
                                       * applicability conditions
                                       * repair hint if available
Examples:
                                       * AASL-TPL-001 missing required field
                                       * AASL-REF-004 unresolved target reference
                                       * AASL-CAN-007 noncanonical claim slot name
                                       * AASL-DOC-003 missing HEADER metadata
                                       * AASL-ONT-006 invalid operation value
This rule registry is important because it makes validation:
                                       * explainable
                                       * testable
                                       * governable
                                       * extensible
________________


18. Incremental Validation
Because AASL will have IDE tooling and live editing, the validator must support incremental validation.
Instead of revalidating a whole document on every keystroke, it should be able to:
                                       * revalidate changed objects
                                       * revalidate objects affected by changed references
                                       * revalidate changed sections
                                       * reuse cached rule results where safe
This is especially important for:
                                       * editor diagnostics
                                       * large multi-object documents
                                       * shared semantic workspaces
So the validator must be architecture-aware of dependency graphs, not just flat files.
________________


19. Validation and Canonical Repair
The validator should not only detect issues. In some cases it should be able to support repair workflows.
Examples of auto-fixable or repairable issues:
                                       * reorder fields canonically
                                       * replace shorthand claim slots with canonical names
                                       * normalize timestamp to UTC
                                       * normalize enum case
                                       * remove explicit null fillers
                                       * move object to proper section if the rule permits repair
However, the validator itself should not always mutate objects directly. Instead it should:
                                       * report repair opportunities
                                       * optionally invoke formatter/canonicalizer flows
                                       * preserve explicit control over whether repair is applied
This separation keeps trust boundaries clean.
________________


20. Validation and the Compiler
AASC relies on the validator as an internal correctness gate.
Compiler flow should include:
                                       * generated semantic candidates
                                       * generated AASL
                                       * parser-backed validation
                                       * canonical validation
                                       * compile report generation
If validation fails:
                                       * compiler may halt
                                       * compiler may emit draft output with warnings
                                       * compiler may require human review for ambiguous mappings
So the validator is part of what turns the compiler into a serious semantic compiler rather than a loose translator.
________________


21. Validation and the Runtime
The runtime should integrate with the validator continuously.
Validation can happen:
                                       * on document load
                                       * on mutation
                                       * on transaction commit
                                       * before export
                                       * before canonical hashing
                                       * before insertion into trusted memory
The runtime should track validation state per object and per document.
Examples:
                                       * object validation pending
                                       * object validation passed
                                       * object invalid
                                       * document valid but noncanonical
                                       * document canonical and admissible
This lets downstream systems make informed trust decisions.
________________


22. Validation and Shared Memory Admission
Even though shared memory is a later subsystem, the validator must already be designed with memory admission in mind.
Before an object or document is stored in trusted semantic memory, validation should be able to answer:
                                       * is it structurally valid
                                       * is it semantically valid
                                       * is it canonical
                                       * are references resolved
                                       * does it meet provenance/verification policy
                                       * is it safe to admit under the target policy profile
So the validator is effectively the gate between:
                                       * candidate semantic artifact
                                       * trusted semantic memory
That role must be explicit in the architecture.
________________


23. Validation and Hashing
Canonical hashes must only be generated from objects that meet the required canonical validation threshold.
The validator therefore determines:
                                       * whether an object is canonical enough for hashing
                                       * whether all prerequisite fields are stable
                                       * whether unresolved or draft state disqualifies hashing
                                       * whether document-level canonicality is sufficient for checksum generation
This prevents two noncanonical variants of the same meaning from being treated as separate canonical truths.
So hashing depends on validator authority.
________________


24. Validation and Ontology Versioning
AASL supports versioning and future modules, so the validator must be version-aware.
It should support:
                                       * validating under declared aasl_version
                                       * validating under declared ontology_version
                                       * compatibility rules across minor versions
                                       * explicit rejection or warning for unsupported future versions
                                       * module-aware rule expansion
This means the validator is also one of the key mechanisms that enforces governance in practice.
________________


25. Policy and Admission Layer
Beyond pure language correctness, the validator architecture should include a policy/admission layer.
This layer decides whether a valid artifact is acceptable for a specific destination.
Examples:
                                       * valid but noncanonical → allowed in editor, not allowed in canonical store
                                       * valid and canonical but unverified → allowed in draft workspace, not allowed in trusted registry
                                       * valid with unresolved external references → allowed in partial semantic workspace, not allowed in final export
This distinction is essential:
                                       * validation answers “is it correct under the rules”
                                       * admission answers “is it acceptable for this environment”
They are related but not identical.
________________


26. Internal Validator Architecture
Internally, the validator should be modular.
Recommended subcomponents:
Validation Coordinator
Runs the validation pipeline.
Structural Rule Engine
Handles parser/structure-level checks.
Template Rule Engine
Handles object template checks.
Value Rule Engine
Handles numeric, temporal, and literal value checks.
Ontology Rule Engine
Handles vocabulary and module validation.
Reference Rule Engine
Handles graph/reference integrity.
Canonical Rule Engine
Handles canonical form checks.
Document Rule Engine
Handles document section and metadata checks.
Policy Engine
Handles environment-specific admissibility decisions.
Report Builder
Assembles validation results.
Incremental Validation Manager
Supports editor/live update validation.
This modular structure mirrors the layered rule model.
________________


27. Validation Outputs
The validator should produce more than a boolean.
A validation result package should include:
                                       * overall verdict
                                       * per-layer verdicts
                                       * per-object verdicts
                                       * document verdict
                                       * canonicality verdict
                                       * admissibility verdict for target profile
                                       * errors, warnings, notes
                                       * suggested fixes or normalization actions
                                       * validation trace metadata
                                       * rule coverage summary
This result package is what other systems consume.
Examples:
                                       * compiler reads it to decide whether build passes
                                       * runtime reads it to decide whether mutation commits
                                       * CLI reads it to show developer output
                                       * IDE reads it to underline invalid fields
                                       * storage engine reads it for admission control
________________


28. Security and Robustness Considerations
The validator is also a security boundary.
It must guard against:
                                       * malformed semantic injection
                                       * deliberate ID collisions
                                       * excessive reference fan-out
                                       * impossible or contradictory object states
                                       * oversized or pathological documents
                                       * invalid experimental namespace abuse
That means the validator should support:
                                       * hard limits
                                       * configurable thresholds
                                       * namespace restrictions
                                       * reserved prefix protection
                                       * strict conflict detection
This helps keep the broader semantic system safe.
________________


29. What the Validator Changes About AASL
Before the validator exists, AASL is:
                                       * parseable
                                       * compilable
                                       * operational in runtime
But there is no authoritative way to distinguish:
                                       * good from broken
                                       * canonical from merely readable
                                       * trustworthy from malformed
                                       * admissible from unsafe
After the validator exists, AASL becomes:
                                       * enforceable
                                       * quality-gated
                                       * canonically testable
                                       * admission-controlled
                                       * reliable enough to underpin larger systems
This is the subsystem that gives the language engineering rigor.
________________


30. Final Summary
The AASL Validator Architecture is the rule-enforcement and admissibility layer of the AASL ecosystem. It checks structure, object templates, value types, ontology conformity, references, canonicalization compliance, document integrity, and target-environment policy. It produces detailed validation reports, supports incremental and strict modes, and serves as the quality gate for compilation, runtime mutation, canonical hashing, storage, and trusted semantic workflows.
It is the system that determines whether an AASL artifact is not merely readable, but correct enough to trust.
________________


The next subsystem we should work on is:
AASL File Infrastructure Architecture
That is the right next step because we now have:
                                       * parser
                                       * runtime
                                       * compiler
                                       * validator
The next logical layer is to define how AASL artifacts are physically handled as files and containers, including:
                                       * .aas file reader
                                       * .aas file writer
                                       * document builder
                                       * section assembler
                                       * version metadata handling
                                       * compatibility wrappers
                                       * binary .aasb architecture
                                       * file import/export behavior
That is the next complete narrative buildout.
AASL File Infrastructure Architecture
The AASL File Infrastructure Architecture defines how AASL exists as a real artifact on disk, in repositories, across transfer systems, in build pipelines, and inside future Atrahasis tooling. If the parser makes AASL readable, the runtime makes it active, the compiler makes it producible, and the validator makes it trustworthy, the file infrastructure makes it portable, storable, exchangeable, and durable.
This subsystem answers:
How does AASL physically exist as a document, container, file family, and transportable artifact?
That question matters because AASL is not only an internal in-memory graph. It is also intended to become:
                                       * a native authoring format
                                       * a machine semantic document
                                       * a canonical storage artifact
                                       * a repository asset
                                       * a compiler target
                                       * a validation target
                                       * a transfer format between systems
                                       * a long-lived knowledge container
So the file infrastructure is not just “reader and writer code.” It is the architecture that defines how AASL is packaged, moved, versioned, imported, exported, wrapped, and preserved over time.
________________


1. Purpose of the AASL File Infrastructure
The file infrastructure exists to manage the full lifecycle of AASL artifacts as files and document containers.
Its responsibilities are:
                                       * define the canonical .aas document representation on disk
                                       * read AASL files safely and consistently
                                       * write AASL files in canonical form
                                       * preserve document metadata and version declarations
                                       * assemble section-based AASL documents from semantic objects
                                       * support compatibility wrappers where needed
                                       * support import/export across formats
                                       * support future binary AASL containers
                                       * support repository and build-tool integration
                                       * preserve identity, integrity, and traceability of semantic artifacts
The file infrastructure is therefore the boundary between:
                                       * semantic knowledge structures
                                       * persistent file artifacts
________________


2. Position in the AASL Ecosystem
The file infrastructure sits between storage/transport and semantic interpretation.
Flow on ingest:
Disk / Repository / Upload / Transfer
        ↓
File Infrastructure
        ↓
File Reader
        ↓
Parser
        ↓
Runtime / Validator / Compiler
Flow on emission:
Runtime / Canonicalizer / Compiler
        ↓
Document Builder
        ↓
File Writer
        ↓
Disk / Repository / Transfer / Archive
It also supports compatibility flows:
Canonical AASL
        ↓
Compatibility Export
        ↓
.aas.md / .txt / JSON wrapper
And future binary flows:
Canonical Semantic Document
        ↓
Binary Encoder
        ↓
.aasb
So the file layer is the operational packaging layer for the whole language.
________________


3. Design Philosophy
The file infrastructure must be designed as if AASL is a real long-term language ecosystem, not an experimental notation.
Its design philosophy should be:
Canonical-first
The file layer should preserve and prefer canonical AASL.
Container-aware
AASL files are structured semantic containers, not loose text blobs.
Loss-aware
File handling must preserve document identity, source metadata, and integrity state.
Compatibility-aware
The ecosystem must support wrapper formats and interchange without confusing the language itself.
Version-aware
Every file must clearly declare the language and ontology context required to interpret it.
Deterministic
The same semantic document should emit the same canonical file under the same rules.
Extensible
The file system must support future binary forms, signatures, embedded indexes, and module-aware metadata.
________________


4. What an AASL File Actually Is
An AASL file is not just any text with curly braces. It is a semantic document container with defined structural regions and metadata.
In the .aas text form, the file contains:
                                       * document header metadata
                                       * optional lexicon definitions
                                       * structured object declarations
                                       * relationship objects
                                       * provenance objects
                                       * verification objects
                                       * footer/integrity metadata
So the file is both:
                                       * a language artifact
                                       * a document container
This means file infrastructure must treat an AASL file as a first-class document type with:
                                       * internal sections
                                       * metadata contract
                                       * canonical emission rules
                                       * lifecycle expectations
________________


5. Core File Types in the AASL Ecosystem
The file infrastructure should formally define a family of file artifacts.
5.1 .aas
The native text AASL semantic document.
Primary role:
                                       * canonical human-inspectable semantic artifact
5.2 .aas.md
Compatibility wrapper for environments that only recognize markdown-like text extensions.
Primary role:
                                       * temporary interoperability transport
                                       * AI uploader compatibility
                                       * repository readability in limited tooling
5.3 .aas.txt
Fallback plain-text compatibility wrapper.
Primary role:
                                       * lowest common denominator transport
5.4 .aas.json
Structured JSON serialization of the same semantic content.
Primary role:
                                       * API interop
                                       * debugging
                                       * systems integration
5.5 .aasb
Native binary AASL container.
Primary role:
                                       * high-performance transport and storage
                                       * compact runtime ingestion
                                       * large-scale agent systems
These are not separate languages. They are different physical representations of the same semantic system.
________________


6. Canonical .aas Text File Model
The canonical .aas file should be the standard reference artifact for text form AASL.
It should preserve the section model already defined:
                                       * HEADER
                                       * LEXICON
                                       * OBJECTS
                                       * RELATIONS
                                       * PROVENANCE
                                       * VERIFICATION
                                       * FOOTER
The file infrastructure must define:
                                       * whether sections are required or optional
                                       * what ordering is canonical
                                       * what object classes are allowed in each section
                                       * how whitespace is normalized
                                       * how line endings are handled
                                       * how document integrity metadata is stored
This makes .aas a real file standard rather than just “save this text with a different extension.”
________________


7. File Reader Architecture
The file reader is responsible for safely loading AASL files from disk or equivalent storage into a form the parser can consume.
Its responsibilities are:
                                       * open local or remote AASL artifacts
                                       * identify file representation type
                                       * normalize encoding
                                       * preserve raw source content
                                       * extract file-level metadata
                                       * hand off clean source text or decoded binary payloads to downstream layers
The reader must support:
                                       * .aas
                                       * .aas.md
                                       * .aas.txt
                                       * .aas.json
                                       * later .aasb
The reader should not perform full semantic validation itself. Its job is to:
                                       * identify what the artifact is
                                       * decode it safely
                                       * normalize it for the parser/compiler pipeline
________________


8. Reader Ingestion Modes
The reader should support multiple ingestion modes.
8.1 Native Text Mode
Reads .aas directly as AASL text.
8.2 Compatibility Wrapper Mode
Reads .aas.md or .aas.txt, extracts the AASL payload, and treats it as canonical text content if appropriate.
8.3 JSON Serialization Mode
Reads .aas.json and maps it into internal semantic structures or text-equivalent parse input.
8.4 Binary Decode Mode
Reads .aasb and decodes it into semantic document structures.
8.5 Embedded Payload Mode
Allows future support for AASL content embedded inside other files or packaging systems.
This makes file handling flexible while keeping the core language stable.
________________


9. File Identification Strategy
The file layer must not rely only on filename extension forever.
It should identify artifacts through a combination of:
                                       * extension
                                       * declared encoding in metadata
                                       * file header signatures
                                       * container markers
                                       * content heuristics when needed
For example:
                                       * .aas strongly implies native AASL-T
                                       * .aasb strongly implies binary AASL
                                       * .aas.md implies compatibility-wrapped AASL text
                                       * .aas.json implies JSON serialization
Long term, native forms should also support clear header markers so the reader can verify that a file claiming to be AASL actually is.
________________


10. Encoding and Character Handling
The text file layer must standardize character encoding and normalization.
The safest default is:
                                       * UTF-8 text encoding
                                       * normalized line endings on read
                                       * stable emission rules on write
                                       * explicit handling of unusual whitespace
                                       * preservation of source offsets where needed for parser diagnostics
The file infrastructure must define:
                                       * how tabs are treated
                                       * whether trailing whitespace is preserved or normalized
                                       * newline normalization policy
                                       * Unicode acceptance policy in strings or comments
Because AASL is machine-oriented, the file layer should strongly prefer stable, normalized text conventions.
________________


11. File Writer Architecture
The file writer is responsible for turning canonical semantic documents into persistent artifacts on disk or in storage systems.
Its responsibilities are:
                                       * accept canonical semantic document structures
                                       * apply output format policy
                                       * assemble document sections
                                       * format objects in canonical layout
                                       * emit metadata and integrity fields
                                       * write stable output files
                                       * ensure deterministic emission where required
The writer must support:
                                       * canonical .aas
                                       * .aas.md
                                       * .aas.txt
                                       * .aas.json
                                       * later .aasb
The writer is not just a serializer. It is the official emission mechanism for stable artifacts.
________________


12. Document Builder
The Document Builder is the internal subcomponent that assembles semantic objects into a valid document container before the file writer emits the final artifact.
Its responsibilities are:
                                       * create HEADER
                                       * create LEXICON when used
                                       * group semantic objects into correct sections
                                       * sort objects in canonical order
                                       * populate FOOTER integrity metadata
                                       * enforce document-level structural consistency
                                       * prepare the document for canonical output
This builder matters because runtime state may not already be organized in file-ready order. The builder converts:
                                       * live semantic graph state
into
                                       * stable document structure
So the builder is the bridge between runtime semantics and file artifacts.
________________


13. Section Assembler
Inside the document builder, the Section Assembler decides how objects are placed into sections.
It must know:
                                          * what belongs in OBJECTS
                                          * what belongs in RELATIONS
                                          * what belongs in PROVENANCE
                                          * what belongs in VERIFICATION
                                          * what metadata belongs in HEADER
                                          * what integrity data belongs in FOOTER
This is not trivial, because some runtime objects may exist without a section context once merged across multiple sources. The assembler must decide:
                                          * where they belong in canonical output
                                          * how section rules are enforced
                                          * whether mixed placement is valid or repairable
This component is critical for deterministic file generation.
________________


14. Canonical Formatting Policy
The file infrastructure must define a canonical output formatting policy.
This includes:
                                          * section ordering
                                          * blank-line policy between sections
                                          * blank-line policy between objects
                                          * single-line vs multi-line object emission rules
                                          * indentation rules for multi-line blocks
                                          * comment preservation policy
                                          * footer formatting
                                          * ordering of objects within a section
This policy matters because canonical hashing and stable repository diffs depend on deterministic output.
The writer should therefore support:
                                          * canonical mode
                                          * readable mode
                                          * compact mode if desired later
But there must be one official canonical text formatting standard.
________________


15. Header Metadata Handling
The HEADER is the file’s semantic identity block. The file infrastructure must ensure it is correctly created, read, and updated.
The header should carry at least:
                                          * aasl_version
                                          * ontology_version
                                          * document_id
                                          * encoding
                                          * created
                                          * author
The file system layer must define:
                                          * which fields are mandatory
                                          * how defaults are assigned
                                          * what fields can be auto-generated
                                          * how updates affect immutability or provenance
                                          * whether additional metadata namespaces are allowed
This makes the file self-describing enough for future tools.
________________


16. Footer Integrity Handling
The FOOTER is the document integrity block.
The file infrastructure must define how it is generated and checked.
Potential fields include:
                                          * object_count
                                          * relation_count
                                          * verification_count
                                          * checksum
The writer should compute these values from the semantic document, not trust arbitrary runtime input blindly.
