The reader and validator should be able to compare declared values against actual document contents and flag mismatches.
This makes the file format suitable for:
                                          * consistency checks
                                          * archival integrity
                                          * later verification processes
________________


17. Compatibility Wrapper Strategy
Because many present-day systems do not recognize .aas, the file infrastructure must support compatibility wrappers as a first-class part of the architecture, not an afterthought.
Compatibility wrappers should preserve:
                                          * the exact AASL content
                                          * explicit indication that the payload is AASL
                                          * enough metadata for downstream tools to recover native form
For example, .aas.md should not be treated as “markdown content in spirit.” It should be treated as:
                                          * AASL content
                                          * carried inside a markdown-compatible transport wrapper
The file system should define:
                                          * wrapper conventions
                                          * payload boundaries if needed
                                          * naming conventions
                                          * native recovery rules
This prevents compatibility from corrupting the semantic standard.
________________


18. Import and Export Architecture
The file layer must support import/export behavior across all official representations.
Import means:
                                          * ingest .aas, .aas.md, .txt, .json, later .aasb
                                          * normalize into a stable internal document representation
Export means:
                                          * emit canonical .aas
                                          * emit wrapper forms
                                          * emit JSON serialization
                                          * emit binary serialization later
Import/export should be architecture-driven, not ad hoc file conversion.
This means the file layer should define:
                                          * representation families
                                          * conversion fidelity guarantees
                                          * metadata preservation guarantees
                                          * canonical equivalence rules across formats
That ensures that .aas, .aas.json, and .aasb all represent the same semantic truth.
________________


19. Round-Trip Integrity
A major requirement of the file infrastructure is round-trip stability.
The system should support:
.aas
  ↓ read
internal semantic document
  ↓ write
.aas
without semantic corruption.
Likewise:
.aas
  ↓ convert
.aas.json
  ↓ convert back
.aas
should preserve canonical meaning.
The file layer must define what round-trip integrity means:
                                          * textual identity where canonical mode is used
                                          * semantic identity always
                                          * metadata identity where possible
                                          * comment preservation only when supported by the chosen representation
This is essential for trust and tooling.
________________


20. Multi-Artifact Packaging
As the ecosystem grows, some use cases may require packaging multiple related AASL artifacts together.
Examples:
                                          * a semantic document plus compile trace
                                          * a semantic document plus validation report
                                          * a semantic document plus attached source excerpts
                                          * a semantic document plus signature/verification package
So the file infrastructure should anticipate future packaging mechanisms such as:
                                          * directory bundles
                                          * manifest-based AASL packages
                                          * signed semantic containers
                                          * archive bundles
This does not need to replace .aas, but it should be anticipated in the architecture so the ecosystem can grow cleanly.
________________


21. Binary .aasb Architecture
The binary AASL file form is a major part of the long-term file architecture.
.aasb should not be treated as an afterthought or merely compression. It should be designed as a native high-performance semantic container.
Its goals are:
                                          * lower storage size
                                          * faster load times
                                          * smaller transport payloads
                                          * efficient runtime hydration
                                          * stable indexed symbol encoding
                                          * future support for embedded indexes and signatures
The binary file architecture should include:
21.1 File Signature
Clear binary magic/header for identification.
21.2 Version Metadata Block
AASL version and ontology version.
21.3 Symbol Table / Enum Table
Maps repeated terms to compact codes.
21.4 Object Records
Encoded semantic objects.
21.5 Reference Table
Efficient object linking.
21.6 Provenance/Verification Blocks
Optional compact sections for traceability and trust data.
21.7 Integrity Block
Checksum or hash block.
Even if text form remains the authoring standard, .aasb should be part of the core architecture from the start.
________________


22. File Naming and Identity Conventions
The file infrastructure should also define naming conventions, even if not strictly mandatory.
Naming matters because files will live in repositories, toolchains, and exchange systems.
Conventions should address:
                                          * document naming patterns
                                          * relation between file name and document_id
                                          * version suffix conventions
                                          * wrapper naming conventions
                                          * binary naming conventions
Examples:
                                          * knowledge_claims.aas
                                          * knowledge_claims.aas.md
                                          * knowledge_claims.aas.json
                                          * knowledge_claims.aasb
File names should not be the sole source of truth, but conventions improve interoperability and tooling.
________________


23. Repository and Build Integration
AASL files are intended to live in repositories and build pipelines.
So the file layer should support:
                                          * stable diffs in text form
                                          * deterministic output for version control
                                          * file-based validation hooks
                                          * lint/format workflows
                                          * compile pipelines from .md to .aas
                                          * artifact generation in CI/CD-like systems
This means the file format must be:
                                          * deterministic enough for source control
                                          * strict enough for build checks
                                          * readable enough for review
                                          * canonical enough for machine trust
The file infrastructure is therefore part of the build ecosystem, not only runtime storage.
________________


24. File Locking, Mutation, and Save Semantics
Because AASL may be edited by humans and tools, the file layer should define safe save behavior.
It should support:
                                          * atomic writes
                                          * temp-file then replace semantics
                                          * rollback on failed write
                                          * checksum re-generation on save
                                          * revalidation on save where configured
                                          * mutation-safe canonical write paths
This matters because partial or corrupted writes could create semantically dangerous artifacts.
For larger systems, file layer design should anticipate:
                                          * concurrent editing
                                          * document version conflicts
                                          * optimistic locking
                                          * last-write detection
________________


25. Security Considerations
Because AASL is a semantic language that may eventually influence agent behavior, file infrastructure must be hardened.
It should guard against:
                                          * maliciously oversized files
                                          * malformed wrapper payloads
                                          * invalid declared encoding
                                          * binary container abuse
                                          * checksum spoofing
                                          * path traversal in import/export workflows
                                          * extension confusion attacks
So the file layer should always:
                                          * verify container identity
                                          * verify declared vs actual encoding
                                          * enforce size limits
                                          * sanitize file paths
                                          * separate trusted and untrusted import workflows
This makes the file system layer a security boundary, not just an IO utility.
________________


26. Preservation of Provenance Across Files
The file infrastructure must preserve provenance not only inside semantic objects but also at the artifact level.
That means a file should support tracking:
                                          * file creation origin
                                          * source compilation context
                                          * file export history if needed
                                          * wrapper/native transformations
                                          * artifact lineage
For example:
                                          * a .aas file compiled from a markdown spec
                                          * later wrapped as .aas.md
                                          * later re-emitted as canonical .aas
The system should be able to trace that chain cleanly when needed.
This is especially important for long-lived knowledge artifacts.
________________


27. Interoperability with Non-AASL Systems
The file infrastructure must support operation in ecosystems that do not know what AASL is.
That includes:
                                          * generic editors
                                          * file uploaders
                                          * APIs
                                          * data lakes
                                          * archives
                                          * code repositories
So it should provide:
                                          * compatibility wrappers
                                          * JSON serialization
                                          * content signatures
                                          * recoverable metadata
                                          * reverse rendering where useful
The goal is not to make AASL dependent on those systems, but to make AASL survivable and portable within them.
________________


28. Internal File Infrastructure Subsystems
Internally, the file architecture should be modular.
Recommended subcomponents:
File Type Detector
Identifies representation type.
File Reader
Loads and decodes artifacts.
Wrapper Handler
Handles .aas.md and .aas.txt compatibility forms.
Document Builder
Builds semantic document containers.
Section Assembler
Places objects into canonical sections.
File Writer
Emits deterministic text and wrapper outputs.
JSON Serializer / Deserializer
Handles .aas.json.
Binary Encoder / Decoder
Handles .aasb.
Metadata Manager
Handles header/footer and artifact-level metadata.
Integrity Manager
Handles checksums and future signatures.
Import/Export Coordinator
Manages format conversions.
This keeps file handling coherent and extensible.
________________


29. Relationship to the Other AASL Components
The file infrastructure depends on:
                                          * the language spec
                                          * document format rules
                                          * parser
                                          * runtime
                                          * validator
                                          * canonicalizer/compiler
It feeds:
                                          * repositories
                                          * editors
                                          * validators
                                          * runtime loaders
                                          * batch conversion pipelines
                                          * interoperability tooling
It interacts bidirectionally with:
                                          * compiler
                                          * validator
                                          * runtime
                                          * future storage and signing systems
So it is not peripheral. It is the persistent artifact layer of the entire AASL stack.
________________


30. What the File Infrastructure Changes About AASL
Before the file infrastructure exists, AASL is:
                                          * a language design
                                          * a parser/runtime ecosystem
                                          * a semantic model
But it does not yet have a complete physical life as a durable artifact family.
After the file infrastructure exists, AASL becomes:
                                          * a native file standard
                                          * a repository artifact
                                          * a portable semantic container
                                          * an import/export family
                                          * a long-term archival format
                                          * a real ecosystem file type with future binary evolution
This is the subsystem that turns AASL into a true document and artifact standard.
________________


31. Final Summary
The AASL File Infrastructure Architecture defines how AASL is physically represented, read, written, packaged, wrapped, exported, imported, versioned, and preserved. It governs .aas as the native canonical text file, supports compatibility wrappers like .aas.md and .aas.txt, defines JSON and future binary .aasb representations, and includes the reader, writer, document builder, section assembler, metadata manager, integrity manager, and format conversion mechanisms.
It is the layer that makes AASL not just a live semantic system in memory, but a durable and portable semantic artifact ecosystem.
________________


The next subsystem we should work on is:
AASL Query Engine Architecture
That is the right next step because we now have:
                                          * parser
                                          * runtime
                                          * compiler
                                          * validator
                                          * file infrastructure
The next logical layer is to define how agents, tools, and systems actually retrieve meaning from live AASL semantic state, including:
                                          * exact lookup
                                          * graph traversal
                                          * relationship queries
                                          * claim/evidence/verification retrieval
                                          * multi-document semantic search
                                          * query APIs over runtime objects
That is the next complete narrative buildout.
AASL Query Engine Architecture
The AASL Query Engine Architecture defines how live AASL knowledge is retrieved, traversed, filtered, and reasoned over once it exists inside the runtime. If the parser makes AASL readable, the runtime makes it active, the compiler makes it producible, the validator makes it admissible, and the file infrastructure makes it durable, the query engine makes it usable as knowledge.
This subsystem answers:
How do agents, models, tools, and systems ask questions of AASL and get back the right semantic objects, relationships, and trust context?
That question matters because AASL is not valuable merely as a storage notation. Its value comes from being a machine-native semantic substrate that can be queried precisely and deterministically. Agents need to retrieve claims. Verification systems need to find provenance. Coordinators need to locate tasks and actions. Human-facing tools need to inspect semantic structures. The query engine is the access layer that makes all of that possible.
So the query engine is not just a search utility. It is the semantic retrieval system of the AASL ecosystem.
________________


1. Purpose of the Query Engine
The AASL Query Engine exists to provide structured access to runtime AASL knowledge.
Its responsibilities are:
                                          * retrieve objects by identity
                                          * retrieve objects by type
                                          * traverse relationships across the semantic graph
                                          * filter by fields, state, section, document, and ontology class
                                          * retrieve attached evidence, provenance, confidence, and verification context
                                          * support multi-document semantic lookup
                                          * support exact deterministic queries
                                          * support higher-level semantic query patterns
                                          * provide stable query APIs for agents, tools, storage systems, and developer interfaces
The query engine does not replace the runtime. The runtime stores and manages live semantic state. The query engine is the mechanism that lets systems navigate and interrogate that state.
________________


2. Position in the AASL System
The query engine sits on top of the runtime and uses parser/runtime-loaded semantic objects as its substrate.
Flow:
.aas / compiled semantic artifact
        ↓
Parser
        ↓
Runtime
        ↓
Query Engine
        ├── Agent APIs
        ├── CLI
        ├── IDE tools
        ├── Visualizers
        ├── Validation support
        ├── Compiler support
        └── Future memory/storage interfaces
The query engine depends on:
                                          * runtime object store
                                          * reference registry
                                          * document registry
                                          * state metadata
                                          * ontology metadata
It serves:
                                          * agent reasoning
                                          * semantic inspection
                                          * developer tooling
                                          * storage/retrieval interfaces
                                          * verification workflows
So it is the semantic access layer of live AASL state.
________________


3. Why the Query Engine Is Necessary
Without a query engine, every AASL consumer would have to manually:
                                          * iterate over all objects
                                          * inspect object types one by one
                                          * resolve references by hand
                                          * reconstruct provenance trails
                                          * search verification attachments manually
                                          * build custom traversal code repeatedly
That would defeat the point of having a formal semantic language.
AASL is designed to make knowledge explicit. The query engine is what turns that explicit structure into usable retrieval capability.
It is the difference between:
                                          * having a semantic graph
and
                                          * being able to work with the semantic graph
________________


4. Query Engine Design Philosophy
The query engine must follow the overall AASL philosophy.
Deterministic
Exact semantic queries must yield predictable results.
Type-aware
Queries must respect object types and relationship semantics.
Reference-native
Traversal should treat IDs and references as first-class navigational constructs.
State-aware
Results should be filterable by validation, canonicalization, and verification state.
Document-aware
The engine must understand document boundaries and multi-document environments.
Explainable
Results should be traceable to specific objects, documents, and reference paths.
Extensible
The query engine must support future ontology modules and advanced query forms.
________________


5. What the Query Engine Queries
The query engine operates over the full runtime semantic substrate.
That includes:
                                             * AGT objects
                                             * MOD objects
                                             * DS objects
                                             * TSK objects
                                             * ACT objects
                                             * CLM objects
                                             * EVD objects
                                             * CNF objects
                                             * PRV objects
                                             * VRF objects
                                             * CST objects
                                             * TIM objects
It also operates over:
                                             * reference links
                                             * document membership
                                             * section membership
                                             * validation state
                                             * canonical state
                                             * verification state
                                             * provenance chains
So the query engine is not only querying “objects.” It is querying:
                                             * objects
                                             * relationships
                                             * context
                                             * state
________________


6. Major Query Classes
The query engine should support several major classes of query.
6.1 Identity Queries
Retrieve by exact object ID.
Examples:
                                             * get ag.research.01
                                             * get c.correlation.001
6.2 Type Queries
Retrieve all objects of a given type.
Examples:
                                             * all CLM
                                             * all VRF
                                             * all ACT
6.3 Field Queries
Retrieve objects by attribute values.
Examples:
                                             * all claims with type:correlation
                                             * all agents with role:verification
                                             * all constraints with max_time_ms > 1000
6.4 Relationship Queries
Traverse references between objects.
Examples:
                                             * all objects referenced by this action
                                             * all confidence records for this claim
                                             * all verification records attached to this claim
6.5 Document Queries
Retrieve objects by document membership or section.
Examples:
                                             * all objects in doc.climate.001
                                             * all objects from VERIFICATION section
6.6 State Queries
Retrieve by runtime state.
Examples:
                                             * all unresolved references
                                             * all canonical objects
                                             * all verified claims
                                             * all invalid objects
6.7 Composite Semantic Queries
Queries that combine multiple criteria.
Examples:
                                             * all verified correlation claims in a specific document
                                             * all actions by research agents targeting datasets
                                             * all claims with confidence above 0.9 and verified status
These classes form the core semantic query model.
________________


7. Query Engine Layers
The query engine should be layered internally.
7.1 Lookup Layer
Fast direct retrieval by ID, type, or section.
7.2 Filter Layer
Applies attribute and state filters to retrieved sets.
7.3 Relationship Traversal Layer
Navigates between linked objects.
7.4 Semantic Composition Layer
Builds higher-level domain queries out of primitive query steps.
7.5 Result Shaping Layer
Returns results in useful forms for downstream consumers.
This prevents the engine from becoming an unstructured collection of helper functions.
________________


8. Identity Lookup
The most basic query capability is exact ID lookup.
This should be constant-time or near constant-time using the runtime object registry.
Examples:
                                             * retrieve ds.climate.44
                                             * retrieve t.analyze.101
Identity lookup is foundational because:
                                             * all references are ID-based
                                             * many other query types begin with object resolution
                                             * tools and agents often know the exact semantic target they want
The query engine should provide:
                                             * exact lookup
                                             * not-found response handling
                                             * optional typed lookup
                                             * optional state-qualified lookup
Example:
                                             * get object by ID
                                             * get object by ID if canonical
                                             * get object by ID if verified
________________


9. Type-Based Retrieval
Type retrieval allows systems to inspect the semantic space by object class.
Examples:
                                             * get all CLM
                                             * get all AGT
                                             * get all PRV
This capability is essential for:
                                             * auditing
                                             * summarization
                                             * visualization
                                             * analytics
                                             * agent coordination logic
The engine should support:
                                             * all objects of type X
                                             * all objects of type X in document Y
                                             * all objects of type X in section Z
                                             * all objects of type X with state S
This forms the basis for many composite query workflows.
________________


10. Field and Attribute Filtering
The query engine must support filtering over object attributes.
Examples:
                                             * claims where type = correlation
                                             * verification records where status = verified
                                             * agents where role = research
                                             * constraints with max_time_ms >= 5000
Filters should support:
                                             * equality
                                             * inequality where meaningful
                                             * set membership
                                             * range filters for numerics
                                             * existence checks for optional fields
This capability lets systems move beyond crude type lookup into semantic narrowing.
________________


11. Relationship Traversal
Because AASL is reference-native, relationship traversal is one of the most important query capabilities.
The engine must understand:
                                             * outbound references from an object
                                             * inbound references to an object
                                             * semantically meaningful attachment relationships
Examples:
                                             * from an ACT, retrieve its actor and target
                                             * from a CLM, retrieve attached CNF, PRV, and VRF
                                             * from a DS, retrieve all actions targeting it
                                             * from an AGT, retrieve all actions performed by it
This means the query engine should not force consumers to manually inspect raw IDs. It should provide semantic traversal methods that understand the meaning of those references.
________________


12. Semantic Attachment Queries
Some AASL relationships are more than generic references. They are semantic attachments.
Examples:
                                             * CNF.target -> CLM
                                             * VRF.target -> CLM
                                             * PRV.target -> CLM or another object
                                             * TIM.target -> object
The query engine should expose special retrieval patterns for these.
For example:
                                             * get confidence for claim
                                             * get verification for claim
                                             * get provenance chain for object
                                             * get timestamps attached to object
These are not merely graph traversals. They are semantically meaningful retrieval patterns that should be first-class in the engine.
________________


13. Provenance Queries
Provenance is one of the most important semantic access patterns in AASL.
The query engine must support questions like:
                                             * where did this claim come from
                                             * what source objects contributed to this action
                                             * what provenance records attach to this object
                                             * what document and section originated this node
                                             * what semantic lineage exists for this object
This requires the query engine to unify:
                                             * runtime source metadata
                                             * explicit PRV objects
                                             * document membership
                                             * transformation provenance where available
AASL is intended to be traceable knowledge, so provenance querying must be a first-class capability.
________________


14. Verification Queries
The engine must also support verification-driven retrieval.
Examples:
                                             * get verification status of a claim
                                             * list all verified claims
                                             * list all disputed claims
                                             * retrieve verification method and hash for an object
                                             * retrieve all claims not yet verified
                                             * retrieve all claims whose verification status conflicts across documents
This is crucial because agents and systems will often need to decide whether semantic objects are trusted enough to use.
So verification state should be queryable as naturally as object type.
________________


15. State Queries
The query engine must understand runtime object state.
This includes querying by:
                                             * parsed
                                             * loaded
                                             * unresolved
                                             * validated
                                             * invalid
                                             * canonical
                                             * noncanonical
                                             * verified
                                             * pending
                                             * disputed
                                             * immutable
                                             * dirty
Examples:
                                             * all objects pending validation
                                             * all unresolved references
                                             * all noncanonical claims
                                             * all immutable verified objects
This is important because AASL is not only a semantic graph; it is a semantic graph with lifecycle state.
________________


16. Document and Section Queries
AASL is also document-oriented, so the query engine must support document-aware access.
Examples:
                                             * all objects from doc.climate.001
                                             * all objects in the VERIFICATION section
                                             * all claims in a given document
                                             * all provenance objects across a corpus
                                             * all documents containing a given object ID
This allows users and systems to work at both:
                                             * object level
                                             * document level
That duality is essential for real-world workflows.
________________


17. Multi-Document Query Model
The runtime is intended to support more than one document at a time, so the query engine must be multi-document aware.
That means it should support:
                                             * corpus-level queries
                                             * document-scoped queries
                                             * namespace-aware lookups
                                             * cross-document reference traversal
                                             * conflict-aware result reporting
Examples:
                                             * all claims across all loaded documents
                                             * all documents referencing ds.climate.44
                                             * all conflicting definitions of c.correlation.001
                                             * all verification records for this claim across the corpus
This is what turns the engine from a file query utility into a true semantic workspace query system.
________________


18. Conflict-Aware Queries
Once multiple documents are loaded, conflicts become query-relevant.
The engine should be able to expose:
                                             * duplicate IDs
                                             * conflicting claim variants
                                             * superseded objects
                                             * shadowed objects
                                             * disputed verification records
This matters because agents and tools need to be able to inspect semantic disagreement, not just hide it.
So the query engine should support explicit conflict queries such as:
                                             * all conflicting objects
                                             * all disputed claims
                                             * all IDs with multiple active definitions
That capability becomes important in larger distributed knowledge environments.
________________


19. Query Result Shapes
The query engine should support multiple result shapes depending on downstream needs.
19.1 Raw Object Results
Return actual runtime objects.
19.2 Object ID Results
Return only object identifiers.
19.3 Relationship Paths
Return traversal paths or graph edges.
19.4 Bundled Semantic Context
Return an object plus attached confidence, provenance, and verification.
19.5 Summarized Results
Return reduced summaries for UI or reporting.
This matters because not every client needs the same output shape.
For example:
                                             * a CLI may want a summarized table
                                             * an agent API may want full object bundles
                                             * a visualizer may want graph edges and nodes
                                             * a validator helper may want only IDs
________________


20. Query Language vs Query API
The query engine should eventually support both:
                                             * programmatic query APIs
                                             * a formal query language
The API layer is easier to define first conceptually:
                                             * get by ID
                                             * get by type
                                             * filter by field
                                             * traverse references
                                             * retrieve attachments
But long term, a declarative AASL query language should likely exist so systems can express more complex semantic retrieval in a stable, serializable form.
For now architecturally, the engine should be built so that:
                                             * internal core = semantic query model
                                             * API layer = immediate interface
                                             * declarative query language = later front-end on top of the same core
This avoids locking the engine to only imperative calls.
________________


21. Query Planning
Even in early design, the query engine should be thought of as a planned system, not just direct scans.
It should support:
                                             * choosing efficient indexes
                                             * minimizing unnecessary traversal
                                             * narrowing candidate sets early
                                             * reusing registries and caches
                                             * avoiding repeated graph walks when possible
A query planner can remain lightweight at first, but architecturally it should exist as a concept because AASL knowledge spaces may become large.
For example:
                                             * direct ID lookup should bypass general search
                                             * type filtering should use type registries
                                             * provenance lookups should use attachment indexes
                                             * document-scoped queries should use document registries
This keeps the system scalable.
________________


22. Indexing Strategy
The query engine depends on indexes built over runtime state.
At minimum, it should use:
22.1 ID Index
For exact object lookup.
22.2 Type Index
For fast type retrieval.
22.3 Section Index
For section-scoped queries.
22.4 Document Index
For document-scoped queries.
22.5 Reference Index
For inbound/outbound traversal.
22.6 State Index
For canonical/verified/invalid/etc. retrieval.
22.7 Attachment Index
For confidence/provenance/verification retrieval.
These indexes may be maintained by the runtime, but the query engine depends on them conceptually and operationally.
________________


23. Query and Runtime Mutation
Because runtime state can change, the query engine must handle mutation correctly.
It should remain consistent when:
                                             * objects are added
                                             * objects are updated
                                             * references are resolved
                                             * verification state changes
                                             * documents are unloaded
                                             * canonicalization replaces draft forms
That means the query engine must either:
                                             * update indexes incrementally
or
                                             * invalidate and rebuild affected query structures safely
It cannot assume the runtime is static.
________________


24. Query and Access Control
As AASL becomes part of larger systems, not every query should necessarily expose all data to all callers.
So the query architecture should anticipate:
                                                * scoped query views
                                                * object visibility filtering
                                                * document-level access restrictions
                                                * state-based visibility rules
                                                * provenance redaction where required
This may later integrate with governance and policy systems, but the query engine should be designed so that access control can be layered in without rewriting the retrieval core.
________________


25. Query and the Compiler
AASC will benefit from query capabilities too.
The compiler may need to:
                                                * check whether an entity already exists
                                                * reuse prior IDs
                                                * detect duplicate claims
                                                * retrieve known ontology mappings
                                                * inspect existing semantic memory before generating new objects
So the query engine is not only for runtime consumers after compilation. It also helps the compiler align new output with existing semantic state.
This is one of the ways the ecosystem becomes self-reinforcing over time.
________________


26. Query and the Validator
Validation also benefits from query support.
The validator may use the query engine to:
                                                * inspect duplicate IDs
                                                * check type compatibility of references
                                                * retrieve all attachments targeting an object
                                                * detect cross-document conflicts
                                                * inspect document-level object completeness
So the query engine becomes shared infrastructure not only for agents and tools, but also for core integrity systems.
________________


27. Query and Visualization
The semantic graph viewer and object explorer will depend heavily on the query engine.
They will need to:
                                                * retrieve an object and its neighbors
                                                * traverse attachments
                                                * expand provenance chains
                                                * inspect conflicts
                                                * show document membership
                                                * summarize verification state
This means the query engine should support not only exact retrieval, but graph-shaped result forms suitable for visual tools.
________________


28. Developer and Agent Interfaces
The query engine must expose stable interfaces for different consumers.
Developer/Tooling Interfaces
Used by:
                                                * CLI
                                                * IDE extension
                                                * visualizers
                                                * debugging tools
Agent Interfaces
Used by:
                                                * coordinators
                                                * workers
                                                * verifiers
                                                * future memory services
Library/API Interfaces
Used by:
                                                * AASC
                                                * validators
                                                * storage engines
                                                * runtime utilities
These interfaces may present different convenience methods, but they should all rest on the same semantic query core.
________________


29. Internal Query Engine Architecture
Internally, the query engine should be modular.
Recommended subcomponents:
Query API Layer
Accepts query requests from consumers.
Query Parser / Request Normalizer
Normalizes query requests into internal query plans.
Query Planner
Chooses indexes and traversal strategy.
Lookup Core
Executes direct ID/type/document/state lookups.
Traversal Engine
Handles graph navigation and reference walks.
Filter Engine
Applies field, state, and predicate filters.
Result Shaper
Formats results into requested shapes.
Cache Manager
Caches frequent query results where safe.
Access Scope Layer
Applies visibility and policy constraints.
This modularity will matter a lot as complexity grows.
________________


30. Performance and Caching
AASL query performance should be designed deliberately.
The engine should support:
                                                * cached ID lookups
                                                * cached document object sets
                                                * cached attachment sets
                                                * cached query plans for common patterns
                                                * invalidation on mutation
                                                * minimal graph recomputation
This matters because one of AASL’s goals is to reduce repeated semantic work. The query engine should reinforce that by making already-structured semantic knowledge cheap to retrieve.
________________


31. Error Handling in Queries
The query engine must have a clear error and response model.
Possible query outcomes include:
                                                * exact result
                                                * empty result
                                                * partial result with unresolved references
                                                * conflict result
                                                * access-restricted result
                                                * malformed query error
                                                * invalid type/field error
Errors should be precise and machine-readable, especially for:
                                                * invalid object type requests
                                                * invalid field predicates
                                                * illegal traversal patterns
                                                * unsupported query combinations
                                                * document scope failures
This keeps the query system predictable and safe.
________________


32. What the Query Engine Changes About AASL
Before the query engine exists, AASL is:
                                                * parsed
                                                * loaded
                                                * validated
                                                * stored
                                                * canonicalized
But semantic retrieval remains manual and fragmented.
After the query engine exists, AASL becomes:
                                                * navigable
                                                * inspectable
                                                * traversable
                                                * filterable
                                                * corpus-queryable
                                                * agent-usable as live knowledge
This is the subsystem that makes AASL feel like a real semantic operating substrate rather than a static graph in memory.
________________


33. Final Summary
The AASL Query Engine Architecture is the semantic retrieval layer of the AASL ecosystem. It provides exact lookup, type retrieval, field filtering, relationship traversal, provenance and verification access, state-aware querying, document-aware querying, and multi-document corpus querying over the live AASL runtime. It depends on runtime indexes and registries, serves agents and developer tools, and provides the access model that turns structured AASL into usable semantic knowledge.
It is the system that lets the rest of Atrahasis ask questions of AASL and reliably get back the right meaning.
________________


The next subsystem we should work on is:
AASL Developer Tooling Architecture
That is the right next step because we now have:
                                                * parser
                                                * runtime
                                                * compiler
                                                * validator
                                                * file infrastructure
                                                * query engine
The next logical layer is to define how humans and engineers will actually interact with the AASL ecosystem through:
                                                * CLI tools
                                                * IDE integration
                                                * formatting
                                                * linting
                                                * autocomplete
                                                * semantic inspection
                                                * graph visualization
                                                * authoring workflows
That is the next complete narrative buildout.
AASL Developer Tooling Architecture
The AASL Developer Tooling Architecture defines how engineers, researchers, and operators interact with the AASL ecosystem while building, inspecting, and maintaining semantic artifacts. If the parser, runtime, compiler, validator, file infrastructure, and query engine make AASL technically functional, developer tooling makes it usable in practice.
A new language ecosystem cannot succeed if interacting with it requires manually writing raw files, running low-level commands, or navigating semantic structures blindly. Developers need visibility, feedback, automation, and ergonomic workflows. The tooling layer provides these capabilities by integrating the AASL system into everyday development environments.
In effect, this subsystem answers:
How do humans effectively work with AASL?
________________


1. Purpose of Developer Tooling
The AASL tooling layer exists to make the language approachable, observable, and manageable during development and operational use.
Its responsibilities include:
                                                * assisting authors while writing AASL documents
                                                * providing real-time feedback on errors and rule violations
                                                * offering ontology-aware autocomplete
                                                * formatting AASL into canonical structure
                                                * visualizing semantic graphs
                                                * validating documents during editing
                                                * enabling command-line compilation and inspection
                                                * integrating AASL workflows with standard development pipelines
Without these tools, AASL would remain technically sound but difficult to adopt.
________________


2. Position in the AASL Ecosystem
Developer tools sit at the outer layer of the AASL stack, interacting with the internal components that have already been designed.
Typical workflow:
Developer / Engineer
        ↓
IDE / CLI Tooling
        ↓
Parser + Validator + Canonicalizer
        ↓
Runtime + Query Engine
        ↓
Compiler / File Infrastructure
The tools provide a human interface while relying on the underlying language subsystems to perform semantic operations.
________________


3. Categories of AASL Developer Tools
Developer tooling should be organized into several coordinated categories.
3.1 Command-Line Tools
Direct command interfaces for compiling, validating, formatting, and inspecting AASL artifacts.
3.2 IDE Integration
Editor extensions that provide syntax highlighting, semantic diagnostics, autocomplete, and navigation.
3.3 Visualization Tools
Graphical tools for exploring semantic relationships inside AASL documents.
3.4 Inspection and Debugging Tools
Utilities that allow developers to inspect runtime objects, trace references, and examine canonical transformations.
3.5 Build and Repository Integration
Tools that integrate AASL validation and compilation into development workflows and version-control systems.
Together, these categories form the operational interface for the language.
________________


4. Command-Line Interface (CLI)
The CLI is the most basic but also the most essential developer interface.
It allows engineers to interact with AASL from scripts, terminals, CI pipelines, and automation systems.
Typical CLI commands should include:
aasl validate file.aas
aasl compile document.md
aasl normalize draft.aas
aasl query knowledge.aas
aasl graph knowledge.aas
aasl export knowledge.aas --format=json
These commands correspond directly to the internal systems already designed.
For example:
                                                * validate invokes the validator subsystem.
                                                * compile invokes AASC.
                                                * normalize invokes canonicalization.
                                                * query invokes the query engine.
                                                * graph invokes visualization utilities.
The CLI should serve as the foundational developer entry point.
________________


5. CLI Architecture
Internally, the CLI should function as a thin orchestration layer that invokes the core subsystems.
Example execution flow:
CLI Command
      ↓
Argument Parser
      ↓
Command Router
      ↓
Subsystem Invocation
      ├ Parser
      ├ Validator
      ├ Canonicalizer
      ├ Query Engine
      └ File Writer
This design ensures the CLI does not duplicate language logic but instead exposes existing subsystems in a usable way.
________________


6. IDE Integration
AASL should have first-class support in modern development environments, especially Visual Studio Code.
IDE integration dramatically improves usability by allowing developers to see semantic problems as they write code.
Key capabilities include:
                                                * syntax highlighting
                                                * semantic error diagnostics
                                                * ontology-aware autocomplete
                                                * inline validation warnings
                                                * object navigation
                                                * reference resolution preview
                                                * document structure outline
This transforms .aas files from opaque text into interactive semantic documents.
________________


7. Syntax Highlighting
Syntax highlighting provides visual cues that make the language easier to read.
The editor should highlight:
                                                * object type tokens (AGT, CLM, ACT, etc.)
                                                * field keys (id, role, target)
                                                * identifiers (ag.research.01)
                                                * numbers
                                                * timestamps
                                                * section headers
                                                * comments
This simple feature significantly improves readability and reduces errors during manual editing.
________________


8. Ontology-Aware Autocomplete
Autocomplete should suggest valid values as the user types.
For example:
If a developer types:
AGT{id:ag.analysis.01 role:
The editor should suggest:
research
analysis
planning
simulation
verification
coordination
human
system
These suggestions come from the ontology registry and help ensure vocabulary consistency.
Autocomplete should also suggest:
                                                * object types
                                                * field names
                                                * ontology values
                                                * existing IDs in the document
This feature prevents many errors before they occur.
________________


9. Real-Time Validation
During editing, the IDE should continuously validate the document using the validator subsystem.
Errors might include:
                                                * missing required fields
                                                * invalid ontology terms
                                                * malformed identifiers
                                                * incorrect field ordering
                                                * unresolved references
Instead of discovering these problems later during compilation, developers receive immediate feedback.
This capability relies on incremental validation provided by the validator architecture.
________________


10. Navigation and Object Inspection
Large AASL documents may contain hundreds or thousands of semantic objects.
The editor should allow developers to navigate them easily.
Navigation capabilities should include:
                                                * jump to object by ID
                                                * find references to an object
                                                * list objects by type
                                                * show document section outline
                                                * highlight relationships between objects
This transforms the editing experience from reading raw text into navigating a semantic structure.
________________


11. Graph Visualization
One of the most powerful tools in the ecosystem will be the semantic graph viewer.
Instead of reading:
ACT{id:a.analyze.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}
CNF{target:c.correlation.001 val:0.92}
the developer can see a graph such as:
Agent → Action → Dataset
                ↓
               Claim
                ↓
            Confidence
This visualization makes it easier to understand how objects relate to each other and how knowledge structures are built.
________________


12. Semantic Graph Explorer
Beyond simple visualization, the explorer should allow interactive navigation.
Capabilities might include:
                                                * expanding relationships
                                                * filtering objects by type
                                                * showing provenance trails
                                                * highlighting verification state
                                                * detecting conflicts or duplicates
This tool becomes especially important when analyzing complex semantic documents.
________________


13. Debugging Tools
Developers need insight into how the system interprets AASL documents.
Debugging tools should allow them to:
                                                * inspect parsed AST structures
                                                * inspect runtime objects
                                                * view canonicalization transformations
                                                * see validation results in detail
                                                * trace reference resolution
These tools are particularly useful when debugging compiler or runtime behavior.
________________


14. Formatting Tools
Because AASL enforces canonical formatting, the tooling ecosystem must include automatic formatters.
Formatting tools should:
                                                * reorder fields into canonical order
                                                * normalize indentation
                                                * normalize whitespace
                                                * remove redundant values
                                                * rewrite shorthand fields into canonical names
Developers should be able to run:
aasl format file.aas
and receive a canonical version of the document.
This ensures consistency across repositories.
________________


15. Linting Tools
Linting is related to validation but focuses on style and best practices.
Examples of lint warnings might include:
                                                * noncanonical object ordering
                                                * missing optional metadata
                                                * inconsistent naming patterns
                                                * overly complex object structures
                                                * redundant provenance declarations
Linting helps developers maintain high-quality semantic documents.
________________


16. Repository Integration
AASL should integrate naturally with version control systems such as Git.
This requires:
                                                * deterministic canonical output
                                                * readable text format
                                                * stable object ordering
                                                * validation hooks in pre-commit workflows
Example workflow:
git commit
    ↓
pre-commit hook
    ↓
aasl validate + format
    ↓
commit accepted or rejected
This ensures semantic artifacts remain valid throughout development.
________________


17. Continuous Integration Support
Build pipelines should be able to run automated checks on AASL files.
CI tasks might include:
                                                * validating all .aas files in a repository
                                                * compiling markdown sources into AASL
                                                * verifying canonical formatting
                                                * running ontology compatibility checks
This keeps large semantic repositories consistent and trustworthy.
________________


18. Batch Conversion Tools
Large knowledge bases may need to be converted into AASL.
Developer tools should support commands like:
aasl ingest ./documents/
This command could compile thousands of markdown or JSON files into semantic artifacts using the AASC compiler.
Batch conversion tools are essential for large-scale knowledge ingestion.
________________


19. Documentation Integration
Developer tools should integrate with documentation workflows.
For example:
                                                * converting AASL objects into readable reports
                                                * embedding semantic explanations into documentation
                                                * generating diagrams automatically
This helps bridge the gap between machine semantic artifacts and human-readable documentation.
________________


20. Tooling Extensibility
The tooling architecture must allow future plugins.
Examples include:
                                                * domain-specific ontology helpers
                                                * specialized visualization modules
                                                * dataset inspection tools
                                                * verification audit tools
This ensures that the ecosystem can evolve without rewriting the core tooling system.
________________


21. Tooling Architecture
Internally, the developer tooling layer should consist of several cooperating components:
                                                * CLI command framework
                                                * IDE extension
                                                * syntax highlighting grammar
                                                * autocomplete provider
                                                * incremental validation engine
                                                * graph visualization engine
                                                * runtime inspection interface
                                                * formatting and linting modules
Each component should rely on the core subsystems rather than reimplementing language logic.
________________


22. Tooling Interaction with Core Systems
Developer tools should interact with the core AASL systems through stable APIs.
Examples:
IDE → Parser → Validator
CLI → Compiler → File Writer
Visualizer → Runtime → Query Engine
Formatter → Canonicalizer
This keeps tooling consistent with the language implementation.
________________


23. Developer Workflow
With full tooling, a typical developer workflow might look like:
                                                1. Write semantic objects in a .aas file.
                                                2. The editor highlights syntax and suggests ontology values.
                                                3. Validation errors appear immediately.
                                                4. The formatter normalizes the document.
                                                5. The graph viewer shows relationships.
                                                6. The CLI compiles or exports artifacts.
                                                7. The repository hook verifies correctness before commit.
This workflow allows humans to interact with AASL comfortably.
________________


24. What Developer Tooling Changes About AASL
Before tooling exists, AASL is a language that can technically be used but is difficult to interact with.
After tooling exists, AASL becomes:
                                                * readable
                                                * navigable
                                                * debuggable
                                                * writable
                                                * explorable
                                                * maintainable
The difference is similar to the difference between editing raw assembly code in a text file and using a modern programming environment.
________________


25. Final Summary
The AASL Developer Tooling Architecture provides the human interface for the entire language ecosystem. It includes command-line utilities, IDE extensions, semantic graph visualization tools, formatting and linting systems, debugging tools, repository integration, and batch conversion workflows. These tools rely on the core parser, runtime, validator, compiler, and query engine to provide real-time semantic feedback and automation.
Together, they transform AASL from a theoretical language specification into a practical engineering environment.
________________


Next subsystem to design
The next subsystem we should build is:
AASL Knowledge Storage Architecture
This will define:
                                                * how AASL documents are stored persistently
                                                * how semantic objects are indexed
                                                * how multi-document knowledge corpora are managed
                                                * how shared memory for Atrahasis is implemented
                                                * how semantic deduplication works
                                                * how large-scale semantic retrieval operates
This subsystem turns AASL into the knowledge backbone of Atrahasis.
AASL Knowledge Storage Architecture
The AASL Knowledge Storage Architecture defines how AASL moves from being an active runtime language into a persistent, scalable, shared semantic knowledge system. If the parser makes AASL readable, the runtime makes it active, the compiler makes it producible, the validator makes it admissible, the file infrastructure makes it durable, the query engine makes it retrievable, and developer tooling makes it usable by humans, the storage architecture makes it accumulative, persistent, reusable, and collective.
This subsystem answers:
Where does AASL knowledge live over time, how is it organized, and how is it retrieved and reused across documents, agents, systems, and workflows?
That question matters because AASL is not just meant to describe isolated files. It is intended to become the internal semantic substrate of Atrahasis. That means it must support:
                                                * long-term storage
                                                * multi-document corpora
                                                * semantic indexing
                                                * shared memory
                                                * deduplication
                                                * versioned knowledge
                                                * trust-aware retrieval
                                                * provenance retention
                                                * cross-agent reuse
So this is not just “save files in a folder.” It is the architecture of the AASL semantic memory layer.
________________


1. Purpose of the Knowledge Storage Layer
The knowledge storage layer exists to persist AASL semantic artifacts and expose them as a durable semantic corpus rather than isolated files.
Its responsibilities are:
                                                * store canonical AASL documents and their component objects
                                                * preserve document identity and version history
                                                * maintain indexes over objects, relationships, provenance, and verification state
                                                * support cross-document semantic retrieval
                                                * support shared semantic memory across agents and systems
                                                * support deduplication and canonical equivalence detection
                                                * support versioning, supersession, and conflict tracking
                                                * support provenance-preserving updates
                                                * support efficient rehydration into runtime
                                                * support trust-aware and policy-aware admission into persistent memory
This makes the storage layer the persistent counterpart to the runtime.
________________


2. Position in the AASL System
The knowledge storage layer sits downstream of validation and canonicalization and upstream of future shared-memory use.
Typical flow:
Raw source
   ↓
AASC Compiler
   ↓
Parser
   ↓
Validator
   ↓
Canonical AASL / Runtime Objects
   ↓
Knowledge Storage Layer
   ├── Document Store
   ├── Object Store
   ├── Indexes
   ├── Version Registry
   ├── Shared Semantic Memory
   └── Retrieval / Rehydration
On the retrieval side:
Agent / Tool / Compiler / Query request
        ↓
Knowledge Storage Layer
        ↓
Indexed semantic retrieval
        ↓
Runtime rehydration / direct query result
So the knowledge store is the bridge between:
                                                * ephemeral semantic runtime state
                                                * persistent semantic memory
________________


3. Design Philosophy
The knowledge storage system must be designed as a first-class semantic storage engine, not as a thin wrapper over files.
Its design philosophy should be:
Canonical-first
Only canonical or explicitly staged noncanonical forms should be stored under known states.
Document-and-object duality
The system must preserve both whole documents and decomposed semantic objects.
Reference-native
Storage must preserve and accelerate graph-like reference relationships.
Trust-aware
Validation, canonicalization, and verification state must influence admission and retrieval.
Provenance-preserving
Knowledge without traceability must not lose its origin and lineage.
Version-aware
Stored knowledge must support supersession, revisions, and multi-version coexistence rules.
Shared-memory oriented
The architecture must support many agents and systems using one semantic corpus.
Extensible
The store must support future ontology modules, signatures, binary encodings, and distributed deployments.
________________


4. What Is Being Stored
The knowledge storage layer stores more than .aas files.
It must persist:
                                                * full AASL documents
                                                * individual semantic objects
                                                * object relationships
                                                * document metadata
                                                * section membership
                                                * provenance graphs
                                                * verification records
                                                * canonical hashes
                                                * validation status
                                                * version and supersession records
                                                * indexes for retrieval
                                                * optionally compile traces and storage-level audit metadata
That means the store must support two complementary views:
4.1 Document View
The semantic artifact as a coherent document.
4.2 Semantic Object View
The artifact decomposed into addressable objects and relationships.
This dual model is essential.
________________


5. Core Storage Domains
The storage system should be split conceptually into several domains.
5.1 Document Storage
Stores the canonical document artifact and metadata.
5.2 Object Storage
Stores normalized object-level representations.
5.3 Relationship Storage
Stores explicit references and semantic attachments.
5.4 Index Storage
Stores retrieval indexes and lookup structures.
5.5 Provenance and Verification Storage
Stores traceability and trust-related structures.
5.6 Version and Conflict Storage
Tracks revisions, supersession, duplicates, and disputes.
5.7 Shared Memory View
Exposes the stored corpus as a live reusable semantic memory substrate.
This decomposition prevents the store from collapsing into either:
                                                * a file archive only
or
                                                * an object graph only
It must be both.
________________


6. Document Store
The Document Store preserves whole AASL artifacts as coherent units.
Its responsibilities are:
                                                   * store canonical .aas or equivalent canonical semantic representation
                                                   * preserve document identity
                                                   * preserve document-level metadata
                                                   * preserve section structure
                                                   * preserve document version information
                                                   * store document-level integrity values
                                                   * support retrieval of entire documents
                                                   * support export back to file infrastructure
A document should remain a first-class entity in storage, not merely a temporary packaging for objects.
This matters because:
                                                   * human review is document-oriented
                                                   * provenance is often document-oriented
                                                   * versioning often begins at the document level
                                                   * import/export workflows depend on full artifacts
________________


7. Object Store
The Object Store preserves semantic objects as individually addressable units.
Its responsibilities are:
                                                   * store objects by stable ID
                                                   * store object type
                                                   * store field data in normalized form
                                                   * preserve document membership
                                                   * preserve object lifecycle state
                                                   * support direct object retrieval
                                                   * support object-level deduplication and version checks
                                                   * support semantic graph hydration into runtime
The object store is what makes the AASL corpus more than a file archive. It allows:
                                                   * direct retrieval of claims
                                                   * direct retrieval of agents
                                                   * direct retrieval of evidence
                                                   * graph traversal without opening whole documents every time
This is the beginning of AASL as true semantic memory.
________________


8. Relationship Store
Because AASL is reference-native, relationships need dedicated storage treatment.
The relationship store should persist:
                                                   * outbound references
                                                   * inbound references
                                                   * semantic attachments
                                                   * cross-document relationships
                                                   * supersession and lineage links
