Governance is what allows AASL to grow without fragmenting.
________________


19. Final Summary
The AASL Governance and Evolution Architecture provides the framework for managing the long-term development of the language and its ontology. It establishes versioning rules, compatibility guarantees, extension modules, proposal processes, deprecation policies, and distributed upgrade coordination. By defining how the language evolves, governance ensures that AASL can remain stable, interoperable, and trustworthy as the ecosystem grows.
________________


Next subsystem to design
The next subsystem to design is:
AASL Security and Integrity Architecture
This will define:
                                                   * cryptographic identity for semantic artifacts
                                                   * document signing
                                                   * trust verification mechanisms
                                                   * protection against malicious artifacts
                                                   * artifact authenticity across distributed nodes
This subsystem will ensure that AASL artifacts can be trusted in hostile or untrusted environments.
AASL Security and Integrity Architecture
The AASL Security and Integrity Architecture defines how AASL artifacts, semantic objects, nodes, and exchanges are protected against tampering, forgery, corruption, replay, and malicious semantic injection. Up to this point, the AASL ecosystem has been designed as a language, runtime, compiler, validator, file system, query substrate, storage system, visualization layer, distributed synchronization protocol, and governed evolving standard. Security now addresses a different but equally foundational question:
How do we know an AASL artifact is authentic, unmodified, admissible, and safe to trust in hostile or semi-trusted environments?
This matters because AASL is not just storing text. It is intended to carry machine-usable meaning. A malicious or forged AASL artifact is not merely a bad file; it can become corrupted knowledge, false provenance, counterfeit verification state, poisoned shared memory, or manipulated inter-node synchronization. The security layer therefore protects both the integrity of semantic artifacts and the trustworthiness of the semantic network.
In effect, this subsystem makes AASL safe to use beyond a purely local, fully trusted development environment.
________________


1. Purpose of the Security and Integrity Layer
The security layer exists to ensure that semantic artifacts and distributed semantic interactions can be trusted within clearly defined boundaries.
Its responsibilities include:
                                                   * authenticating the origin of AASL artifacts
                                                   * detecting tampering or corruption
                                                   * protecting canonical integrity
                                                   * binding semantic artifacts to stable identities
                                                   * securing distributed exchange between nodes
                                                   * protecting storage tiers from malicious or malformed admission
                                                   * detecting replay or stale artifact injection
                                                   * preserving trust metadata across versioning and synchronization
                                                   * supporting signatures, checksums, and future trust proofs
                                                   * enforcing security policies around admission, federation, and retrieval
Without this layer, the rest of the ecosystem can still function technically, but it cannot safely operate across untrusted systems, organizations, or networks.
________________


2. Security Scope
Security in AASL must apply across several domains simultaneously.
Artifact Security
Protection of .aas, .aasb, and related semantic containers.
Object Security
Protection of individual semantic objects and their canonical identity.
Document Integrity
Protection against file-level tampering and corruption.
Provenance Integrity
Protection against forged origin records or broken lineage.
Verification Integrity
Protection against counterfeit confidence, verification, or trust states.
Node and Federation Security
Protection of synchronization and distributed exchange across peers.
Storage Admission Security
Protection against malicious insertion into semantic memory.
Runtime Safety
Protection against unsafe or malicious semantic state entering live runtime.
This broad scope is necessary because AASL semantics can move through many layers, and every one of them can become a trust boundary.
________________


3. Security Design Philosophy
The security architecture must align with the semantics-first nature of AASL.
Its core philosophy should be:
Canonical-first
Security must bind to canonical semantic form, not arbitrary text formatting.
Trust-explicit
The system must make trust visible and inspectable, not implicit.
Layered
Different threat classes should be handled by separate mechanisms.
Provenance-preserving
Security measures must not destroy traceability.
Federation-aware
The system must work across distributed nodes, not only within one machine.
Policy-driven
Security enforcement should be shaped by explicit policies, not hidden assumptions.
Fail-safe
Suspicious or unverifiable artifacts should degrade into quarantine, draft, or untrusted states rather than being silently accepted.
________________


4. What Must Be Protected
The security layer must protect all semantically meaningful units in the ecosystem.
This includes:
                                                   * canonical documents
                                                   * object identities
                                                   * reference relationships
                                                   * provenance records
                                                   * verification records
                                                   * version lineage
                                                   * ontology declarations
                                                   * node identities
                                                   * synchronization messages
                                                   * admission decisions
                                                   * storage tiers
                                                   * query visibility boundaries
Some of these are file artifacts. Others are runtime or network constructs. The security architecture must cover all of them.
________________


5. Threat Model
AASL security must be designed against a clear threat model.
Potential threats include:
                                                   * artifact tampering after creation
                                                   * forged provenance chains
                                                   * forged verification status
                                                   * malicious node pretending to be a trusted peer
                                                   * replay of outdated or superseded artifacts
                                                   * duplicate-ID injection attacks
                                                   * namespace spoofing
                                                   * malformed artifact floods
                                                   * binary payload corruption
                                                   * silent corruption in storage or transfer
                                                   * semantic poisoning via valid-looking but deceptive artifacts
                                                   * partial replacement of object bundles during synchronization
                                                   * admission of unvalidated artifacts into trusted tiers
A strong architecture does not assume all nodes or all files are trustworthy.
________________


6. Security Layers
The AASL security architecture should be divided into layers.
6.1 Integrity Layer
Ensures artifacts and objects have not been altered unexpectedly.
6.2 Authenticity Layer
Ensures the claimed originator is actually the originator.
6.3 Provenance Trust Layer
Ensures provenance chains are preserved and verifiable.
6.4 Transport and Exchange Security Layer
Protects inter-node transfer.
6.5 Storage Admission Security Layer
Protects knowledge stores from unsafe artifacts.
6.6 Access and Policy Layer
Controls who may read, write, promote, or synchronize artifacts.
6.7 Runtime Safety Layer
Ensures unsafe artifacts do not become active trusted knowledge.
This layered architecture allows different mechanisms to reinforce one another.
________________


7. Canonical Integrity as the Security Foundation
The first and most important principle is that security must bind to canonical semantic form, not arbitrary file text.
Because AASL is canonicalized, semantically equivalent artifacts should normalize to the same canonical representation. That means integrity checks should be computed over:
                                                   * canonical object structure
                                                   * canonical field order
                                                   * normalized identifiers
                                                   * normalized values
                                                   * canonical section layout where required
This prevents a malicious actor from changing superficial formatting while trying to preserve the appearance of equivalence. It also ensures that integrity checks reflect meaning, not incidental layout.
This principle makes canonical hashing the foundation of security.
________________


8. Checksums vs Canonical Hashes
The architecture must distinguish between different integrity constructs.
8.1 File Checksum
Detects accidental corruption of a specific emitted artifact.
Examples:
                                                   * storage corruption
                                                   * transfer corruption
                                                   * truncation
This may be recorded in the FOOTER or storage metadata.
8.2 Canonical Semantic Hash
Represents the identity of canonical semantic content.
This is computed over canonical AASL semantics, not raw source bytes.
It supports:
                                                   * deduplication
                                                   * semantic equivalence
                                                   * trust binding
                                                   * version comparison
                                                   * federation reconciliation
Both are needed:
                                                   * checksum protects exact artifact bytes
                                                   * canonical hash protects semantic identity
________________


9. Artifact Signing
To establish authenticity, AASL artifacts should support digital signatures.
A signature should bind at least:
                                                   * canonical semantic hash
                                                   * artifact/document ID
                                                   * origin node or author identity
                                                   * timestamp
                                                   * version metadata
This allows a receiving system to answer:
                                                   * who signed this artifact
                                                   * what exact semantic content they signed
                                                   * whether it has been altered since signing
Signatures should not depend on uncontrolled formatting. They must sign the canonical form or a canonical-hash-derived manifest.
This ensures authenticity survives export/import and round-trip transformations.
________________


10. Object-Level Integrity
In addition to full-document integrity, the architecture should support object-level integrity where useful.
This allows:
                                                   * individual claims to be hashed and signed
                                                   * object bundles to be protected
                                                   * distributed systems to exchange subsets of documents securely
                                                   * object supersession to preserve trust continuity
Object-level integrity is especially important in distributed or shared-memory contexts where systems may synchronize semantic objects without always moving full documents.
Examples of object-level protected units:
                                                   * a claim
                                                   * a verification record
                                                   * a provenance bundle
                                                   * a claim + confidence + provenance + verification set
________________


11. Identity and Key Model
The security layer must define stable identities for entities that originate or authorize semantic artifacts.
This includes:
Node identities
Federation peers and storage authorities.
Service identities
Compiler services, validation services, storage promotion services.
Agent identities
Where relevant for semantic generation or verification.
Human or organizational identities
Where artifacts are intended to be attributable beyond machine nodes.
Each identity should be bound to cryptographic keys or an equivalent trust root.
This allows the ecosystem to answer:
                                                   * which node emitted this artifact
                                                   * which authority signed this document
                                                   * whether this verification record came from an authorized source
________________


12. Trust Anchors
The architecture needs trust anchors to determine whether identities should be believed.
Trust anchors may include:
                                                   * local trusted key registries
                                                   * organizational root keys
                                                   * federation trust registries
                                                   * signed trust lists
                                                   * future trust-weighted reputation systems
A signature alone does not imply trust. It only proves a key signed the artifact. The system must also know whether that key belongs to an accepted trust domain.
This distinction is important:
                                                   * authenticity proves origin
                                                   * trust policy determines acceptance
________________


13. Provenance Integrity
AASL already models provenance semantically. The security layer must protect that provenance from forgery and silent breakage.
Provenance integrity means:
                                                   * provenance links must remain intact
                                                   * source metadata must not be rewritable without detection
                                                   * compiler and transformation lineage should be append-only or explicitly versioned
                                                   * provenance claims may themselves need signatures or protected hashes
In a distributed system, provenance should not collapse when an artifact moves between nodes. Instead, each transfer or transformation should append to a protected provenance chain.
This makes provenance not only traceable, but trustworthy.
________________


14. Verification Record Integrity
Verification data is especially sensitive because it may influence trust decisions.
Security protections should ensure that:
                                                   * VRF objects cannot be forged without detection
                                                   * verification status changes are attributable
                                                   * verification method declarations are authentic
                                                   * verification hashes match the actual canonical target
                                                   * outdated or superseded verification records cannot masquerade as current ones
In practice, this means:
                                                   * verification records should be bound to the exact target object/hash
                                                   * verification records should carry signer identity
                                                   * verification records should carry issuance time and status lineage where relevant
This protects the trust layer from counterfeit certainty.
________________


15. Admission Security
The knowledge store should never accept an artifact into high-trust tiers solely because it parses.
Admission security should require checks such as:
                                                   * structural validation
                                                   * canonical validation
                                                   * signature verification where required
                                                   * origin trust evaluation
                                                   * provenance integrity checks
                                                   * duplicate/conflict checks
                                                   * policy threshold checks
The storage layer should therefore support multiple admission outcomes:
                                                   * reject
                                                   * quarantine
                                                   * store as untrusted draft
                                                   * store as canonical but untrusted
                                                   * store as trusted/verified
This is essential because not every valid semantic artifact is trustworthy enough for the same tier.
________________


16. Quarantine and Suspicion Handling
Artifacts that are suspicious but not definitively malformed should not necessarily be discarded immediately.
The security layer should support quarantine states such as:
                                                   * signature missing
                                                   * signature invalid
                                                   * origin unknown
                                                   * canonical hash mismatch
                                                   * provenance discontinuity
                                                   * replay suspected
                                                   * policy violation
                                                   * conflict with trusted artifact
Quarantine allows:
                                                   * later review
                                                   * forensic inspection
                                                   * policy decisions
                                                   * selective manual promotion
This prevents the system from being forced into a binary accept/reject model too early.
________________


17. Replay Protection
In distributed systems, a common attack is replaying old but valid artifacts to confuse semantic state.
The security layer should detect replay scenarios such as:
                                                   * reintroduction of superseded documents
                                                   * re-propagation of outdated verification records
                                                   * old versions presented as current artifacts
                                                   * repeated synchronization of stale state to trigger conflict or rollback conditions
Replay protection mechanisms may include:
                                                   * monotonic version tracking
                                                   * supersession registries
                                                   * signed timestamps
                                                   * artifact freshness windows where relevant
                                                   * federation-level replay caches or seen-artifact registries
This helps ensure that “previously valid” does not become “silently current.”
________________


18. Synchronization Security
When nodes exchange AASL artifacts, the synchronization process itself must be secured.
Synchronization security should include:
                                                   * peer authentication
                                                   * transport encryption where appropriate
                                                   * signed artifact exchange
                                                   * integrity verification on receipt
                                                   * compatibility and policy checks before admission
                                                   * rate limiting and backpressure
                                                   * explicit synchronization scopes
This ensures that federation does not become a vector for:
                                                   * forged artifact injection
                                                   * node impersonation
                                                   * artifact flooding
                                                   * partial or corrupted updates
________________


19. Namespace and Ontology Security
Because AASL supports ontology modules and evolution, attackers could attempt namespace spoofing or semantic confusion.
The security layer should therefore protect:
                                                   * reserved namespaces
                                                   * official ontology registries
                                                   * module origin authenticity
                                                   * ontology version claims
                                                   * experimental namespace boundaries
A receiving node should be able to verify:
                                                   * whether an ontology extension is legitimate
                                                   * whether a module declaration came from an accepted source
                                                   * whether a claimed term belongs to an authorized registry version
This prevents semantic poisoning through fake vocabulary.
________________


20. Storage Integrity and Tamper Detection
Once artifacts are stored, the store itself becomes part of the trust boundary.
Storage security should include:
                                                   * file-level integrity checks
                                                   * object-level consistency checks
                                                   * index integrity verification
                                                   * audit trails for mutation and promotion
                                                   * immutable historical storage for trusted tiers where appropriate
                                                   * tamper-evident logs of changes
A stored semantic artifact should not be silently mutable without record. Trusted tiers especially should behave in a tamper-evident manner.
This is crucial if stored semantic knowledge is later used for agent reasoning or decision support.
________________


21. Access Control and Permission Model
Not every actor in the AASL ecosystem should have the same powers.
The security architecture should anticipate permissions such as:
                                                   * read draft artifacts
                                                   * read canonical artifacts
                                                   * read trusted artifacts
                                                   * submit draft artifacts
                                                   * validate or canonicalize artifacts
                                                   * sign artifacts
                                                   * promote artifacts between tiers
                                                   * synchronize artifacts across nodes
                                                   * approve ontology modules
Permissions should be scoped to identities and policy domains rather than hardcoded assumptions.
This becomes especially important when multiple services and organizations participate in one semantic network.
________________


22. Runtime Safety
Even before persistent storage, runtime state itself is a trust boundary.
Runtime safety should ensure that:
                                                   * unvalidated objects are marked clearly
                                                   * unsafe artifacts do not masquerade as canonical/trusted runtime objects
                                                   * dangerous or malformed reference graphs do not get activated blindly
                                                   * policy-sensitive queries can exclude unsafe objects
                                                   * mutation actions are auditable and permission-aware
This prevents the live semantic substrate from becoming polluted by unsafe knowledge before storage ever happens.
________________


23. Security Metadata
Artifacts and objects may need associated security metadata beyond ordinary semantic fields.
This may include:
                                                   * signer identity
                                                   * signature block references
                                                   * trusted issuer status
                                                   * trust tier classification
                                                   * artifact issuance time
                                                   * integrity verification time
                                                   * admission outcome
                                                   * quarantine reason
                                                   * replay status
                                                   * origin trust domain
This metadata should not replace the semantic content, but it should travel alongside it in a consistent and queryable way.
________________


24. Audit and Forensics
The security layer must support investigation after the fact.
Forensic capabilities should include:
                                                   * artifact origin tracing
                                                   * signature verification history
                                                   * admission decision history
                                                   * synchronization path tracing
                                                   * supersession and replay tracing
                                                   * conflict provenance
                                                   * node interaction logs
This is especially important in a distributed network where semantic corruption may not be obvious at the moment it first appears.
AASL is intended to preserve meaning and provenance; the security layer extends that to preserve trust history.
________________


25. Security Policy Engine
Many security decisions are policy-dependent rather than universally fixed.
A security policy engine should be able to answer questions like:
                                                   * are unsigned artifacts allowed in draft tier
                                                   * are only trusted-node artifacts eligible for verified tier
                                                   * what signature algorithms or trust anchors are acceptable
                                                   * which ontology modules are allowed from which issuers
                                                   * whether stale but authentic artifacts may be imported at all
                                                   * what provenance continuity is required for promotion
This keeps the architecture flexible while preserving strong trust boundaries.
________________


26. Security in the Developer Workflow
Developer tooling should expose security state, not hide it.
For example:
                                                   * IDE should show whether a document is signed or unsigned
                                                   * CLI should verify signatures and integrity
                                                   * visualization tools should show trust tier and provenance authenticity
                                                   * repository workflows should support signing, integrity checking, and policy validation before promotion
This prevents a split between “developer workflows” and “security workflows.” In AASL, security must be part of normal semantic hygiene.
________________


27. Security and Governance Interaction
Security and governance are closely linked.
Governance defines:
                                                   * trusted registries
                                                   * official ontology issuers
                                                   * versioning rules
                                                   * deprecation policies
                                                   * federation trust models
Security enforces:
                                                   * authenticity of those registries and issuers
                                                   * integrity of version declarations
                                                   * controlled adoption of extensions
                                                   * authenticity of signed release artifacts
So governance without security can be spoofed, and security without governance lacks policy meaning. The two systems must reinforce each other.
________________


28. Security in Binary and Wrapper Formats
The security layer must apply consistently across:
                                                   * .aas
                                                   * .aas.md
                                                   * .aas.txt
                                                   * .aas.json
                                                   * .aasb
It should not matter which representation is used for transport; authenticity and canonical identity should remain consistent.
That means:
                                                   * signatures should bind to canonical semantics or a canonical manifest
                                                   * wrapper formats should not invalidate integrity checks
                                                   * binary containers should include secure manifest/integrity sections
                                                   * representation conversions should preserve trust linkage
This keeps trust stable across file infrastructure boundaries.
________________


29. Internal Security Architecture
Internally, the security subsystem should be modular.
Recommended subcomponents include:
Integrity Engine
Computes and verifies checksums and canonical hashes.
Signature Engine
Signs and verifies artifacts and semantic units.
Identity Registry
Manages node/service/issuer identities and trust anchors.
Admission Security Gate
Applies security checks during storage/runtime admission.
Federation Security Layer
Secures inter-node exchange.
Policy Engine
Applies security policies and trust thresholds.
Replay and Freshness Manager
Tracks stale or repeated artifacts.
Audit Log and Forensics Layer
Preserves security-relevant history.
Security Metadata Manager
Stores and exposes trust metadata.
This modular structure keeps security manageable and extensible.
________________


30. What the Security Layer Changes About AASL
Before the security and integrity layer exists, AASL can be:
                                                   * parsed
                                                   * compiled
                                                   * validated
                                                   * stored
                                                   * synchronized
                                                   * queried
But there is no strong assurance that the artifacts being used are authentic, untampered, current, or safely admitted.
After the security layer exists, AASL becomes:
                                                   * authenticity-aware
                                                   * tamper-evident
                                                   * trust-tiered
                                                   * resistant to forged semantic artifacts
                                                   * safer for distributed deployment
                                                   * suitable for adversarial or semi-trusted environments
This is the subsystem that makes AASL safe enough to move from a controlled internal ecosystem toward a broader federated semantic network.
________________


31. Final Summary
The AASL Security and Integrity Architecture protects semantic artifacts, objects, provenance chains, verification records, storage tiers, runtime state, and distributed synchronization against tampering, forgery, replay, corruption, and malicious semantic injection. It anchors trust to canonical semantic form, supports checksums and canonical hashes, introduces signatures and identity models, enforces admission security, preserves trust metadata, and secures federation across nodes. It ensures that AASL artifacts are not only structurally valid and semantically meaningful, but also authentic and safe to trust.
________________


The next subsystem we should work on is:
AASL Testing, Conformance, and Certification Architecture
That is the right next step because we now have:
                                                   * parser
                                                   * runtime
                                                   * compiler
                                                   * validator
                                                   * file infrastructure
                                                   * query engine
                                                   * developer tooling
                                                   * knowledge storage
                                                   * visualization
                                                   * federation
                                                   * governance
                                                   * security
The next logical layer is to define how we prove implementations are actually correct and compatible, including:
                                                   * language conformance suites
                                                   * parser and validator correctness tests
                                                   * compiler round-trip tests
                                                   * runtime behavior tests
                                                   * federation interoperability tests
                                                   * certification levels for AASL implementations
That is the next complete narrative buildout.
AASL Testing, Conformance, and Certification Architecture
The AASL Testing, Conformance, and Certification Architecture defines how implementations of AASL are verified to be correct, interoperable, and trustworthy. By this stage in the system design we have described every functional subsystem of the language ecosystem: the parser, runtime model, compiler (AASC), validator, file infrastructure, query engine, developer tooling, knowledge storage layer, visualization and inspection layer, distributed synchronization and federation model, governance framework, and security architecture. All of those components define what AASL is supposed to do. The testing and certification subsystem defines how we prove that real implementations actually do it correctly.
Without formal testing and conformance procedures, two systems claiming to support AASL might behave differently. One parser might interpret an object incorrectly. Another compiler might generate noncanonical output. A validator might allow invalid ontology terms. A storage engine might corrupt provenance relationships. A distributed node might accept artifacts that another node would reject. The result would be fragmentation of the ecosystem and erosion of trust in the semantic network.
The testing and certification architecture prevents that fragmentation. It establishes a rigorous framework for validating implementations, verifying interoperability, and ensuring that AASL artifacts behave consistently across tools, runtimes, storage systems, and distributed nodes.
________________


1. Purpose of the Testing and Certification Layer
The testing layer ensures that AASL implementations behave according to the official language specification and its associated operational rules.
Its responsibilities include:
                                                   * verifying parser correctness
                                                   * verifying compiler behavior
                                                   * verifying canonicalization stability
                                                   * verifying validator rule enforcement
                                                   * verifying runtime object behavior
                                                   * verifying query engine semantics
                                                   * verifying file infrastructure compatibility
                                                   * verifying distributed synchronization correctness
                                                   * verifying security and integrity enforcement
                                                   * verifying interoperability between independent implementations
                                                   * certifying systems that correctly implement the language
In effect, it ensures that AASL means the same thing everywhere.
________________


2. Position in the AASL Ecosystem
Testing and certification sit above every subsystem and interact with all of them.
Typical relationship:
AASL Implementation
       ↓
Conformance Test Suite
       ↓
Subsystem Validation
       ├ Parser tests
       ├ Compiler tests
       ├ Validator tests
       ├ Runtime tests
       ├ Storage tests
       ├ Query tests
       ├ Federation tests
       └ Security tests
       ↓
Certification Results
The certification layer is not part of runtime execution but acts as an external verification authority for implementations.
________________


3. Categories of Testing
Testing in AASL must occur across several dimensions.
Language Conformance Tests
Verify correct interpretation of the language specification.
Subsystem Tests
Verify the behavior of each component (parser, runtime, compiler, etc.).
Interoperability Tests
Verify that independent implementations interact correctly.
Security Tests
Verify that malicious or malformed artifacts are handled safely.
Performance Tests
Ensure that large-scale semantic workloads remain feasible.
Migration Tests
Verify compatibility between different AASL versions.
Each of these categories addresses a different risk in the ecosystem.
________________


4. Conformance Test Suite
The conformance test suite is the central testing artifact for the language.
It contains a large collection of test cases that exercise every aspect of the specification.
Each test case includes:
                                                   * input artifact
                                                   * expected parsed structure
                                                   * expected canonical output
                                                   * expected validation result
                                                   * expected runtime behavior
These test cases are designed so that any conforming implementation should produce the same results.
The conformance suite acts as the authoritative reference for correct behavior.
________________


5. Parser Testing
Parser tests verify that AASL text is interpreted correctly.
Tests include:
                                                   * valid object parsing
                                                   * multi-line object parsing
                                                   * section boundary detection
                                                   * identifier parsing
                                                   * comment handling
                                                   * whitespace normalization
                                                   * malformed syntax recovery
The expected output is typically an abstract syntax tree representation.
Parser tests ensure that all implementations produce identical semantic structures when given the same source text.
________________


6. Compiler Testing
Compiler tests verify that the AASC compiler correctly transforms external data into canonical AASL.
Test cases may include:
                                                   * English source → canonical AASL
                                                   * Markdown documentation → canonical AASL
                                                   * JSON data → semantic objects
                                                   * dataset ingestion → semantic artifacts
Tests verify that:
                                                   * ontology mappings are correct
                                                   * canonical field ordering is applied
                                                   * identifiers are generated correctly
                                                   * provenance information is preserved
                                                   * canonical hashes remain stable
These tests ensure that knowledge ingestion behaves consistently across environments.
________________


7. Canonicalization Tests
Canonicalization is central to AASL because it ensures that semantically identical knowledge produces identical representations.
Canonicalization tests verify that:
                                                   * synonyms map to canonical ontology terms
                                                   * field ordering follows canonical rules
                                                   * identifier patterns follow required conventions
                                                   * timestamps normalize to UTC
                                                   * numeric values normalize correctly
                                                   * redundant values are removed
The most important test verifies that two semantically identical artifacts with different surface formatting produce the same canonical output.
This property enables deduplication and semantic hashing.
________________


8. Validator Tests
Validator tests ensure that invalid artifacts are correctly rejected or flagged.
Examples include:
                                                   * missing required fields
                                                   * invalid ontology values
                                                   * malformed identifiers
                                                   * invalid timestamps
                                                   * invalid numeric ranges
                                                   * reference integrity failures
                                                   * section placement violations
The validator test suite must confirm that every rule in the rule registry is enforced correctly.
These tests protect the semantic integrity of the language.
________________


9. Runtime Behavior Tests
Runtime tests verify how semantic objects behave once loaded.
Tests include:
                                                   * object loading
                                                   * reference resolution
                                                   * object lifecycle transitions
                                                   * mutation handling
                                                   * validation state transitions
                                                   * canonicalization integration
Runtime tests ensure that the internal semantic graph behaves consistently across implementations.
________________


10. Query Engine Tests
Query tests verify that semantic retrieval works correctly.
Test scenarios include:
                                                   * lookup by object ID
                                                   * retrieval by object type
                                                   * attribute filtering
                                                   * relationship traversal
                                                   * provenance chain retrieval
                                                   * verification state filtering
                                                   * multi-document queries
Each test defines both the query and the expected result set.
This ensures that semantic retrieval behaves consistently across different implementations.
________________


11. File Infrastructure Tests
File tests verify correct reading and writing of AASL artifacts.
These tests confirm that:
                                                   * .aas files load correctly
                                                   * wrapper formats (.aas.md, .aas.txt) are interpreted properly
                                                   * JSON serialization round-trips correctly
                                                   * binary encoding preserves semantic structure
                                                   * canonical output formatting remains stable
These tests protect the durability and portability of AASL artifacts.
________________


12. Distributed Synchronization Tests
Federation tests verify that nodes exchange knowledge safely.
These tests simulate:
                                                   * artifact exchange between nodes
                                                   * canonical identity detection across nodes
                                                   * conflict detection
                                                   * version reconciliation
                                                   * synchronization policies
They ensure that distributed networks behave predictably and safely.
________________


13. Security Tests
Security tests verify that malicious artifacts cannot compromise the system.
Examples include:
                                                   * tampered artifact detection
                                                   * forged signature detection
                                                   * replay artifact detection
                                                   * namespace spoofing detection
                                                   * malformed artifact rejection
                                                   * admission policy enforcement
Security tests confirm that trust boundaries are respected.
________________


14. Performance and Scalability Tests
Large semantic networks may contain millions of objects.
Performance tests measure:
                                                   * parser throughput
                                                   * runtime memory usage
                                                   * query latency
                                                   * synchronization bandwidth
                                                   * storage indexing performance
These tests ensure that AASL can scale to large knowledge corpora.
________________


15. Compatibility Tests
Compatibility tests verify behavior across different versions of the language.
These tests ensure that:
                                                   * older artifacts remain interpretable
                                                   * minor version upgrades remain compatible
                                                   * migration tools function correctly
                                                   * distributed nodes can negotiate compatible versions
Compatibility tests are essential for long-term stability.
________________


16. Certification Levels
Certification programs allow implementations to demonstrate compliance.
Possible certification levels include:
Core Language Certification
Parser, validator, and canonicalization compliance.
Runtime Certification
Runtime and query engine compliance.
Full Ecosystem Certification
Compliance across all subsystems including storage, federation, and security.
Each certification level requires passing a defined subset of tests.
________________


17. Reference Implementation
To support testing, the ecosystem should maintain a reference implementation.
The reference implementation provides:
                                                   * a canonical parser
                                                   * canonical validator
                                                   * canonical compiler
                                                   * canonical query behavior
It acts as a baseline for comparison during certification.
Implementations do not have to use the reference code, but they must behave identically according to the conformance suite.
________________


18. Continuous Testing
Testing should not occur only during certification.
Implementations should run continuous test suites during development.
Continuous testing ensures that:
                                                   * new code changes do not break language compatibility
                                                   * regression bugs are detected early
                                                   * implementations remain aligned with evolving specifications
CI pipelines for AASL tools should integrate the full conformance suite.
________________


19. Test Artifact Repository
All test artifacts should be maintained in a public repository.
This repository should contain:
                                                   * example AASL documents
                                                   * canonical outputs
                                                   * invalid artifact examples
                                                   * federation test cases
                                                   * security test cases
Maintaining this repository ensures that test cases evolve alongside the language.
________________


20. What Testing and Certification Change About AASL
Before the testing architecture exists, AASL is:
                                                   * a language specification
                                                   * a set of reference tools
                                                   * a conceptual ecosystem
After testing and certification exist, AASL becomes:
                                                   * a verifiable standard
                                                   * an interoperable semantic protocol
                                                   * a language that independent systems can implement safely
Testing ensures that implementations remain compatible and trustworthy as the ecosystem grows.
________________


21. Final Summary
The AASL Testing, Conformance, and Certification Architecture establishes the framework for verifying that implementations of the AASL ecosystem behave according to the language specification. It defines test suites for parsers, compilers, validators, runtime systems, query engines, file infrastructure, federation protocols, and security mechanisms. Certification programs ensure interoperability across independent implementations, while continuous testing protects the language from regressions as it evolves.
________________


What comes next
At this point, the complete AASL language ecosystem architecture has been designed.
The next major design effort is integration with the Atrahasis intelligence system, which will define how:
                                                   * AASL semantic artifacts become the knowledge layer of Atrahasis
                                                   * agent systems read and write AASL
                                                   * the verification layer interacts with AASL objects
                                                   * distributed intelligence workflows operate over the AASL knowledge substrate.
This integration will transform AASL from a language ecosystem into the operational semantic backbone of the Atrahasis network.
AASL–Atrahasis Integration Architecture
(How AASL becomes the operational semantic backbone of the Atrahasis intelligence system)
Up to this point we designed the entire AASL ecosystem itself:
                                                   * language specification
                                                   * parser
                                                   * runtime
                                                   * compiler (AASC)
                                                   * validator
                                                   * file infrastructure
                                                   * query engine
                                                   * developer tooling
                                                   * knowledge storage
                                                   * visualization
                                                   * federation
                                                   * governance
                                                   * security
                                                   * testing / certification
Those pieces together define how AASL works as a language.
The next step is how Atrahasis actually uses it.
Atrahasis is the distributed intelligence architecture you uploaded in the project files: an orchestrated network of models and millions of agents cooperating through shared knowledge and verification pipelines. In that system, AASL becomes the universal semantic substrate connecting agents, memory, verification, and orchestration.
This architecture answers the question:
How does Atrahasis actually operate using AASL as its internal language?
________________


1. Core Principle of Integration
The fundamental rule is simple:
All internal knowledge inside Atrahasis is represented in AASL.
Human language and external data are only entry points.
Internally, everything becomes semantic objects.
So the architecture becomes:
Human Input / External Data
        ↓
AASC Compiler
        ↓
AASL Semantic Objects
        ↓
Atrahasis Runtime Systems
        ↓
Agents / Verification / Knowledge Memory
That means AASL acts as the lingua franca of intelligence inside the system.
________________


2. Atrahasis Layer Model
From the documents you uploaded, Atrahasis is structured as a layered architecture:
Users / Applications
        ↓
Interface Layer
        ↓
CIOS (Collective Intelligence OS)
        ↓
Agent Layer
        ↓
Verification Layer (Verichain)
        ↓
Knowledge Layer
        ↓
Infrastructure
AASL integrates across these layers in different ways.
________________


3. Interface Layer Integration
The interface layer is where humans and external systems interact with Atrahasis.
Examples:
                                                   * web interfaces
                                                   * mobile apps
                                                   * CLI interfaces
                                                   * API integrations
These systems still operate in normal formats like:
                                                   * English
                                                   * markdown
                                                   * JSON
                                                   * datasets
The AASC compiler converts those inputs into AASL before they enter the system.
Example workflow:
User query
   ↓
Interface layer
   ↓
AASC compiler
   ↓
AASL semantic representation
   ↓
CIOS orchestration
This ensures the internal system never depends on ambiguous natural language.
________________


4. CIOS (Collective Intelligence Operating System)
CIOS is the orchestration layer coordinating all agents.
It manages:
                                                   * task decomposition
                                                   * agent discovery
                                                   * workload distribution
                                                   * result aggregation
Inside CIOS, tasks should be represented as AASL objects.
Example:
TSK{id:t.climate.analysis op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
Instead of opaque JSON messages, the system routes semantic objects.
Benefits include:
                                                   * deterministic interpretation
                                                   * explicit dependencies
                                                   * easier verification
                                                   * reusable semantic structures
________________


5. Agent Runtime Integration
Agents are the core reasoning units of Atrahasis.
Each agent interacts with the system through AASL objects.
Example agent workflow:
Receive task (AASL)
     ↓
Query semantic memory (AASL query engine)
     ↓
Invoke model reasoning
     ↓
Produce result as AASL objects
     ↓
Submit result to verification layer
Agents never manipulate raw text internally. They operate on semantic structures.
For example:
ACT{id:a.analyze.201 actor:ag.research.01 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44}
CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}
This dramatically simplifies reasoning pipelines.
________________


6. Model Interface
Agents frequently query AI models.
Instead of returning raw text, model outputs should be compiled back into AASL.
Workflow:
Agent prompt → model inference → textual output
                              ↓
                      AASC semantic extraction
                              ↓
                         AASL objects
This keeps the entire reasoning pipeline inside the semantic representation.
________________


7. Verichain Integration
The verification network ensures that knowledge entering shared memory is trustworthy.
AASL makes verification far easier because semantic structure is explicit.
Verification pipeline:
Agent output (AASL)
        ↓
Replication by verification agents
        ↓
Result comparison
        ↓
Consensus score
        ↓
VRF object generated
Example:
VRF{
 target:c.correlation.001
 status:verified
 method:consensus
}
The verification layer attaches verification objects directly to semantic claims.
________________


8. Knowledge Memory Integration
The knowledge layer stores verified AASL artifacts.
Instead of storing text documents, Atrahasis stores semantic knowledge objects.
Examples of stored objects:
                                                   * agents
                                                   * datasets
                                                   * actions
                                                   * claims
                                                   * evidence
                                                   * verification records
Because everything is canonicalized, the system can:
                                                   * detect duplicate knowledge
                                                   * merge identical claims
                                                   * track provenance
                                                   * maintain trust levels
This becomes the shared semantic memory of the intelligence network.
________________


9. Query Integration
Agents, models, and orchestration systems retrieve knowledge through the AASL query engine.
Example queries:
                                                   * retrieve claims about dataset ds.climate.44
                                                   * retrieve verified correlation claims
                                                   * retrieve all actions performed by ag.research.01
Because knowledge is structured, queries are deterministic rather than fuzzy.
________________


10. Semantic Cache Behavior
One major advantage of AASL is semantic caching.
If a task is performed once and its results stored in semantic memory, later agents can reuse that knowledge.
Example:
Agent receives task
        ↓
Check AASL memory
        ↓
If result already exists
        ↓
Return existing claim
This reduces redundant computation across the agent network.
________________


11. Distributed Federation
When Atrahasis expands into multiple clusters, AASL artifacts become the synchronization unit between nodes.
Clusters exchange:
                                                   * semantic documents
                                                   * claim updates
                                                   * verification results
                                                   * provenance chains
Because AASL is canonical, different clusters can detect equivalent knowledge automatically.
This allows the intelligence network to scale globally.
________________


12. Governance Integration
Governance rules operate directly on AASL artifacts.
Policies may control:
                                                   * which nodes may submit artifacts
                                                   * which verification methods are acceptable
                                                   * which ontology modules are permitted
                                                   * how conflicts are resolved
Because governance operates over explicit semantic structures, rule enforcement becomes transparent.
________________


13. Security Integration
The security architecture binds cryptographic trust to AASL artifacts.
For example:
canonical hash
   ↓
digital signature
   ↓
artifact authenticity
When artifacts move between nodes, signatures and provenance chains ensure that knowledge has not been altered.
________________


14. Observability and Monitoring
Because Atrahasis stores knowledge semantically, monitoring tools can inspect system activity.
Examples:
                                                   * agent activity graphs
                                                   * claim creation rates
                                                   * verification success rates
                                                   * knowledge growth over time
Visualization tools use the AASL query engine to build these views.
________________


15. System Lifecycle Example
A complete lifecycle in Atrahasis looks like:
User request
     ↓
AASC compiler converts input to AASL
     ↓
CIOS decomposes task
     ↓
Agents perform reasoning
     ↓
Agents produce AASL claims
     ↓
Verification network validates results
     ↓
Verified artifacts stored in semantic memory
     ↓
Future agents reuse knowledge
This loop gradually builds a collective intelligence corpus.
________________


16. Why AASL Is Critical to Atrahasis
Without AASL, the system would operate on:
                                                   * raw text
                                                   * inconsistent JSON structures
                                                   * ad-hoc message formats
That would make:
                                                   * verification unreliable
                                                   * knowledge reuse difficult
                                                   * distributed synchronization fragile
AASL provides:
                                                   * canonical semantics
                                                   * explicit relationships
                                                   * deterministic hashing
                                                   * structured verification
                                                   * reusable knowledge
It effectively becomes the operating language of Atrahasis.
________________


17. Final Integration Picture
Once integrated, the architecture becomes:
External Inputs
      ↓
AASC Compiler
      ↓
AASL Semantic Language
      ↓
CIOS Orchestration
      ↓
Agent Swarm
      ↓
Verichain Verification
      ↓
Shared Semantic Memory
      ↓
Distributed Federation
Everything inside the system operates on AASL.
________________


18. What This Completes
With this final subsystem, the design now includes:
                                                   * AASL language architecture
                                                   * full implementation ecosystem
                                                   * distributed network protocols
                                                   * governance and security
                                                   * verification infrastructure
                                                   * knowledge memory
                                                   * Atrahasis system integration
Together these form a complete semantic intelligence infrastructure.
________________


If you want, the next extremely important thing we should do is something that will immediately reveal whether the architecture is sound:
define the first minimal working Atrahasis runtime built entirely around AASL, which would include:
                                                   * a small AASL parser
                                                   * a semantic runtime
                                                   * 3–5 basic agent types
                                                   * a simple verification pipeline
                                                   * a shared AASL memory store.
That prototype would validate the entire design end-to-end.
First Minimal Working Atrahasis Runtime Built Entirely Around AASL
This is the first complete operational buildout of Atrahasis in executable form. It is called “minimal” only in the sense that it is the smallest complete end-to-end system that proves the architecture. It is not a toy. It is the first full runtime that demonstrates the actual loop:
                                                   * input enters the system
                                                   * input is converted to AASL
                                                   * tasks are created in AASL
                                                   * agents operate on AASL
                                                   * results are returned in AASL
                                                   * results are verified in AASL
                                                   * verified knowledge is stored in shared semantic memory
                                                   * future tasks reuse that stored semantic knowledge
This runtime is the first point where AASL stops being only a language ecosystem and becomes a living semantic operating substrate for Atrahasis.
________________


1. Purpose of the First Runtime
The first Atrahasis runtime exists to prove five things at once:
                                                   1. AASL can function as the internal language of the system.
                                                   2. Agents can receive, produce, and exchange AASL objects.
                                                   3. A shared semantic memory can store and reuse AASL knowledge.
                                                   4. Verification can operate directly on semantic artifacts.
                                                   5. The orchestration layer can coordinate all of this without falling back to raw text or ad hoc JSON.
So this runtime is the first real test of the entire architecture.
________________


2. Core Principle
The runtime must enforce one absolute rule:
All internal state is represented as AASL semantic objects or AASL-derived runtime objects.
That means:
                                                   * human text is only an ingress format
                                                   * external JSON is only an ingress format
                                                   * model text output is only an intermediate artifact
                                                   * internal truth is AASL
That rule is what makes the runtime truly AASL-native.
________________


3. What the First Runtime Includes
The first runtime should include these major components:
                                                   1. AASL ingress and compilation layer
                                                   2. AASL parser
                                                   3. AASL runtime object store
                                                   4. task orchestration core
                                                   5. agent execution framework
                                                   6. verification loop
                                                   7. shared semantic memory
                                                   8. result reuse and semantic cache lookup
                                                   9. export and observability layer
This is the smallest full loop that proves the system.
________________


4. Runtime Scope
This first runtime should not try to launch the full eventual Atrahasis network with millions of agents. Instead it should be built as a complete but contained semantic operating environment.
It should support:
                                                   * 1 coordinator
                                                   * 3 to 5 core agent types
                                                   * 1 verification path
                                                   * 1 shared semantic memory store
                                                   * 1 AASL runtime
                                                   * 1 query layer
                                                   * 1 storage layer
That gives a real working system without artificial incompleteness.
________________


5. The First Five Agent Types
The first runtime should define five core agent roles.
5.1 Coordinator Agent
Responsible for:
                                                   * receiving normalized task input
                                                   * decomposing tasks
                                                   * assigning work
                                                   * collecting results
                                                   * routing results to verification
                                                   * committing verified knowledge to memory
5.2 Research Agent
Responsible for:
                                                   * inspecting source materials
                                                   * extracting useful semantic content
                                                   * generating draft claims and evidence objects
5.3 Analysis Agent
Responsible for:
                                                   * evaluating relationships
                                                   * producing formal claims
                                                   * attaching confidence estimates
5.4 Verification Agent
Responsible for:
                                                   * independently re-evaluating a task result
                                                   * comparing outputs
                                                   * generating verification state
5.5 Memory Agent
Responsible for:
                                                   * checking whether semantic knowledge already exists
                                                   * retrieving reusable claims/evidence
                                                   * writing verified knowledge into semantic memory
                                                   * managing semantic deduplication and reuse
These five agents are enough to prove the full semantic loop.
________________


6. End-to-End Runtime Flow
The complete first runtime loop should look like this:
User Input / External Input
        ↓
AASC Compiler
        ↓
AASL Task Objects
        ↓
Coordinator Agent
        ↓
Memory Agent checks shared semantic memory
        ↓
If no sufficient result exists:
    Research Agent / Analysis Agent execute
        ↓
    AASL Result Objects
        ↓
    Verification Agent evaluates
        ↓
    VRF object produced
        ↓
    Memory Agent stores verified semantic bundle
Else:
    Existing semantic result is returned
This is the first true Atrahasis semantic reasoning cycle.
________________


7. Ingress Layer
The runtime begins at the ingress layer.
Incoming user or system input may be:
                                                   * English
                                                   * markdown
                                                   * JSON
                                                   * uploaded documents
                                                   * structured datasets
The ingress layer passes that input into AASC, which produces:
                                                   * TSK
                                                   * CST
                                                   * optional DS
                                                   * optional PRV
                                                   * document-level metadata
So the first thing the runtime receives is never raw text. It receives semantic objects.
Example:
TSK{id:t.climate.001 op:analyze target:ds.climate.44 priority:high}
CST{target:t.climate.001 max_time_ms:10000}
That becomes the unit of orchestration.
________________


8. AASL Runtime Core
The runtime core loads all compiled and retrieved AASL into live runtime state.
It manages:
                                                   * object registry
                                                   * reference graph
                                                   * document membership
                                                   * validation state
                                                   * canonical state
                                                   * verification state
                                                   * memory hydration and de-hydration
This runtime is the semantic operating space in which all agents work.
It must support:
                                                   * loading new task bundles
                                                   * loading previously stored semantic knowledge
                                                   * attaching new result objects
                                                   * exposing query interfaces to agents
________________


9. Coordinator Agent Behavior
The Coordinator Agent is the conductor of the runtime.
Its lifecycle is:
                                                   1. receive new task object bundle
                                                   2. inspect target and task type
                                                   3. ask Memory Agent whether a reusable answer already exists
                                                   4. if not, decompose work into agent assignments
                                                   5. send tasks to Research/Analysis Agents
                                                   6. collect returned semantic outputs
                                                   7. send outputs to Verification Agent
                                                   8. if verification succeeds, commit to semantic memory
                                                   9. return final semantic bundle as the result
The Coordinator does not “understand” raw prose as its working substrate. It understands AASL objects.
________________


10. Memory-First Execution
A defining trait of Atrahasis should be memory-first execution.
Before any agent computes anything expensive, the system should ask:
                                                   * do we already have a verified claim for this target
                                                   * do we already have related evidence
                                                   * do we already have a reusable semantic bundle
                                                   * is there a prior equivalent canonical hash
This is the first place where AASL’s value becomes obvious.
Because knowledge is canonicalized, the Memory Agent can determine reuse much more reliably than with raw text.
This turns the system into a true semantic cache.
________________


11. Research Agent Behavior
The Research Agent’s job is to transform source material into structured semantic evidence candidates.
Its runtime flow is:
                                                   1. receive an AASL task bundle
                                                   2. load referenced documents or datasets
                                                   3. extract relevant semantic units
                                                   4. return AASL draft objects such as:
                                                   * ACT
                                                   * EVD
                                                   * preliminary CLM
                                                   * PRV
Example output:
ACT{id:a.research.001 actor:ag.research.01 op:extract target:ds.climate.44}
EVD{id:e.dataset.001 type:dataset_reference source:ds.climate.44}
PRV{target:e.dataset.001 source:dataset}
The Research Agent is evidence-oriented, not final-truth oriented.
________________


12. Analysis Agent Behavior
The Analysis Agent receives task context and evidence objects and turns them into more formal semantic conclusions.
Its flow is:
                                                   1. receive the task plus evidence context
                                                   2. analyze semantic relationships
                                                   3. produce claims
                                                   4. attach confidence
                                                   5. preserve provenance links
Example output:
CLM{id:c.correlation.001 type:correlation subject:var.temp object:var.co2}
CNF{target:c.correlation.001 val:0.92}
PRV{target:c.correlation.001 source:dataset}
The Analysis Agent should always return semantic claims, not narrative text, as the primary result.
________________


13. Verification Agent Behavior
